# Certified Information Privacy Professional/Canada Study Guide

Exam code: CIPP/C

This study guide is organized by exam domain and objective. Content is
extracted from 2 source books.

## Domain 1: Introduction to Privacy in Canada

**Weight: 28%**

### 1.1: Understand the Canadian governmental structure and its relationship to privacy

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 1.1: Understand the Canadian governmental structure and its relationship to privacy

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Key concepts

- **Federal system of government**: Canada divides governmental powers between
  federal and provincial/territorial levels, creating a multi-layered privacy
  regulatory environment.
  - Federal government has jurisdiction over federally regulated industries
    (banking, telecommunications, transportation)
  - Provinces and territories have jurisdiction over property, civil rights,
    and matters of a local nature
  - This division directly affects which privacy laws apply to organizations

- **Privacy commissioners**: Independent officers responsible for privacy
  oversight at both federal and provincial levels.
  - Federal: Office of the Privacy Commissioner of Canada (OPC)
  - Provincial commissioners exist in provinces with their own privacy
    legislation
  - Powers vary by jurisdiction and legislation

- **Provincial privacy oversight alternatives**: In provinces without a
  dedicated privacy commissioner, the Ombudsman office handles privacy
  oversight responsibilities.

- **Substantially similar legislation**: Mechanism where provincial
  private-sector privacy laws can be deemed equivalent to federal law (PIPEDA).
  - Alberta's PIPA, British Columbia's PIPA, and Quebec's Act Respecting the
    Protection of Personal Information in the Private Sector are substantially
    similar
  - When provincial law applies, PIPEDA generally does not apply to
    intra-provincial commercial activities

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Organization operates only within BC | Apply BC PIPA | Provincial substantially similar law applies to intra-provincial commercial activity |
| Federally regulated bank in Alberta | Apply PIPEDA | Federal legislation governs federally regulated industries regardless of province |
| Cross-border data transfer from Quebec | Consider both Quebec law and PIPEDA | PIPEDA applies to interprovincial and international commercial activity |
| Individual seeks recourse in province without privacy commissioner | Contact Ombudsman | Ombudsman handles privacy oversight where no commissioner exists |

## Gotchas

- **Provincial vs. federal jurisdiction**: Organizations cannot simply choose
  which law to follow; jurisdiction is determined by the nature of the
  organization's activities and industry sector.

- **Substantially similar does not mean identical**: Provincial laws deemed
  substantially similar to PIPEDA may have different specific requirements,
  timelines, or enforcement mechanisms.

- **Federal reach**: Even in provinces with substantially similar legislation,
  PIPEDA still applies to federally regulated industries and interprovincial or
  international commercial activities.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Privacy Act response time | 30 days | Government institution response to access requests |
| Continuing education requirement | 20 credits/year | Required to maintain CIPP/C certification |

## Related topics

- **PIPEDA scope**: Understanding when federal private-sector law applies vs.
  provincial legislation (Objective 2.B)
- **Provincial private-sector laws**: Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, Quebec's Act
  (Objective 2.A)
- **Public-sector privacy**: Privacy Act and provincial FOI/POP Acts
  (Domain III)
- **Privacy commissioners' powers**: Oversight, investigation, and enforcement
  mechanisms (Domain I)

---

*Note: This extract is based on the IAPP Free Study Guide, which provides an
overview of exam topics. For comprehensive coverage of Canadian governmental
structure and privacy, consult the authoritative textbook "Canadian Privacy:
Data Protection Law and Policy for the Practitioner."*

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 1.1: Understand the Canadian governmental structure and its relationship to privacy

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Constitutional division of powers**: Canada's Constitution Act, 1867 divides
  legislative authority between federal and provincial governments. This division
  directly determines which level of government can enact privacy legislation for
  different sectors and activities.

- **Federal jurisdiction**: The federal government has authority over matters
  including banking, telecommunications, interprovincial transportation, and
  federal government institutions. PIPEDA applies to federally regulated private
  sector organizations and cross-border or interprovincial commercial activities.

- **Provincial jurisdiction**: Provinces have authority over property and civil
  rights, health care, education, and provincially incorporated businesses. This
  enables provinces to enact their own private sector, public sector, and health
  privacy legislation.

- **Civil law vs. common law**: Quebec operates under a civil law system derived
  from French law, while the rest of Canada follows the common law tradition from
  English law. Privacy legislation must be interpreted within the applicable
  legal tradition.
  - Civil law: Codified statutes are the primary source; judicial decisions
    interpret the code
  - Common law: Court decisions (precedent) and statutes both serve as sources
    of law

- **Sources of law in Canada**:
  - Constitutional law (Constitution Act, Charter of Rights and Freedoms)
  - Federal statutes (e.g., PIPEDA, Privacy Act, CASL)
  - Provincial statutes (e.g., Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, Quebec Law 25)
  - Regulations made under statutes
  - Common law and civil law principles
  - Case law and tribunal decisions

- **Courts**: The Canadian court system includes:
  - Supreme Court of Canada: Final court of appeal for all matters
  - Federal Court and Federal Court of Appeal: Jurisdiction over federal
    government matters, including Privacy Act complaints
  - Provincial superior courts: General jurisdiction over civil and criminal
    matters
  - Provincial courts: Handle most day-to-day matters

- **Administrative tribunals**: Specialized bodies that adjudicate disputes
  within their statutory mandate. In the privacy context, Privacy Commissioners
  function as administrative bodies with investigation and oversight powers,
  though their specific remedial authorities vary by jurisdiction.

- **Privacy Commissioners**: Independent officers responsible for overseeing
  privacy law compliance:
  - Office of the Privacy Commissioner of Canada (OPC): Oversees PIPEDA and the
    Privacy Act at the federal level
  - Provincial and territorial commissioners: Each province and territory has an
    information and privacy commissioner (or ombudsman) with jurisdiction over
    provincial/territorial public bodies and, where applicable, private sector
    and health sector organizations

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Organization operates only within one province | Determine if provincial "substantially similar" law applies | AB, BC, and QC have private sector laws that apply to intra-provincial commercial activities |
| Federal government department collects personal information | Apply the Privacy Act | Federal public sector is governed by the Privacy Act, not PIPEDA |
| Bank collects customer information | Apply PIPEDA | Banks are federally regulated regardless of where they operate |
| Hospital in Ontario collects patient information | Apply PHIPA (provincial health act) | Health sector has separate provincial legislation |
| Complaint about federal government's handling of personal information | File with OPC under Privacy Act; Federal Court has jurisdiction for appeals | Privacy Act complaints go through federal channels |
| Complaint about provincially regulated organization | File with provincial commissioner | Provincial commissioners oversee organizations under their jurisdiction |

## Gotchas

- **The Charter applies to government action, not private actors.** The Canadian
  Charter of Rights and Freedoms constrains government conduct but does not
  directly apply to private sector organizations. Privacy rights against private
  entities come from statutes like PIPEDA, not the Charter.

- **"Federally regulated" refers to the industry, not the location.** A
  telecommunications company operating only in Alberta is still federally
  regulated because telecommunications is under federal jurisdiction.

- **Provincial commissioners' powers vary significantly.** Some have
  order-making authority (e.g., Alberta, BC, Quebec private sector), while
  others can only make recommendations (e.g., OPC under PIPEDA). Know the
  enforcement model for each jurisdiction.

- **Courts and commissioners have different roles.** Privacy Commissioners
  investigate complaints and make findings or recommendations. Courts provide
  judicial review of commissioner decisions and can award damages in some
  circumstances.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Federal private sector law | PIPEDA | Applies unless provincial "substantially similar" law governs |
| Federal public sector law | Privacy Act | Covers federal government institutions |
| Provinces with substantially similar private sector laws | AB, BC, QC | Their laws apply to intra-provincial commercial activities |
| Federal court system for Privacy Act | Federal Court | Has jurisdiction over federal government privacy matters |
| Supreme Court of Canada | Final appellate authority | Can hear privacy cases on constitutional or significant legal questions |

## Related topics

- **PIPEDA and provincial private sector laws**: The division of powers
  determines when federal vs. provincial private sector law applies (Domain II)
- **Privacy Act and provincial FOIPPAs**: Federal and provincial public sector
  laws reflect the constitutional division of powers (Domain III)
- **Provincial health privacy acts**: Health care falls under provincial
  jurisdiction, enabling province-specific health privacy legislation (Domain IV)
- **Privacy Commissioners' enforcement powers**: Understanding the governmental
  structure helps contextualize why different commissioners have different
  authorities

### 1.2: Apply concepts and development of fair information practices (FIPPs)

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 1.2: Apply concepts and development of fair information practices (FIPPs)

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Key concepts

- **CSA Model Code for the Protection of Personal Information**: Referenced as one of
  two Canadian model codes for privacy compliance; forms part of the CIPP/C key subject
  matter areas.
- **Generally Accepted Privacy Principles (GAPP)**: Referenced alongside the CSA Model
  Code as a framework for privacy compliance in Canada.

## Source limitations

The free study guide provides only a brief reference to FIPPs-related frameworks without
substantive content. It lists the CSA and GAPP frameworks as key subject matter areas
but does not explain:

- The history or development of fair information practices
- The OECD Guidelines that influenced Canadian privacy law
- The specific principles within each framework
- How FIPPs map to Canadian legislation

## Related topics

- **PIPEDA Schedule 1**: The free study guide notes that PIPEDA is a key subject
  matter area but does not detail how the CSA Model Code was incorporated as Schedule 1.
- **Provincial private-sector laws**: Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, and Quebec's private
  sector legislation are mentioned as key subject areas; these laws also implement
  FIPPs-based principles.

## Exam preparation note

The IAPP recommends studying the authoritative textbook "Canadian Privacy: Data
Protection Law and Policy for the Practitioner" for substantive content on this
objective. The free study guide serves as an exam preparation overview rather than a
source of detailed legal content.

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 1.2: Apply concepts and development of fair information practices (FIPPs)

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Fair Information Practices (FIPPs)**: Foundational privacy principles that govern
  the collection, use, and disclosure of personal information; originated in the 1970s
  and form the basis for modern privacy legislation worldwide, including Canadian laws.
- **1973 HEW Report**: U.S. Department of Health, Education, and Welfare report that
  first articulated fair information practices as a response to computerized record-
  keeping; established the conceptual framework later adopted internationally.
- **OECD Guidelines on the Protection of Privacy and Transborder Flows of Personal
  Data (1980)**: Eight principles adopted by the Organisation for Economic Co-operation
  and Development; became the international standard and directly influenced Canadian
  privacy law development.
  - Collection Limitation Principle
  - Data Quality Principle
  - Purpose Specification Principle
  - Use Limitation Principle
  - Security Safeguards Principle
  - Openness Principle
  - Individual Participation Principle
  - Accountability Principle
- **CSA Model Code for the Protection of Personal Information (1996)**: Canadian
  Standards Association voluntary code that adapted OECD principles for Canadian
  context; adopted as Schedule 1 to PIPEDA, making it legally binding for private
  sector organizations.
  - Ten principles: Accountability, Identifying Purposes, Consent, Limiting Collection,
    Limiting Use/Disclosure/Retention, Accuracy, Safeguards, Openness, Individual
    Access, Challenging Compliance.
- **Generally Accepted Privacy Principles (GAPP)**: Framework developed by AICPA and
  CICA (now CPA Canada); provides ten principles with measurable criteria for privacy
  program implementation and auditing.

## OECD principles mapped to Canadian law

| OECD Principle | CSA/PIPEDA Equivalent | Core requirement |
| -------------- | --------------------- | ---------------- |
| Collection Limitation | Limiting Collection | Collect only what is necessary; use fair and lawful means; obtain consent where appropriate |
| Data Quality | Accuracy | Personal information must be accurate, complete, and current for its intended purpose |
| Purpose Specification | Identifying Purposes | State purposes at or before collection; document purposes |
| Use Limitation | Limiting Use, Disclosure, Retention | Use/disclose only for stated purposes or with consent; retain only as long as necessary |
| Security Safeguards | Safeguards | Protect against loss, unauthorized access, destruction, use, modification, or disclosure |
| Openness | Openness | Make policies and practices readily available; be transparent about information handling |
| Individual Participation | Individual Access | Individuals can access their data, challenge accuracy, and have corrections made |
| Accountability | Accountability | Designate individual(s) responsible; ensure compliance throughout organization |

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Organization collects more data than needed "just in case" | Violates Collection Limitation; collect only what is necessary for identified purposes | FIPPs require minimization; excess collection increases privacy risk |
| Customer asks what personal information the company holds | Provide access within 30 days under PIPEDA; apply Individual Participation principle | Access is a fundamental right under both OECD and CSA frameworks |
| Collected data is used for a new marketing campaign | Obtain fresh consent or ensure new use falls within original stated purposes | Use Limitation restricts secondary uses without consent |
| Third-party processor will handle customer data | Ensure contractual safeguards; organization remains accountable | Accountability principle extends to third parties |
| Privacy policy is buried in legal jargon on website | Revise to be readily understandable and accessible | Openness requires genuine transparency, not technical compliance |

## Gotchas

- **OECD Guidelines are not legally binding**: They are recommendations that influenced
  legislation; the CSA Model Code in PIPEDA Schedule 1 is enforceable law in Canada.
- **CSA Model Code has 10 principles, not 8**: The OECD's eight principles were
  expanded to ten in the Canadian context - notably splitting some principles and
  adding "Challenging Compliance."
- **Accountability goes beyond designation**: Having a privacy officer is not
  sufficient; the organization must implement policies, procedures, training, and
  demonstrate compliance throughout all operations.
- **Consent is not always required for collection**: FIPPs allow collection without
  consent in specific circumstances (employment necessity, legal requirements, publicly
  available information); the principle is "consent where appropriate."
- **GAPP vs. CSA Model Code**: GAPP is an auditing/assurance framework with measurable
  criteria; CSA Model Code is the legal standard in PIPEDA. Both derive from FIPPs but
  serve different purposes.

## Historical development

| Year | Development | Significance |
| ---- | ----------- | ------------ |
| 1973 | HEW Report (U.S.) | First articulation of FIPPs |
| 1980 | OECD Guidelines adopted | International consensus on privacy principles |
| 1996 | CSA Model Code published | Canadian voluntary standard adapting OECD principles |
| 2001 | PIPEDA comes into force (Phase 1) | CSA Model Code becomes Schedule 1; federal law applies to federally regulated industries |
| 2004 | PIPEDA full application | Extends to all commercial activities in provinces without substantially similar legislation |

## Related topics

- **Schedule 1 to PIPEDA**: Contains the CSA Model Code; understanding the ten
  principles is essential for applying PIPEDA's requirements.
- **Substantially similar provincial legislation**: Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, and Quebec's
  private sector law implement FIPPs principles with provincial variations.
- **Privacy by Design**: Modern evolution of FIPPs; embeds privacy principles into
  system design rather than retrofitting compliance.
- **Accountability frameworks**: OPC guidance on demonstrating accountability builds
  directly on the first CSA/FIPPs principle.

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 1.3: Know the OECD Guidelines on the Protection of Privacy and their eight principles

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **OECD Guidelines on the Protection of Privacy and Transborder Flows of Personal
  Data**: Adopted in 1980 by the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and
  Development, these guidelines represent the first internationally agreed-upon
  framework for privacy protection. Updated in 2013 to address modern data
  processing practices.
  - Established the foundation for fair information practices worldwide
  - Non-binding but highly influential in shaping national privacy laws
  - Canada was a founding participant and signatory
  - Directly influenced the development of PIPEDA and the CSA Model Code

- **Purpose of the OECD Guidelines**: Harmonize national privacy legislation while
  preventing unjustified barriers to transborder data flows. Balance individual
  privacy rights with the free flow of information necessary for international
  commerce.

## The eight OECD privacy principles

1. **Collection Limitation Principle**: Personal data should be obtained by lawful
   and fair means and, where appropriate, with the knowledge or consent of the data
   subject.
   - Limits on what can be collected, not just how it is used
   - Consent should be meaningful and informed
   - Lawfulness requirement addresses both legal authority and ethical collection

2. **Data Quality Principle**: Personal data should be relevant to the purposes for
   which it is used and, to the extent necessary, should be accurate, complete and
   kept up-to-date.
   - Organizations must ensure fitness for purpose
   - Inaccurate data can cause harm to individuals
   - Obligation to maintain and correct data as needed

3. **Purpose Specification Principle**: The purposes for collection should be
   specified no later than the time of collection. Subsequent use should be limited
   to fulfilling those purposes or compatible purposes.
   - Requires clear articulation of why data is needed
   - Prevents function creep and unauthorized repurposing
   - Purposes must be specified before or at collection, not after

4. **Use Limitation Principle**: Personal data should not be disclosed, made
   available or used for purposes other than specified except with data subject
   consent or by authority of law.
   - Complements the Purpose Specification Principle
   - Two exceptions: consent and legal authority
   - Applies to both internal use and external disclosure

5. **Security Safeguards Principle**: Personal data should be protected by
   reasonable security safeguards against risks such as loss, unauthorized access,
   destruction, use, modification or disclosure.
   - Risk-based approach to security
   - Must address multiple threat types
   - "Reasonable" implies proportionality to sensitivity and context

6. **Openness Principle**: There should be a general policy of openness about
   developments, practices and policies regarding personal data. Means should be
   readily available to establish the existence and nature of personal data, the
   main purposes of use, and the identity and location of the data controller.
   - Transparency requirement for organizations
   - Individuals should be able to discover who holds their data
   - Supports informed consent and access rights

7. **Individual Participation Principle**: An individual should have the right to:
   - Obtain confirmation of whether a data controller holds their data
   - Have data communicated within a reasonable time, at reasonable cost, in
     intelligible form
   - Be given reasons if a request is denied, with ability to challenge denial
   - Challenge data and have it erased, rectified, completed or amended

8. **Accountability Principle**: A data controller should be accountable for
   complying with measures that give effect to the other principles.
   - Organizations must take responsibility for compliance
   - Cannot delegate accountability even when outsourcing processing
   - Foundation for demonstrable compliance and governance programs

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Marketing wants to use HR data for campaigns | Deny unless new consent obtained | Use Limitation: original purpose was employment, not marketing |
| Collecting "nice to have" fields on forms | Remove non-essential fields | Collection Limitation: only collect what is necessary |
| Third-party vendor receives customer data | Ensure vendor accountability contractually | Accountability: controller remains responsible |
| Data breach occurs | Report as required; review security measures | Security Safeguards: must protect against unauthorized access |
| Customer cannot understand privacy policy | Rewrite in plain language | Openness: means must be "readily available" and intelligible |

## Gotchas

- **Principles are interconnected**: Purpose Specification and Use Limitation work
  together - you cannot have one without the other. Exam questions may test whether
  a scenario violates one or both.

- **Collection Limitation vs. Data Minimization**: The OECD principle focuses on
  lawfulness and consent at collection; data minimization (a related but distinct
  concept) focuses on limiting collection to what is necessary. The OECD principle
  is broader.

- **Accountability is about the controller**: Even when processing is outsourced,
  the original data controller remains accountable. This is distinct from having a
  contract in place.

- **2013 Updates added new dimensions**: The 2013 revision added guidance on
  privacy management programs, data security breach notification, and national
  privacy strategies - expect questions on the evolution of the Guidelines.

- **Non-binding but foundational**: The OECD Guidelines are recommendations, not
  law. However, they directly influenced binding legislation like PIPEDA, which
  incorporated the principles through the CSA Model Code.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Original adoption year | 1980 | First international privacy framework |
| Major revision year | 2013 | Added accountability provisions and breach notification |
| Number of core principles | 8 | Often tested as a specific count |
| Binding status | Non-binding | Recommendations, not treaty obligations |

## Related topics

- **CSA Model Code**: Translates OECD principles into Canadian context; formed
  Schedule 1 of PIPEDA
- **PIPEDA principles**: The 10 PIPEDA principles derive from and expand upon the
  OECD Guidelines
- **Fair Information Practices (FIPs)**: Broader family of principles that includes
  OECD Guidelines, US FTC principles, and APEC framework
- **GAPP (Generally Accepted Privacy Principles)**: AICPA/CICA framework that
  builds on OECD Guidelines for privacy governance
- **Transborder data flows**: OECD Guidelines specifically address cross-border
  transfers while maintaining privacy protection

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 1.4: Understand the CSA Model Code for the Protection of Personal Information

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **CSA Model Code for the Protection of Personal Information (CAN/CSA-Q830-96)**:
  Published in 1996 by the Canadian Standards Association, this voluntary standard
  established privacy principles for the private sector. It became the foundation
  for PIPEDA when incorporated as Schedule 1 to the Act in 2000.
  - First comprehensive Canadian privacy code for the private sector
  - Developed through multi-stakeholder consensus (industry, government, consumer
    groups)
  - Directly based on the 1980 OECD Guidelines, adapted for Canadian context
  - Became legally binding when adopted into PIPEDA

- **Relationship to PIPEDA**: The CSA Model Code forms Schedule 1 of PIPEDA. The
  10 principles in the Model Code are the 10 PIPEDA principles. Understanding the
  CSA Code means understanding the core obligations under PIPEDA.
  - Schedule 1 is incorporated by reference into PIPEDA
  - Courts and commissioners interpret PIPEDA principles through the CSA Code text
  - The Model Code provides implementation guidance beyond the principle statements

- **Development context**: Created in response to growing concern about private
  sector data practices in the 1990s, particularly as electronic commerce expanded.
  Canada sought a made-in-Canada approach that aligned with international standards.

## The 10 CSA Model Code principles

1. **Accountability**: An organization is responsible for personal information under
   its control. A designated individual (privacy officer) must be accountable for
   compliance.
   - Accountability continues during transfers to third parties for processing
   - Must implement policies and practices to give effect to principles
   - Must establish complaint handling procedures

2. **Identifying Purposes**: The purposes for collecting personal information must
   be identified at or before the time of collection.
   - Purposes must be documented
   - When a new purpose is identified, consent must be obtained before use
   - Purposes must be specific enough to be meaningful

3. **Consent**: The knowledge and consent of the individual are required for
   collection, use or disclosure (with limited exceptions).
   - Form of consent (express or implied) varies with sensitivity
   - Individuals can withdraw consent subject to legal or contractual restrictions
   - Consent must not be obtained through deception

4. **Limiting Collection**: Collection must be limited to what is necessary for
   identified purposes. Information must be collected by fair and lawful means.
   - No collection "just in case" it might be useful
   - Collection methods must be transparent and appropriate
   - Aligns with OECD Collection Limitation Principle

5. **Limiting Use, Disclosure and Retention**: Personal information shall not be
   used or disclosed for purposes other than those for which it was collected,
   except with consent or as required by law. Information shall be retained only
   as long as necessary.
   - Establishes the linkage between purpose and use
   - Requires retention schedules and destruction procedures
   - Exceptions for legal requirements or consent

6. **Accuracy**: Personal information shall be as accurate, complete and up-to-date
   as necessary for the purposes for which it is to be used.
   - Standard is fitness for purpose, not absolute accuracy
   - Organizations should not routinely update all information
   - Information used for decision-making about individuals requires higher accuracy

7. **Safeguards**: Personal information shall be protected by security safeguards
   appropriate to the sensitivity of the information.
   - Administrative, technical and physical measures required
   - Employees must understand the importance of confidentiality
   - Security must be proportionate to sensitivity and risk

8. **Openness**: An organization shall make readily available specific information
   about its policies and practices relating to the management of personal
   information.
   - Must be understandable and accessible to individuals
   - Includes contact information for the privacy officer
   - Privacy policies should not be hidden or overly legalistic

9. **Individual Access**: Upon request, an individual shall be informed of the
   existence, use and disclosure of their personal information and given access.
   Individuals can challenge accuracy and completeness.
   - Response must be timely and at minimal or no cost
   - Information must be in understandable form
   - Limited exceptions exist (legal privilege, third-party information)

10. **Challenging Compliance**: An individual shall be able to address a challenge
    concerning compliance to the designated accountable individual.
    - Organizations must have complaint procedures
    - All complaints must be investigated
    - Must inform individuals of avenues for recourse (e.g., Privacy Commissioner)

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| No privacy officer designated | Appoint one immediately | Accountability: must have designated individual |
| Collecting SIN for loyalty program | Do not collect; SIN not necessary | Limiting Collection: must be necessary for purpose |
| Using customer data for new product development | Obtain fresh consent first | Identifying Purposes / Consent: new purpose requires new consent |
| Third-party processor receives data | Ensure contractual protections | Accountability: organization remains responsible |
| Customer requests access to their file | Respond within 30 days with the information | Individual Access: timely response required |
| Sensitive health data stored on laptop | Encrypt and apply enhanced security | Safeguards: proportionate to sensitivity |

## Gotchas

- **CSA Code vs. PIPEDA**: The CSA Model Code IS the PIPEDA principles (Schedule 1).
  Some exam questions may phrase things in terms of "CSA principles" while others
  use "PIPEDA principles" - they are the same 10 principles.

- **Voluntary origin, mandatory application**: The CSA Code was originally
  voluntary. When incorporated into PIPEDA, it became legally enforceable. Don't
  confuse the voluntary origins with the current mandatory status.

- **Ten principles vs. eight OECD principles**: The CSA/PIPEDA framework has 10
  principles; the OECD Guidelines have 8. The CSA Code expanded and adapted the
  OECD framework. Know the count difference.

- **Accountability as first principle**: Unlike the OECD Guidelines (where
  accountability is the eighth principle), the CSA Code places Accountability
  first, emphasizing organizational responsibility as the foundation for all other
  principles.

- **Consent is not absolute**: The CSA Code allows exceptions to consent in limited
  circumstances (legal requirements, emergencies, publicly available information).
  Questions may test whether a scenario qualifies for an exception.

- **Purpose limitation is ongoing**: Identifying purposes is not a one-time
  activity. When purposes change, the organization must return to the consent
  principle.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Publication year | 1996 | CAN/CSA-Q830-96 |
| Number of principles | 10 | Two more than OECD's 8 |
| Incorporated into PIPEDA | 2000 | As Schedule 1 |
| Access request response time | 30 days | Under PIPEDA implementation |
| Status before PIPEDA | Voluntary | Became mandatory via legislation |
| Governing standards body | CSA Group | Formerly Canadian Standards Association |

## Related topics

- **OECD Guidelines**: The CSA Model Code directly derives from and adapts the 1980
  OECD privacy principles for Canada
- **PIPEDA Schedule 1**: The legal mechanism by which the CSA Code became binding
  law in Canada
- **Fair Information Practices (FIPs)**: The CSA Code is Canada's expression of
  the global FIPs framework
- **Provincial private sector laws**: Alberta, BC and Quebec laws use similar
  principles, though not always identical language
- **GAPP (Generally Accepted Privacy Principles)**: A related framework that also
  builds on OECD and CSA foundations
- **Privacy Commissioner of Canada**: Interprets and enforces the CSA/PIPEDA
  principles through complaints and investigations

### 1.5: Identify differences between privacy and data protection frameworks

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 1.5: Identify differences between privacy and data protection frameworks

Source: CIPP/C Free Study Guide (IAPP, 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Canadian privacy model codes**: The CSA Model Code for the Protection of
  Personal Information and Generally Accepted Privacy Principles (GAPP)
  provide standards for privacy compliance in the private sector.
  - CSA Model Code: Canadian standards framework; foundational to PIPEDA
  - GAPP: Professional framework with measurable criteria

- **Framework purpose**: Privacy model codes inform Canadian legislation and
  provide reference standards for understanding foundational privacy principles.

## Related topics

- **CSA Model Code**: Canadian adaptation of OECD principles; became Schedule 1
  of PIPEDA
- **GAPP**: Professional governance framework for privacy compliance
- **PIPEDA**: Federal private sector law incorporating CSA principles
- **Provincial private sector laws**: Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, Quebec's Act
  Respecting the Protection of Personal Information in the Private Sector

## Scope limitations

orientation only. The guide explicitly states it does not cover:

- Specific provisions of each statute
- Case law or enforcement decisions
- Detailed compliance procedures
- Cross-border transfer requirements

# 1.5: Identify differences between privacy and data protection frameworks

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Privacy vs. data protection**: Privacy is a broad concept encompassing an
  individual's right to control information about themselves and be free from
  intrusion. Data protection is narrower, focusing on the processing of personal
  data and the rules governing its collection, use, storage and disclosure.
  - Privacy predates data protection as a legal concept
  - Data protection emerged as a response to computerized record-keeping
  - Canadian law uses both concepts but emphasizes fair information practices

- **Framework origins and evolution**:
  - **Fair Information Practices (FIPs)**: Originated in the 1973 US HEW Report;
    established core principles for handling personal information in automated
    systems
  - **OECD Guidelines (1980)**: First international framework; codified eight
    principles for privacy protection and transborder data flows
  - **CSA Model Code (1996)**: Canadian adaptation of OECD principles; 10
    principles for private sector; became Schedule 1 of PIPEDA
  - **EU Data Protection Directive (1995) / GDPR (2016/2018)**: Rights-based
    data protection framework; influenced global standards
  - **GAPP (2009)**: AICPA/CICA framework for privacy governance; 10 principles
    with measurable criteria

- **Privacy framework approach**: Emphasizes individual rights and autonomy.
  Focuses on limiting intrusions into private life and protecting against
  surveillance. Rooted in human rights and constitutional protections.
  - Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms, s. 8 (search and seizure)
  - Civil Code of Quebec, arts. 35-41 (right to privacy)
  - Common law tort of intrusion upon seclusion

- **Data protection framework approach**: Emphasizes organizational obligations
  and procedural requirements. Focuses on lawful processing, purpose limitation
  and accountability. Rooted in administrative law and regulatory compliance.
  - PIPEDA and provincial equivalents
  - Privacy Act (public sector)
  - Provincial health information statutes

## Comparison of major frameworks

| Framework | Scope | Principles | Binding | Canadian relevance |
| --------- | ----- | ---------- | ------- | ------------------ |
| OECD Guidelines | International | 8 | Non-binding | Foundation for PIPEDA |
| CSA Model Code | Canadian private sector | 10 | Via PIPEDA | Schedule 1 of PIPEDA |
| PIPEDA | Federal private sector | 10 | Yes | Primary commercial law |
| GAPP | Professional governance | 10 | Voluntary | CPA Canada guidance |
| EU GDPR | European Economic Area | 7 | Yes | Adequacy considerations |

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Organization needs EU adequacy | Apply GDPR-equivalent protections | GDPR requires adequate protection for transfers |
| Privacy audit for investor due diligence | Use GAPP criteria for measurable assessment | GAPP provides auditable, objective standards |
| New data collection system design | Apply CSA/PIPEDA principles | Binding legal requirements for Canadian organizations |
| Cross-border data transfer to US | Follow PIPEDA accountability provisions | Organization remains responsible regardless of location |
| Comparing frameworks for policy development | Map principles across frameworks | Identify common requirements and gaps |

## Gotchas

- **Number of principles differs by framework**: OECD has 8; CSA/PIPEDA has 10;
  GAPP has 10 (with 77 criteria). Exam questions may test whether you know which
  count applies to which framework.

- **Same principle, different emphasis**: "Accountability" appears in multiple
  frameworks but with different positioning. OECD places it eighth; CSA/PIPEDA
  places it first. The Canadian approach emphasizes accountability as
  foundational rather than supplementary.

- **Binding vs. non-binding confusion**: OECD Guidelines are recommendations.
  CSA Model Code was voluntary until incorporated into PIPEDA. GAPP is voluntary.
  PIPEDA is binding law. Know which framework has legal force in Canada.

- **Privacy rights vs. data protection obligations**: Privacy-focused frameworks
  (Charter, Quebec Civil Code) emphasize what individuals can claim. Data
  protection frameworks (PIPEDA) emphasize what organizations must do. An exam
  scenario might ask which framework applies to a given situation.

- **EU GDPR and Canadian law**: GDPR does not apply directly in Canada but
  affects organizations that handle EU residents' data. Canada has an adequacy
  finding under GDPR for PIPEDA-covered commercial activities, but not for
  public sector or health sector transfers.

- **GAPP is not the same as CSA/PIPEDA**: GAPP was developed by
  AICPA/CICA for privacy governance and attestation. It has 10 principles with
  77 measurable criteria. Do not confuse it with the 10 PIPEDA principles even
  though both frameworks have the same count.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| OECD Guidelines principles | 8 | Foundational international framework |
| CSA Model Code principles | 10 | Canadian adaptation |
| PIPEDA principles | 10 | Same as CSA via Schedule 1 |
| GAPP principles | 10 | With 77 measurable criteria |
| GDPR articles | 99 | Most comprehensive data protection law |
| Canadian Charter privacy section | Section 8 | Search and seizure |
| Canada GDPR adequacy status | Partial | Commercial activities only |

## Related topics

- **OECD Guidelines**: The foundational international privacy framework; eight
  principles for privacy and transborder flows
- **CSA Model Code**: Canadian standards-based adaptation of OECD; became
  Schedule 1 of PIPEDA
- **PIPEDA**: Federal private sector law; incorporates CSA principles as binding
  legal obligations
- **GAPP**: Professional governance framework with measurable criteria; used for
  privacy audits and attestation
- **EU GDPR**: Most comprehensive data protection law; drives global privacy
  standards; Canada has partial adequacy finding
- **Fair Information Practices (FIPs)**: Original US framework; ancestor of all
  modern privacy and data protection frameworks
- **Quebec Law 25**: Provincial law with GDPR-like provisions; demonstrates
  convergence of privacy and data protection approaches

  - Listed as key subject matter area for the CIPP/C exam

  frequently compared with GAPP on exam
- **PIPEDA**: Federal private sector law; GAPP can complement PIPEDA compliance
  but is not a legal substitute
- **Privacy principles development**: GAPP is one framework in the lineage from
  OECD Guidelines to modern Canadian privacy law

# 1.6: Understand Generally Accepted Privacy Principles (GAPP)

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **GAPP origin and purpose**: Developed jointly by the American Institute of
  Certified Public Accountants (AICPA) and the Chartered Professional
  Accountants of Canada (CPA Canada, formerly CICA) in 2009. Designed as a
  comprehensive privacy framework for organizations to build, assess and certify
  their privacy programs.
  - Created for privacy governance and attestation engagements
  - Enables auditors to assess and report on privacy controls
  - Provides measurable, objective criteria for compliance evaluation

- **GAPP structure**: 10 principles with 77 measurable criteria. Each principle
  contains specific criteria that organizations can implement and auditors can
  verify.
  - Principles provide the conceptual framework
  - Criteria provide implementation specifics and audit checkpoints
  - More detailed than PIPEDA's 10 principles or OECD's 8 guidelines

- **The 10 GAPP principles**:
  - **Management**: Organization defines, documents, communicates and assigns
    accountability for its privacy policies and procedures
  - **Notice**: Organization provides notice about its privacy policies and
    identifies the purposes for which personal information is collected, used,
    retained and disclosed
  - **Choice and consent**: Organization describes choices available to
    individuals and obtains implicit or explicit consent for collection, use and
    disclosure of personal information
  - **Collection**: Organization collects personal information only for the
    purposes identified in the notice
  - **Use, retention and disposal**: Organization limits use of personal
    information to purposes identified in notice and for which consent was
    obtained; retains information only as long as necessary and disposes of it
    in a secure manner
  - **Access**: Organization provides individuals with access to their personal
    information for review and update
  - **Disclosure to third parties**: Organization discloses personal information
    to third parties only for identified purposes and with consent
  - **Security for privacy**: Organization protects personal information against
    unauthorized access
  - **Quality**: Organization maintains accurate, complete and relevant personal
    information for identified purposes
  - **Monitoring and enforcement**: Organization monitors compliance with its
    privacy policies and has procedures to address privacy-related complaints
    and disputes

- **GAPP vs. CSA Model Code/PIPEDA**: Both have 10 principles but serve
  different purposes.
  - GAPP: Governance and attestation framework; 77 criteria; voluntary
  - CSA/PIPEDA: Legal compliance framework; 10 principles; mandatory for
    private sector commercial activities
  - GAPP is more granular with auditable criteria
  - PIPEDA principles are incorporated into binding law

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| External privacy audit for SOC 2 engagement | Apply GAPP framework | GAPP provides measurable criteria suitable for attestation |
| Investor due diligence on privacy practices | Use GAPP assessment | Produces objective, verifiable findings |
| Benchmarking privacy program maturity | GAPP criteria mapping | 77 criteria provide granular measurement |
| Regulatory compliance demonstration | Apply PIPEDA principles | PIPEDA is the binding legal requirement |
| Building internal privacy controls | Use both frameworks | GAPP criteria inform how to implement PIPEDA principles |
| Privacy program gap analysis | GAPP criteria checklist | Identifies specific areas needing improvement |

## Gotchas

- **GAPP is not a legal requirement**: Unlike PIPEDA, GAPP is a voluntary
  framework. Organizations may choose to align with GAPP but cannot be
  penalized by regulators for non-compliance with GAPP specifically.

- **77 criteria, not 77 principles**: GAPP has 10 principles and 77 criteria.
  Exam questions may try to confuse the count. Remember: 10 principles with 77
  supporting criteria.

- **Management is first in GAPP; Accountability is first in CSA/PIPEDA**: The
  first principle differs by name and emphasis. GAPP starts with "Management"
  (organizational governance); CSA/PIPEDA starts with "Accountability"
  (designated individual). Both address organizational responsibility but from
  different angles.

- **GAPP is a joint US-Canadian framework**: Developed by AICPA and CPA Canada
  together. It reflects both countries' privacy traditions and is used in both
  jurisdictions for privacy attestation.

- **GAPP vs. AICPA Trust Services Criteria**: GAPP focuses specifically on
  privacy. AICPA's Trust Services Criteria (used in SOC 2 reports) covers five
  categories: Security, Availability, Processing Integrity, Confidentiality,
  and Privacy. The Privacy category aligns with GAPP.

- **Notice vs. notification**: GAPP's "Notice" principle concerns informing
  individuals about privacy practices before collection. This differs from
  breach "notification" which is about informing individuals after a privacy
  incident.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| GAPP principles | 10 | Same count as PIPEDA but different framework |
| GAPP criteria | 77 | Measurable implementation checkpoints |
| GAPP effective date | 2009 | Joint AICPA/CPA Canada publication |
| CSA Model Code principles | 10 | Became Schedule 1 of PIPEDA |
| OECD Guidelines principles | 8 | Foundational international framework |
| SOC 2 Trust Services categories | 5 | Privacy is one of the five |

## Related topics

- **CSA Model Code**: Canadian privacy standard with 10 principles; became
  Schedule 1 of PIPEDA; provides the legal framework GAPP complements
- **OECD Guidelines**: Eight foundational privacy principles from 1980;
  influenced both CSA Model Code and GAPP
- **PIPEDA**: Federal private sector law; incorporates CSA principles; GAPP can
  help demonstrate PIPEDA compliance but is not a substitute
- **SOC 2 Privacy**: AICPA attestation engagement that uses GAPP-aligned
  criteria for the privacy trust services category
- **Fair Information Practices (FIPs)**: Original US framework from 1973; the
  conceptual ancestor of OECD Guidelines, CSA Model Code, and GAPP
- **Privacy Impact Assessments (PIAs)**: GAPP criteria can inform what to assess
  in a PIA; both are governance tools but serve different purposes

### 1.7: Know the role and powers of the Office of the Privacy Commissioner of Canada (OPC)

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 1.7: Know the role and powers of the Office of the Privacy Commissioner of Canada (OPC)

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Office of the Privacy Commissioner of Canada (OPC)**: An independent Officer
  of Parliament responsible for overseeing compliance with both PIPEDA (private
  sector) and the Privacy Act (federal public sector). The Commissioner is
  appointed by the Governor in Council after approval by both houses of
  Parliament.
  - Reports directly to Parliament, not to the government
  - Seven-year term, renewable once
  - Current structure includes Deputy Commissioners and specialized branches

- **Ombudsman model under PIPEDA**: The OPC operates as an ombudsman rather
  than a regulator with direct enforcement power. This means:
  - Cannot issue binding orders against organizations
  - Makes findings and recommendations that organizations may choose to follow
  - Relies on persuasion, public reporting, and court applications for
    enforcement
  - Can refer matters to Federal Court if organization does not comply

- **Investigation powers**: The Commissioner has broad investigative authority:
  - Receive and investigate complaints from individuals
  - Initiate Commissioner-initiated complaints (own-motion investigations)
  - Summon witnesses and compel testimony under oath
  - Enter premises and examine records (with warrant or consent)
  - Audit organization practices proactively

- **Finding categories**: After investigation, the OPC issues findings:
  - Well-founded: Complaint is substantiated
  - Well-founded and resolved: Issue confirmed and corrected during investigation
  - Not well-founded: Complaint lacks merit
  - Settled: Parties reached agreement during investigation
  - Discontinued: Investigation ended without findings (e.g., withdrawn)

- **Federal Court application**: If an organization does not comply with OPC
  recommendations, the Commissioner or complainant can apply to Federal Court
  for:
  - Order requiring organization to correct practices
  - Award of damages to the complainant (including damages for humiliation)
  - Any other order the court considers appropriate

- **Public education and guidance**: The OPC fulfills a significant education
  mandate:
  - Publishes guidance documents on privacy topics
  - Issues findings and reports that establish interpretive precedent
  - Provides information on privacy rights to the public
  - Conducts research on emerging privacy issues

- **Annual reporting**: The Commissioner tables annual reports to Parliament
  covering:
  - Complaint statistics and trends
  - Significant findings from investigations
  - Recommendations for legislative reform
  - Assessment of privacy threats and emerging issues

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Individual believes organization mishandled personal information | File complaint with OPC under PIPEDA | OPC investigates private sector complaints; 1-year limitation from when complainant knew of grounds |
| Organization disagrees with OPC finding | Organization may decline to follow recommendation | OPC cannot issue binding orders; matter may proceed to Federal Court |
| OPC recommends organizational changes | Organization should implement or explain refusal | Non-compliance may result in Federal Court application and public reporting |
| Commissioner identifies systemic privacy issue | OPC may launch Commissioner-initiated complaint | Own-motion investigations address sector-wide concerns without individual complainant |
| Complainant wants damages for privacy violation | File Federal Court application after OPC finding | OPC cannot award damages; only Federal Court has that authority |
| Organization wants to demonstrate compliance proactively | Request OPC advisory opinion or compliance review | OPC offers consultative services separate from enforcement |

## Gotchas

- **The OPC cannot issue binding orders under PIPEDA.** Unlike provincial
  commissioners in Alberta, BC, and Quebec, the federal Privacy Commissioner
  makes recommendations only. Organizations can refuse to comply, though this
  may result in Federal Court proceedings and reputational consequences.

- **Findings are not legally binding but carry significant weight.** While OPC
  findings are not court orders, Federal Courts give substantial deference to
  the Commissioner's expertise. Published findings establish practical
  expectations for compliance.

- **The limitation period is one year from knowledge, not from the incident.**
  A complainant has one year from when they became aware (or reasonably should
  have become aware) of the grounds for complaint, not from when the privacy
  violation occurred.

- **Commissioner-initiated complaints do not require an individual complainant.**
  The OPC can investigate organizations on its own motion when it has reason
  to believe a violation occurred. This enables investigation of systemic
  issues.

- **The Privacy Act and PIPEDA have different enforcement mechanisms.** Under
  the Privacy Act (public sector), the Commissioner also lacks order-making
  power, but complaints proceed to Federal Court rather than following the
  PIPEDA application process.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Commissioner term length | 7 years | Renewable once |
| Complaint limitation period | 1 year | From knowledge of grounds for complaint |
| Who can file Federal Court application | Commissioner or complainant | After OPC investigation complete |
| Federal Court limitation | 1 year | From date of Commissioner's final report |
| Annual report deadline | Tabled in Parliament annually | Covers previous fiscal year activities |
| Order-making authority under PIPEDA | None | OPC makes recommendations only |

## Related topics

- **PIPEDA complaint process**: OPC investigation procedures and timelines
  (Domain II)
- **Privacy Act oversight**: OPC role in federal public sector privacy
  (Domain III)
- **Provincial commissioners**: Comparing OPC ombudsman model to provincial
  order-making models (Domain I.A)
- **Federal Court jurisdiction**: Court's role in privacy enforcement and
  damages awards (Domain I.A)
- **Breach notification**: OPC's role in receiving and monitoring breach
  reports under PIPEDA (Domain II)

### 1.8: Understand the complaint and investigation process for privacy breaches

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 1.8: Understand the complaint and investigation process for privacy breaches

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Key concepts

- **Privacy commissioner oversight**: Canadian privacy commissioners at federal
  and provincial levels are responsible for investigating privacy complaints and
  breaches.
  - Office of the Privacy Commissioner of Canada (OPC) handles federal matters
  - Provincial commissioners handle matters under provincial legislation
  - Powers vary: federal commissioner makes recommendations; some provincial
    commissioners have order-making authority

- **Ombudsman as alternative oversight**: In provinces without a dedicated
  privacy commissioner, the Ombudsman office handles privacy oversight,
  including complaint intake and investigation.

- **Complaint-driven enforcement model**: The Canadian privacy framework relies
  heavily on individual complaints to trigger investigations.
  - Individuals can file complaints alleging violations of their privacy rights
  - Commissioners may also initiate investigations without a complaint
  - Investigation findings inform organizational compliance

- **Multi-jurisdictional considerations**: Which commissioner handles a
  complaint depends on which law applies to the organization.
  - PIPEDA matters go to the OPC
  - Provincial private-sector matters (Alberta, BC, Quebec) go to provincial
    commissioners
  - Health sector matters go to the relevant provincial health information
    commissioner or authority

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Individual believes a federal organization violated PIPEDA | File complaint with OPC | OPC has jurisdiction over PIPEDA matters |
| Complaint against provincially regulated business in BC | File with BC Information and Privacy Commissioner | BC PIPA applies; provincial commissioner has jurisdiction |
| Privacy violation in province without privacy commissioner | Contact provincial Ombudsman | Ombudsman handles privacy oversight in these jurisdictions |
| Breach at federally regulated bank | Report to OPC regardless of province | Federal legislation governs federally regulated industries |

## Gotchas

- **Recommendation vs. order power**: The federal Privacy Commissioner can only
  make recommendations, not binding orders. Alberta, BC, and Quebec
  commissioners have order-making authority. This fundamental difference affects
  enforcement outcomes.

- **Provincial overlap**: Organizations may need to understand multiple
  complaint processes depending on their operations across provinces with
  different privacy regimes.

- **Not all oversight offices have equal powers**: The Ombudsman model in some
  provinces may have different investigative tools and remedial powers compared
  to dedicated privacy commissioners.

## Related topics

- **Role of the OPC**: Commissioner's investigative powers and oversight
  functions (Objective 1.7)
- **Canadian governmental structure**: Federal vs. provincial jurisdiction
  affects which complaint process applies (Objective 1.1)
- **Provincial private-sector laws**: Different complaint and investigation
  processes in Alberta, BC, and Quebec (Domain II)
- **Breach notification requirements**: Triggering events that may lead to
  complaints (Domain II)

---

*Note: This extract is based on the IAPP Free Study Guide, which provides a
high-level overview of exam topics. For comprehensive coverage of complaint
procedures, breach notification requirements, and investigation processes,
consult the authoritative textbook "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and
Policy for the Practitioner."*

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 1.8: Understand the complaint and investigation process for privacy breaches

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Privacy breach vs. privacy complaint**: A privacy breach is an unauthorized
  access to, disclosure of, or loss of personal information. A complaint is a
  formal allegation by an individual that an organization has violated their
  privacy rights. Breaches may trigger complaints, but they are distinct
  processes with different reporting and investigation requirements.

- **Breach notification requirements under PIPEDA**: Organizations must report
  breaches that pose a "real risk of significant harm" (RROSH) to the Office of
  the Privacy Commissioner of Canada (OPC) and affected individuals.
  - Report to OPC "as soon as feasible" after determining RROSH exists
  - Notify affected individuals "as soon as feasible"
  - Maintain records of all breaches for 24 months (regardless of RROSH)
  - Notification to third parties may be required to mitigate harm

- **Real risk of significant harm (RROSH) assessment**: Organizations must
  evaluate whether a breach meets the RROSH threshold by considering:
  - Sensitivity of the personal information involved
  - Probability that the information will be misused
  - Types of harm that could result (bodily harm, humiliation, damage to
    reputation, financial loss, identity theft, negative effects on employment,
    business or professional opportunities)

- **OPC complaint investigation process**: The standard process for PIPEDA
  complaints follows these stages:
  - Intake: OPC receives complaint and confirms jurisdiction
  - Early resolution: Attempt to resolve without full investigation
  - Investigation: Formal examination of facts and practices
  - Finding: Commissioner issues determination (well-founded, not well-founded,
    settled, discontinued, well-founded and resolved)
  - Recommendation: If well-founded, OPC recommends corrective action

- **Filing a complaint with the OPC**: Individuals can file written complaints
  alleging PIPEDA violations.
  - Must be filed within one year of when the complainant became aware (or
    reasonably should have become aware) of the grounds for complaint
  - OPC screens complaints for jurisdiction and admissibility
  - No fee to file a complaint
  - Complaints can also be filed with provincial commissioners where applicable

- **Commissioner-initiated investigations**: The Privacy Commissioner can launch
  investigations without receiving a complaint (own-motion investigations) when:
  - There is reason to believe a violation has occurred
  - Systemic issues affect multiple individuals or organizations
  - A matter is of significant public interest
  - Audit or assessment reveals potential non-compliance

- **Provincial complaint processes**: Provincial commissioners (Alberta, BC,
  Quebec for private sector; all provinces for public sector and health) have
  their own complaint and investigation processes.
  - Alberta, BC, and Quebec commissioners have order-making power
  - Provincial breach notification requirements may differ from PIPEDA
  - Health sector breaches follow provincial health privacy act requirements

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Data breach affects 500 customers with financial information | Assess RROSH; if positive, notify OPC and affected individuals as soon as feasible | Financial data is sensitive; high probability of misuse triggers notification |
| Employee loses unencrypted laptop with HR records | Record breach; assess RROSH; notify if significant harm likely | All breaches must be recorded; RROSH determines notification requirement |
| Individual believes organization disclosed information without consent | File complaint with OPC or provincial commissioner within 1 year | Complaint triggers investigation; limitation period runs from knowledge |
| Organization receives complaint about access request denial | Respond to OPC investigation; provide requested documentation | Cooperation with investigation is expected; obstruction has consequences |
| Breach involves low-sensitivity data with minimal harm risk | Record breach but no notification required | RROSH threshold not met; still must maintain 24-month breach record |
| Data breach in Quebec involving Quebec residents | Report to Commission d'accs  l'information (CAI) | Quebec has its own breach notification regime under Law 25 |

## Gotchas

- **24-month breach record requirement applies to all breaches.** Organizations
  must maintain records of every breach involving personal information, not just
  those requiring notification. The OPC can request these records for review.

- **"As soon as feasible" does not mean "immediately."** Organizations have
  reasonable time to investigate and assess RROSH before notifying. However,
  unreasonable delays can result in findings of non-compliance.

- **The 1-year limitation period runs from knowledge, not from the incident.**
  If an individual discovers a privacy violation years after it occurred, they
  still have one year from discovery to file a complaint.

- **OPC findings are not legally binding orders.** Unlike Alberta, BC, and
  Quebec commissioners, the federal Privacy Commissioner can only make
  recommendations. Non-compliance may lead to Federal Court application but not
  direct enforcement.

- **Provincial breach notification may differ from PIPEDA.** Quebec's Law 25
  (effective 2022-2024) has specific breach notification requirements.
  Organizations must understand which regime applies based on jurisdiction.

- **Early resolution is not a finding.** If a matter is resolved during early
  resolution, the OPC does not issue a formal finding. This differs from
  "well-founded and resolved" which is a formal finding category.

- **Organizations cannot refuse to cooperate with OPC investigations.** The
  Commissioner has statutory powers to compel production of documents and
  testimony. Obstruction can result in additional complaints and reputational
  damage.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Breach record retention | 24 months | From date organization determines breach occurred |
| Complaint limitation period | 1 year | From when complainant knew or should have known |
| OPC notification timing | As soon as feasible | After determining RROSH exists |
| Individual notification timing | As soon as feasible | After determining RROSH exists |
| OPC order-making power | None | Recommendations only; court application required |
| Alberta/BC/Quebec commissioner power | Order-making | Can issue binding compliance orders |
| Federal Court application deadline | 1 year | From date of Commissioner's final report |

## Related topics

- **Role of the OPC**: Commissioner's investigative powers and finding categories
  (Objective 1.7)
- **PIPEDA's 10 principles**: Accountability and safeguards principles relevant
  to breach prevention and response (Domain II)
- **Provincial private sector laws**: Different breach notification and
  complaint processes in AB, BC, QC (Domain II.B)
- **Health sector breaches**: Provincial health privacy acts have sector-specific
  breach protocols (Domain IV)
- **Privacy Impact Assessments**: Proactive identification of breach risks
  (Domain III.B)
- **Safeguarding personal information**: Controls to prevent breaches
  (Domain I.B)

### 1.9: Know recent legal developments and court decisions affecting privacy law

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 1.9: Know recent legal developments and court decisions affecting privacy law

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Key concepts

- **Evolving Canadian privacy legislation**: Canadian privacy law continues to
  develop through new legislation and amendments. The study guide references
  private-sector laws (PIPEDA, Alberta's PIPA, BC's PIPA, Quebec's Act
  Respecting the Protection of Personal Information) as key exam areas,
  indicating the importance of understanding how these laws have evolved.

- **Quebec's Act Respecting the Protection of Personal Information in the
  Private Sector**: Quebec's law underwent significant modernization through
  Law 25 (Bill 64), introducing enhanced requirements.
  - Mandatory Privacy Impact Assessments for high-risk processing
  - Stricter consent and transparency requirements
  - Administrative monetary penalties up to CAD 25 million or 4% global revenue
  - Extraterritorial application to organizations processing Quebec residents'
    data

- **Role of commissioners in shaping privacy law**: Privacy commissioners
  contribute to legal development through investigations, findings, and
  guidance. The study guide highlights "roles and oversight powers of Canadian
  information and privacy commissioners" as key subject matter.
  - OPC findings interpret PIPEDA provisions
  - Provincial commissioners (Alberta, BC, Quebec) can issue binding orders
  - Published decisions establish compliance expectations

- **Court decisions on privacy matters**: Courts interpret privacy statutes
  and resolve disputes. The Federal Court has jurisdiction over PIPEDA appeals;
  provincial courts address provincial privacy legislation.
  - Judicial decisions establish binding precedent
  - Courts clarify statutory ambiguities
  - Decisions shape what constitutes valid consent and reasonable expectations

- **Federal privacy law reform**: Ongoing efforts to modernize PIPEDA reflect
  legal development in Canada. Bill C-27 proposed the Consumer Privacy
  Protection Act (CPPA) and the Artificial Intelligence and Data Act (AIDA) as
  part of the Digital Charter Implementation Act.
  - Proposed administrative monetary penalties
  - Enhanced protections for minors
  - New AI governance requirements

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Quebec enacts new privacy requirements | Review compliance for Quebec residents regardless of org location | Quebec law has extraterritorial reach |
| Privacy Commissioner publishes new findings | Update practices to align with interpretation | Commissioner findings guide compliance expectations |
| Court decision interprets PIPEDA provision | Revise policies to reflect judicial interpretation | Court decisions establish binding precedent |
| Proposed legislation reaches final reading | Prepare implementation plans | Organizations need lead time to adapt programs |
| Provincial law introduces order-making authority | Recognize increased enforcement risk | Binding orders carry greater compliance obligations |

## Gotchas

- **Provincial developments have national impact.** Quebec's Law 25 affects any
  organization with Quebec customers, regardless of where the organization is
  located. Provincial legal changes can force national compliance updates.

- **Federal commissioner recommendations vs. provincial commissioner orders.**
  The OPC can only recommend under PIPEDA, but Alberta, BC, and Quebec
  commissioners can issue binding compliance orders. Enforcement outcomes
  differ significantly between jurisdictions.

- **Legislative reform timelines are uncertain.** Proposed federal reforms
  (CPPA/AIDA) have not yet passed. Organizations should monitor developments
  but cannot assume specific implementation dates.

- **"Recent" is relative to exam date.** The CIPP/C exam is updated
  periodically. Legal developments considered "recent" depend on when the body
  of knowledge was last revised. Check the effective date of exam materials.

- **Court decisions are context-specific.** Similar facts may yield different
  outcomes based on circumstances. Courts apply contextual analysis to privacy
  expectations, so no universal rule applies across all situations.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Quebec Law 25 AMPs | Up to CAD 25 million or 4% global revenue | Whichever is greater |
| PIPEDA Federal Court appeal deadline | 1 year from Commissioner's final report | For judicial review |
| Alberta/BC commissioner authority | Binding compliance orders | Can require specific remedial actions |
| OPC authority under PIPEDA | Recommendations only | No order-making power |
| Proposed CPPA AMPs | Up to CAD 25 million or 5% global revenue | If Bill C-27 passes as proposed |

## Related topics

- **Canadian governmental structure**: Federal vs. provincial jurisdiction
  determines which laws and courts apply (Objective 1.1)
- **Privacy Commissioner roles and powers**: How commissioners contribute to
  legal development through enforcement (Objective 1.7)
- **Complaint and investigation process**: How commissioner findings arise
  (Objective 1.8)
- **Provincial private-sector laws**: Quebec, Alberta, BC laws that continue
  to evolve (Domain II.B)
- **PIPEDA principles**: Foundation for understanding how amendments build on
  existing framework (Domain II.A)

---

*Note: This extract is based on the IAPP Free Study Guide, which provides a
high-level overview of exam topics. The study guide does not detail specific
court decisions or legislative timelines. For comprehensive coverage of recent
legal developments and landmark cases, consult the authoritative textbook
"Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the Practitioner."*

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 1.9: Know recent legal developments and court decisions affecting privacy law

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Role of courts and tribunals in privacy law interpretation**: Canadian courts
  and administrative tribunals shape privacy law through judicial decisions.
  Courts interpret statutory language, resolve ambiguities, and establish
  precedents that guide future compliance. The Federal Court has jurisdiction
  over PIPEDA appeals, while provincial courts address provincial privacy
  statutes.

- **Commissioner rulings and their effect**: Privacy Commissioner findings and
  guidance, while not always legally binding, establish interpretive standards
  that organizations should follow.
  - OPC findings interpret PIPEDA provisions and set compliance expectations
  - Provincial commissioners (AB, BC, QC) issue binding orders
  - Published decisions create de facto standards for similar situations
  - Guidance documents and position statements influence organizational practices

- **Quebec Law 25 (Bill 64)**: Major modernization of Quebec's private sector
  privacy law, phased in from 2022-2024.
  - Mandatory Privacy Impact Assessments for certain processing
  - Mandatory breach notification to Commission d'accs  l'information (CAI)
  - Right to data portability
  - Enhanced consent requirements and transparency obligations
  - Expanded enforcement powers and significant administrative monetary penalties
  - Extraterritorial application to organizations processing Quebec residents' data

- **PIPEDA amendments and proposed reforms**: Ongoing legislative efforts to
  modernize federal privacy law.
  - Bill C-27 (Digital Charter Implementation Act) proposed significant changes
  - Consumer Privacy Protection Act (CPPA) as proposed PIPEDA replacement
  - Artificial Intelligence and Data Act (AIDA) as companion legislation
  - Proposed administrative monetary penalties and private right of action
  - Enhanced protections for minors and sensitive data

- **Consent and reasonable expectations doctrine**: Courts have refined what
  constitutes valid consent and reasonable individual expectations.
  - Consent must be meaningful, informed, and specific to purposes
  - "Bundled consent" and take-it-or-leave-it approaches scrutinized
  - Reasonable expectations of privacy vary by context
  - Express consent required for sensitive personal information

- **Definition of personal information**: Court and commissioner decisions
  continue to clarify the scope of "personal information."
  - Information "about an identifiable individual" broadly interpreted
  - IP addresses, device identifiers, and online activity can be personal info
  - Business contact information exceptions narrowly construed
  - Work product may or may not constitute personal information

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Organization operates in Quebec and other provinces | Comply with Law 25 for Quebec residents plus PIPEDA/other provincial laws | Law 25 applies to Quebec residents regardless of organization location |
| Commissioner issues finding on similar fact pattern | Review and align practices with Commissioner interpretation | Commissioner findings guide compliance even if not binding orders |
| New court decision interprets consent requirement | Update consent processes to reflect judicial interpretation | Courts establish binding precedent on statutory interpretation |
| Privacy Commissioner publishes new guidance | Review organizational practices against guidance | Guidance reflects current enforcement priorities and expectations |
| Proposed legislation would change compliance requirements | Monitor developments; prepare for implementation | Legislative changes require adaptation of privacy programs |
| Court finds business practice violates reasonable expectations | Discontinue practice and remediate harm | Court decisions establish what is legally permissible |

## Gotchas

- **Quebec Law 25 has extraterritorial reach.** Organizations outside Quebec that
  process Quebec residents' personal information must comply. This parallels GDPR
  extraterritoriality and can catch unprepared organizations.

- **OPC findings are persuasive but not binding.** Unlike Alberta, BC, and Quebec
  commissioners who can issue orders, the federal Privacy Commissioner makes
  recommendations. However, Federal Court can issue binding orders on PIPEDA
  matters.

- **Commissioner guidance can change interpretation.** The OPC and provincial
  commissioners regularly update guidance documents. What was acceptable
  practice may become non-compliant when guidance changes.

- **Legislative reform may significantly alter requirements.** Proposed federal
  reforms (CPPA/AIDA) would introduce administrative monetary penalties and
  other major changes. Organizations should monitor and prepare for potential
  new obligations.

- **Provincial developments affect national organizations.** Quebec's Law 25
  created new requirements that affect any organization with Quebec customers.
  Provincial legal developments can have national compliance implications.

- **Court decisions on reasonable expectations are context-dependent.** There is
  no universal standard for reasonable expectations of privacy. Courts apply
  contextual analysis, so similar facts may yield different outcomes based on
  circumstances.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Law 25 PIA requirement | Certain high-risk processing | Biometrics, profiling, large-scale surveillance |
| Law 25 AMPs (organizations) | Up to CAD 25 million or 4% global revenue | Whichever is greater |
| PIPEDA Federal Court appeal deadline | 1 year | From Commissioner's final report |
| Quebec breach notification | CAI and affected individuals | When serious harm risk exists |
| Proposed CPPA AMPs | Up to CAD 25 million or 5% global revenue | Under Bill C-27 as proposed |
| Alberta/BC order-making authority | Binding compliance orders | Can require specific actions |
| OPC order-making authority | None | Recommendations only under current PIPEDA |

## Related topics

- **Privacy Commissioner roles and powers**: Enforcement authorities and
  investigation processes (Objective 1.7)
- **Complaint and investigation process**: How Commissioner findings arise from
  complaints (Objective 1.8)
- **Substantially similar provincial laws**: Quebec, Alberta, BC private sector
  laws and their differences (Domain II.B)
- **Consent requirements**: How judicial decisions shape valid consent standards
  (Domain II.A)
- **International data transfers**: How court decisions affect cross-border data
  flows (Objective 1.10)
- **AI governance**: How emerging legislation addresses AI and automated
  decision-making (Domain I.B)

### 1.10: Understand significance of court and commissioner rulings on privacy law interpretation

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 1.10: Understand significance of court and commissioner rulings on privacy law interpretation
