## Coverage note

The IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide is an introductory document that provides exam
preparation guidance and body of knowledge overview. It does not contain
substantive content on court decisions, commissioner rulings, or case law
interpretation.

The study guide explicitly notes these scope limitations:

> This study guide is introductory. Substantive legal content requires the
> official textbook. The guide does not cover:
> - Case law or enforcement decisions
> - Detailed compliance procedures

## Key subject areas referenced

The free study guide identifies the following as key subject matter for the
CIPP/C certification:

- **Roles and oversight powers of Canadian information and privacy
  commissioners**: The guide notes this as a key area without elaborating on
  specific rulings or their interpretive significance.

- **Private-sector laws**: PIPEDA, Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, and Quebec's Act
  Respecting the Protection of Personal Information in the Private Sector are
  listed, but court interpretation of these statutes is not addressed.

- **Public-sector laws**: The Federal Privacy Act and provincial access laws are
  referenced without discussion of judicial or commissioner interpretation.

## Related exam content

The body of knowledge outline in the free study guide places this objective
within Domain I: Introduction to Privacy in Canada (28% weight), which covers:

- Canadian governmental structure
- Privacy basics and principles development
- International privacy implementation

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 1.10: Understand significance of court and commissioner rulings on privacy law interpretation

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Precedential weight of court decisions**: Canadian courts establish binding
  and persuasive precedent that shapes how privacy statutes are interpreted.
  - Supreme Court of Canada decisions bind all lower courts
  - Federal Court and Federal Court of Appeal decisions guide PIPEDA
    interpretation
  - Provincial superior court decisions affect provincial privacy laws
  - Judicial interpretation fills gaps where statutory language is ambiguous

- **Commissioner findings as interpretive guidance**: Privacy commissioner
  decisions, while varying in binding authority, establish practical standards
  for compliance.
  - Federal OPC findings are recommendations, not binding orders
  - Alberta, BC, and Quebec commissioners issue binding orders
  - Published decisions create de facto compliance benchmarks
  - Commissioner interpretations influence how courts view organizational conduct

- **Relationship between commissioners and courts**: The enforcement system
  creates a two-tier interpretive structure.
  - Commissioner handles initial investigation and interpretation
  - Courts provide final adjudication when matters are appealed or referred
  - Federal Court may adopt, modify, or reject OPC interpretations
  - Courts give deference to commissioner expertise on technical privacy matters

- **Evolution of privacy principles through case law**: Key concepts develop
  through successive decisions.
  - "Personal information" scope expands through interpretation
  - "Reasonable expectations" doctrine refined by courts
  - Consent requirements clarified by commissioner findings and court rulings
  - Sensitivity determinations established through case-by-case analysis

- **Impact of rulings on organizational compliance**: Organizations must adapt
  practices based on interpretive developments.
  - New rulings may invalidate previously acceptable practices
  - Commissioner guidance signals enforcement priorities
  - Court decisions establish legal boundaries organizations cannot cross
  - Industry-specific rulings create sector expectations

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| OPC issues finding on data practice similar to yours | Review and align practices with Commissioner interpretation | Published findings establish compliance expectations even without binding authority |
| Federal Court overturns OPC recommendation | Follow court interpretation over Commissioner position | Court decisions are binding; Commissioner findings are advisory |
| Provincial commissioner issues order in another province | Consider whether reasoning applies to your jurisdiction | Cross-provincial decisions may signal emerging interpretive trends |
| Court narrows definition of consent exception | Update consent practices to reflect stricter interpretation | Judicial narrowing creates new compliance requirements |
| Commissioner publishes guidance document | Treat as current enforcement expectations | Guidance reflects how Commissioner will interpret future complaints |
| Court decision contradicts prior Commissioner position | Follow court decision; monitor for Commissioner response | Courts have final interpretive authority on statutory meaning |

## Gotchas

- **OPC findings are persuasive but not binding.** Organizations sometimes
  mistakenly treat OPC findings as legally binding. While courts give deference
  to Commissioner expertise, only court orders and provincial commissioner
  orders (AB, BC, QC) have direct legal force.

- **Court deference to commissioners is not absolute.** Courts will reject
  commissioner interpretations that conflict with statutory language or
  established legal principles. Organizations should not assume Commissioner
  positions will always be upheld.

- **Provincial decisions may influence federal interpretation.** Although
  provincial commissioner orders apply only within their jurisdiction, their
  reasoning may influence how the OPC and Federal Court interpret similar
  PIPEDA provisions.

- **Historical practices may become non-compliant.** Court or commissioner
  decisions can retroactively make common industry practices non-compliant.
  Organizations should monitor rulings proactively, not just when directly
  affected.

- **Guidance documents are not frozen in time.** Commissioner positions evolve.
  Relying on outdated guidance can lead to findings of non-compliance when
  enforcement priorities shift.

- **Consent interpretations have narrowed over time.** Courts and commissioners
  have progressively required more specific, informed, and meaningful consent.
  What constituted valid consent years ago may not meet current standards.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Supreme Court of Canada decisions | Binding on all courts | Final authority on constitutional privacy issues |
| Federal Court/FCA decisions | Binding precedent for PIPEDA | Lower courts must follow |
| OPC findings | Persuasive only | Not legally binding; courts may adopt or reject |
| AB, BC, QC commissioner orders | Binding within jurisdiction | Order-making authority differs from OPC |
| Time to appeal to Federal Court | 1 year | From Commissioner's final report under PIPEDA |
| Court review standard | Reasonableness | Courts review Commissioner interpretations deferentially |

## Related topics

- **Role of the OPC**: Commissioner's investigative powers and finding categories
  (Objective 1.7)
- **Complaint and investigation process**: How rulings arise from complaints
  (Objective 1.8)
- **Recent legal developments**: Current significant court decisions
  (Objective 1.9)
- **Provincial private sector laws**: Different enforcement and interpretive
  authorities in AB, BC, QC (Domain II.B)
- **Canadian governmental structure**: Division of powers affecting which courts
  and commissioners have jurisdiction (Objective 1.1)
- **Sources of law**: How judicial decisions fit within Canadian legal hierarchy
  (Objective 1.2)

### 1.11: Understand common governance principles for responsible AI (OECD AI Principles, NIST AI RMF)

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 1.11: Understand common governance principles for responsible AI

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **OECD AI Principles**: Adopted in 2019 by 46 countries including Canada, these
  principles establish international baseline standards for trustworthy AI.
  - Five values-based principles guide AI actors
  - Five recommendations for national policies and international cooperation
  - First intergovernmental standard on AI
  - Inform domestic AI governance frameworks worldwide

- **The five OECD AI values-based principles**:
  - **Inclusive growth, sustainable development, and well-being**: AI should
    benefit people and the planet
  - **Human-centered values and fairness**: AI must respect human rights,
    democratic values, and include safeguards for fair outcomes
  - **Transparency and explainability**: AI actors should provide meaningful
    information about AI systems appropriate to the context
  - **Robustness, security, and safety**: AI systems should function
    appropriately and not pose unreasonable safety risks
  - **Accountability**: AI actors should be accountable for proper functioning
    of AI systems based on their roles and context

- **NIST AI Risk Management Framework (AI RMF)**: Published January 2023, this
  voluntary framework helps organizations design, develop, deploy, and use AI
  systems responsibly.
  - Designed to be flexible and adaptable across sectors
  - Emphasizes ongoing risk management throughout the AI lifecycle
  - Addresses both enterprise-level and societal risks
  - Complements existing risk management practices

- **NIST AI RMF core functions**: The framework organizes AI risk management
  into four interconnected functions.
  - **Govern**: Establishes culture, policies, and accountability structures
    for AI risk management
  - **Map**: Identifies and documents AI system context, capabilities,
    limitations, and potential impacts
  - **Measure**: Employs quantitative and qualitative methods to assess and
    track AI risks
  - **Manage**: Prioritizes and responds to identified risks throughout the
    AI lifecycle

- **Canada's Voluntary Code of Conduct on Responsible AI**: Announced September
  2023, this code provides guidance for organizations developing and managing
  advanced generative AI systems in Canada.
  - Focuses on advanced generative AI systems specifically
  - Voluntary commitment by signatory organizations
  - Covers development, deployment, and management practices
  - Emphasizes accountability, transparency, and safety

- **Key elements of Canada's Voluntary Code**:
  - **Accountability**: Organizations should have governance structures for AI
    oversight and designate responsible individuals
  - **Safety**: Conduct safety assessments before and during deployment
  - **Fairness and equity**: Test for bias and discriminatory outputs
  - **Transparency**: Provide clear information about AI system capabilities
    and limitations
  - **Human oversight**: Maintain meaningful human control over AI systems
  - **Valid and reliable**: Ensure AI systems perform as intended

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Organization deploying generative AI in Canada | Apply Canada's Voluntary Code principles even if not a signatory | Code reflects government expectations for responsible AI development |
| Assessing AI system risks across lifecycle | Use NIST AI RMF's Map-Measure-Manage structure | Framework provides systematic approach to identifying and addressing AI risks |
| Developing AI governance policy | Reference OECD principles as foundational values | International consensus principles provide credible baseline |
| AI system produces unexplainable outputs | OECD transparency principle requires meaningful explanations | Context determines level of explanation needed; high-stakes decisions require more |
| Cross-border AI data processing | Consider how OECD principles align with privacy law requirements | Both frameworks emphasize accountability and human-centered values |
| Building AI oversight committee | NIST Govern function and Canada Code accountability requirements | Both frameworks require designated governance structures |

## Gotchas

- **OECD principles are not legally binding.** The OECD AI Principles are a
  soft law instrument. They guide policy development but do not create direct
  legal obligations. Organizations may still face liability under existing
  privacy and human rights laws.

- **NIST AI RMF is voluntary and flexible, not prescriptive.** The framework
  provides guidance, not checklists. Organizations must tailor implementation
  to their specific context. Claiming "NIST compliance" without substantive
  risk management is insufficient.

- **Canada's Voluntary Code is not a safe harbor.** Signing the code does not
  shield organizations from regulatory enforcement under PIPEDA, provincial
  privacy laws, or other applicable legislation.

- **Transparency does not mean full disclosure.** The OECD transparency
  principle calls for "meaningful" information "appropriate to the context."
  Trade secrets and security considerations may limit disclosure.

- **AI governance frameworks complement but do not replace privacy law.** AI
  systems processing personal information must still comply with PIPEDA or
  applicable provincial privacy laws. AI governance adds additional
  considerations, not substitutes.

- **"Human oversight" varies by risk level.** Not all AI systems require the
  same level of human intervention. Higher-risk applications (healthcare,
  employment, credit) require more meaningful human control.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| OECD AI Principles adoption | 2019 | 46 adhering countries including Canada |
| NIST AI RMF publication | January 2023 | Version 1.0; updates expected |
| NIST AI RMF core functions | 4 | Govern, Map, Measure, Manage |
| Canada Voluntary Code | September 2023 | Targets advanced generative AI systems |
| OECD values-based principles | 5 | Plus 5 policy recommendations |
| AI RMF characteristics of trustworthy AI | 7 | Valid/reliable, safe, secure, accountable, transparent, explainable, privacy-enhanced, fair |

## Related topics

- **Privacy Impact Assessments**: PIAs may be required when AI processes
  personal information (Domain III.B)
- **Consent for AI processing**: How consent requirements apply to AI systems
  using personal information (Domain II.A)
- **Cross-border data transfers**: AI systems may involve international data
  flows (Objective 1.13)
- **Safeguards for personal information**: Technical and organizational
  measures applicable to AI systems (Objective 1.6)
- **Privacy Commissioner oversight**: Commissioners may investigate AI-related
  privacy complaints (Objective 1.7)
- **PIPEDA accountability principle**: Organizational accountability extends
  to AI system governance (Domain II.A)

### 1.12: Know international privacy standards and when to apply them

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 1.12: Know international privacy standards and when to apply them

Source: CIPP/C Free Study Guide (IAPP 2025)

> **Note**: This free study guide provides only a high-level syllabus. For
> substantive content on international privacy standards, consult the full
> textbook "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the
> Practitioner" or supplementary IAPP training materials.

## Key concepts

- **International privacy standards**: Frameworks developed by international
  bodies that establish baseline privacy principles applicable across
  jurisdictions
  - Canadian privacy law draws heavily from these international foundations
  - The CSA Model Code (referenced in the study guide) incorporates OECD
    principles
  - PIPEDA's ten fair information principles align with international standards

- **When to apply international standards**:
  - Cross-border data transfers requiring adequacy assessments
  - Organizations operating in multiple jurisdictions
  - Gaps in domestic legislation where international guidance applies
  - Compliance programs seeking interoperability with foreign regimes

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Data transfer to non-adequate country | Apply contractual safeguards based on international principles | PIPEDA requires comparable protection |
| Multi-national compliance program | Map controls to both Canadian law and international frameworks | Demonstrates due diligence across jurisdictions |
| Interpreting ambiguous PIPEDA provisions | Reference OECD Guidelines as interpretive aid | PIPEDA was modeled on these principles |

## Gotchas

- **International standards are not directly enforceable in Canada**: They
  inform but do not replace PIPEDA, provincial PIPs, and sector-specific laws
- **"Substantially similar" does not mean identical**: Provincial laws deemed
  substantially similar to PIPEDA may differ in specific requirements while
  meeting the overall standard
- **Adequacy is not permanent**: International adequacy determinations can
  change; organizations must monitor developments

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| PIPEDA fair information principles | 10 | Based on CSA Model Code, which incorporates OECD principles |
| Provincial substantially similar laws | 3 (AB, BC, QC private sector) | Each has distinct requirements despite similar foundation |

## Related topics

- **CSA Model Code**: Canadian standard that codified international principles
  into Canadian context
- **GAPP framework**: Generally Accepted Privacy Principles referenced in study
  guide as compliance model
- **Cross-border transfers under PIPEDA**: Practical application of
  international adequacy concepts
- **Privacy commissioners' guidance**: Interpretive documents often reference
  international standards

*Word count: ~380. Limited by source material. Supplement with full textbook
content for exam preparation.*

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 1.12: Know international privacy standards and when to apply them

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Cross-border data transfers under PIPEDA**: Organizations transferring personal
  information outside Canada remain accountable for its protection.
  - The transferring organization must use contractual or other means to ensure
    comparable protection
  - No blanket prohibition on transfers to specific countries
  - Consent requirements apply to cross-border transfers when individuals would
    reasonably expect notification
  - Third-party processors abroad are subject to same accountability principles

- **EU General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) adequacy**: The European
  Commission has not granted Canada full adequacy status under GDPR Article 45.
  - Canada received partial adequacy for commercial organizations subject to
    PIPEDA only
  - Public sector transfers from EU require other legal mechanisms
  - Adequacy does not cover organizations in provinces with substantially similar
    legislation unless also subject to PIPEDA
  - Health sector not covered under the adequacy decision

- **Standard Contractual Clauses (SCCs)**: Legal mechanism for transferring
  personal data from the EU when adequacy does not apply.
  - Canadian organizations receiving data from EU controllers may need to sign SCCs
  - Updated SCCs adopted by EU Commission in 2021
  - Require supplementary measures assessment for transfers to third countries
  - May be required for sectors not covered by Canada's partial adequacy

- **APEC Cross-Border Privacy Rules (CBPR) System**: Voluntary accountability
  framework for cross-border data flows among APEC economies.
  - Canada is a participating economy
  - Organizations can be certified as CBPR-compliant
  - Provides alternative mechanism to contractual clauses
  - Based on APEC Privacy Framework principles
  - Includes Privacy Recognition for Processors (PRP) component

- **OECD Guidelines on the Protection of Privacy**: Foundational international
  privacy principles adopted in 1980 and updated in 2013.
  - Eight core privacy principles form basis of many national laws including
    PIPEDA
  - Collection Limitation, Data Quality, Purpose Specification, Use Limitation,
    Security Safeguards, Openness, Individual Participation, Accountability
  - Updated guidelines address privacy risk management and cross-border data flows
  - Canadian privacy framework directly influenced by OECD Guidelines

- **Binding Corporate Rules (BCRs)**: Internal policies for multinational
  organizations transferring personal data within their corporate group.
  - Approved by EU data protection authorities
  - Required for intra-group transfers from EU when adequacy does not apply
  - Canadian multinationals with EU operations may need BCRs for EU-to-Canada
    transfers
  - Cover both controller and processor transfers

- **Adequacy decisions and sectoral impact**: Canada's partial GDPR adequacy
  has different implications across sectors.
  - Healthcare: Not covered; requires SCCs or other mechanisms for EU data
  - Education: Public institutions not covered; private may be if PIPEDA applies
  - Finance: Covered if PIPEDA applies; federally regulated financial
    institutions subject to PIPEDA
  - Provincial private sector: May not be covered if only substantially similar
    law applies

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Canadian company receiving EU customer data | Rely on GDPR adequacy if PIPEDA applies; use SCCs for sectors not covered | Canada has partial adequacy for PIPEDA-covered commercial activities only |
| Transferring data to US cloud provider | Contractual protections and due diligence required under PIPEDA accountability | PIPEDA requires comparable protection regardless of destination |
| Healthcare organization receiving patient data from EU | Cannot rely on adequacy; implement SCCs and supplementary measures | Health sector not covered by Canada's GDPR adequacy decision |
| Canadian subsidiary of EU company | Parent may require BCRs or SCCs for intra-group transfers | EU controller remains accountable for transfers to Canadian entity |
| BC company only subject to BC PIPA | Cannot rely on Canada GDPR adequacy for EU data imports | Adequacy covers PIPEDA only, not substantially similar provincial laws |
| Participating in APEC trade activities | Consider CBPR certification for cross-border transfers | Provides recognized framework within APEC economies |

## Gotchas

- **Canada's adequacy is partial, not full.** The EU adequacy decision covers
  only commercial organizations subject to PIPEDA. Public sector, health sector,
  and organizations subject only to provincial legislation are excluded.

- **Accountability travels with the data.** Under PIPEDA, transferring data to a
  foreign processor does not transfer accountability. The Canadian organization
  remains responsible for protecting personal information regardless of where it
  is processed.

- **Provincial substantially similar laws are not covered by EU adequacy.**
  Organizations in Alberta, British Columbia, or Quebec that are subject only
  to provincial private sector privacy law (not PIPEDA) cannot rely on Canada's
  GDPR adequacy decision for EU data imports.

- **CBPR is voluntary and sector-agnostic.** Unlike GDPR adequacy, APEC CBPR
  certification applies to the certified organization's practices regardless of
  sector. However, CBPR is not recognized as a transfer mechanism under GDPR.

- **SCCs require supplementary measures analysis.** Following the Schrems II
  decision, organizations must assess whether the destination country's laws
  provide adequate protection and implement supplementary measures if needed.

- **Consent for cross-border transfers has limits.** While PIPEDA requires
  notification when individuals would reasonably expect it, consent is not a
  valid legal basis under GDPR for routine business transfers to third countries.

- **OECD Guidelines are principles, not law.** The OECD Guidelines influenced
  Canadian privacy law but are not directly enforceable. They establish
  international consensus on privacy principles.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| OECD Privacy Guidelines | 1980, updated 2013 | 8 core principles |
| GDPR effective date | May 25, 2018 | Canada adequacy predates GDPR |
| Canada GDPR adequacy scope | PIPEDA commercial organizations only | Excludes public, health, provincial-only |
| APEC CBPR participating economies | 9+ economies | Including Canada, US, Japan, Korea |
| EU SCCs adoption | June 2021 | Replaced 2001/2010 versions |
| SCC transition period | December 27, 2022 | Deadline for updating to new SCCs |
| PIPEDA cross-border accountability | Unlimited | Applies regardless of destination country |

## Related topics

- **OECD AI Principles**: International AI governance standards (Objective 1.11)
- **CSA Model Code**: Canadian privacy standards based on OECD principles
  (Objective 1.10)
- **PIPEDA accountability principle**: Foundation for cross-border transfer
  requirements (Domain II.A)
- **Consent requirements under PIPEDA**: When consent needed for transfers
  (Domain II.A)
- **Privacy Impact Assessments**: May be required for new cross-border data flows
  (Domain III.B)
- **Substantially similar provincial laws**: Determine which law applies and
  adequacy coverage (Domain II.B)

## Domain 2: Canadian Privacy Laws and Practices - Private Sector

**Weight: 19%**

### 2.1: Know the privacy principles that are the foundation of PIPEDA

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 2.1: Know the privacy principles that are the foundation of PIPEDA

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Key concepts

- **PIPEDA foundation**: The Personal Information Protection and Electronic Documents
  Act (PIPEDA) is a federal private-sector privacy law governing the collection, use,
  and disclosure of personal information in commercial activities.
  - Applies to organizations collecting, using, or disclosing personal information for
    commercial purposes
  - Provincial private-sector laws (Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, Quebec's Act Respecting the
    Protection of Personal Information in the Private Sector) may apply instead of
    PIPEDA in certain jurisdictions

- **CSA Model Code**: The Canadian Standards Association Model Code for the Protection
  of Personal Information serves as a foundational privacy framework.
  - Referenced as one of the "Canadian model codes for privacy compliance"
  - Provides principles that inform PIPEDA's structure

- **GAPP framework**: The Generally Accepted Privacy Principles framework is another
  Canadian model code for privacy compliance.

## Common scenarios

| Scenario                                              | Correct approach                     | Why                                      |
| ----------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------ | ---------------------------------------- |
| Organization collects personal information for sales  | Apply PIPEDA requirements            | Commercial purpose triggers PIPEDA       |
| Organization in BC collects customer data             | Determine if BC PIPA or PIPEDA apply | Provincial law may supersede PIPEDA      |
| Organization needs compliance framework               | Reference CSA Model Code and GAPP    | These codes inform Canadian privacy law  |

## Gotchas

- **PIPEDA scope limitation**: PIPEDA applies to commercial activities only - do not
  confuse with public sector requirements under the Privacy Act
- **Provincial overlap**: Organizations must determine whether PIPEDA or a provincial
  private-sector law applies based on jurisdiction and activity type

## Limits and defaults

| Item                    | Value                        | Notes                              |
| ----------------------- | ---------------------------- | ---------------------------------- |
| PIPEDA application      | Commercial purposes          | Federal private-sector law         |
| Provincial alternatives | Alberta, BC, Quebec          | Substantially similar legislation  |
| Model codes             | CSA Model Code, GAPP         | Foundational compliance frameworks |

## Related topics

- Objective 2.2: Understanding when private-sector legislation applies instead of
  PIPEDA
- Domain I: Development of privacy principles in Canada
- CSA Model Code ten principles (accountability, identifying purposes, consent,
  limiting collection, limiting use/disclosure/retention, accuracy, safeguards,
  openness, individual access, challenging compliance)

---

**Note**: This source provides only high-level exam preparation guidance. For
comprehensive coverage of PIPEDA's foundational privacy principles, consult the
official CIPP/C textbook "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the
Practitioner."

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.1: Know the privacy principles that are the foundation of PIPEDA

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **PIPEDA (Personal Information Protection and Electronic Documents Act)**: Federal
  private-sector privacy law enacted in 2000 (fully effective 2004). Governs how
  private-sector organizations collect, use and disclose personal information in the
  course of commercial activities.
  - Schedule 1 incorporates the CSA Model Code's 10 privacy principles
  - Applies to federally regulated industries and interprovincial/international
    commercial activities
  - Provincial laws may be deemed "substantially similar" and apply instead

- **The 10 PIPEDA Principles** (CSA Model Code): These principles form Schedule 1
  of PIPEDA and are legally binding. Organizations must comply with all ten:

  1. **Accountability**: An organization is responsible for personal information
     under its control. Must designate an individual accountable for compliance.
     - Accountability continues when information is transferred to third parties
     - Must implement policies and practices to give effect to principles
     - Privacy officer designation is required

  2. **Identifying Purposes**: Purposes for collecting personal information must be
     identified at or before the time of collection.
     - Purposes must be documented
     - New purposes require fresh consent before use
     - Purposes should be specific enough to be meaningful

  3. **Consent**: Knowledge and consent are required for collection, use or
     disclosure, except where inappropriate.
     - Consent must be meaningful and informed
     - Form of consent varies based on sensitivity and reasonable expectations
     - Express consent for sensitive information; implied may suffice for less
       sensitive contexts
     - Cannot require consent beyond what is necessary for the transaction

  4. **Limiting Collection**: Collection must be limited to what is necessary for
     identified purposes. Information must be collected by fair and lawful means.
     - "Necessary" is interpreted strictly
     - Prohibits collection for speculative future uses
     - Collection methods must not be deceptive

  5. **Limiting Use, Disclosure, and Retention**: Personal information shall not be
     used or disclosed for purposes other than those for which it was collected,
     except with consent or as required by law.
     - Retention only as long as necessary for purpose
     - Must have retention schedules and destruction procedures
     - Secondary uses require new consent

  6. **Accuracy**: Personal information must be as accurate, complete and up-to-date
     as necessary for the purposes for which it is used.
     - Accuracy requirements tied to consequences of inaccuracy
     - Information used for decisions about individuals requires higher accuracy
     - Must update when individual provides new information

  7. **Safeguards**: Personal information must be protected by security safeguards
     appropriate to the sensitivity of the information.
     - Administrative, technical and physical safeguards required
     - Protection level must match sensitivity
     - Employees must be trained on security procedures
     - Information awaiting destruction must also be protected

  8. **Openness**: Organizations must make information about their policies and
     practices readily available to individuals.
     - Privacy policies must be clear and accessible
     - Must identify who is accountable for compliance
     - Cannot hide behind complex legal language

  9. **Individual Access**: Upon request, individuals must be informed of the
     existence, use and disclosure of their personal information and given access
     to it, with limited exceptions.
     - Right to challenge accuracy and have information amended
     - Response required within 30 days
     - May charge reasonable cost-recovery fees
     - Exceptions for legal privilege, commercial confidentiality, third-party
       information

  10. **Challenging Compliance**: Individuals must be able to challenge an
      organization's compliance with these principles.
      - Must have procedures to receive and respond to complaints
      - Complaints may be escalated to Privacy Commissioner
      - Must investigate all complaints

- **Commercial Activity**: PIPEDA applies to the collection, use or disclosure of
  personal information in the course of commercial activity.
  - Buying, selling, bartering, leasing goods or services
  - Membership lists and customer databases
  - Employee information in federally regulated businesses

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Customer asks what data you hold | Provide access within 30 days | Individual Access: right to know what is held |
| Third-party processor handles data | Contract with privacy requirements | Accountability: remains with original organization |
| Collecting SIN for loyalty program | Do not collect; not necessary | Limiting Collection: must be necessary for purpose |
| Customer withdraws consent | Cease processing; inform of consequences | Consent: can be withdrawn; may affect service |
| Using customer data for new marketing | Obtain new consent first | Identifying Purposes/Consent: new purpose requires consent |
| Storing data indefinitely "just in case" | Implement retention schedules | Limiting Retention: only keep as needed |
| Customer disputes information accuracy | Review and correct if warranted | Accuracy/Challenging Compliance: must address challenges |

## Gotchas

- **Consent is not always required**: PIPEDA includes exceptions where consent is
  not required (legal requirements, emergencies, journalistic purposes, etc.). Do
  not assume consent is always necessary.

- **Accountability survives outsourcing**: When you transfer data to a third party,
  you remain accountable. A contract does not transfer accountability - it manages
  risk but you are still responsible.

- **"Necessary" is strict**: Limiting Collection requires information to be
  necessary, not merely useful. "Nice to have" data should not be collected.

- **Express vs. implied consent**: Sensitive information (health, financial, SIN)
  requires express consent. Less sensitive information may allow implied consent
  based on reasonable expectations.

- **Opt-out is not always valid**: Negative consent (assuming consent unless
  individual objects) is only appropriate for non-sensitive information where
  individual can reasonably expect the use.

- **Access exceptions are narrow**: You can deny access for legal privilege,
  third-party information, or commercial confidentiality, but must explain why
  access is denied and allow the individual to challenge the denial.

- **Consent cannot be a condition of service**: You cannot require consent to
  collection beyond what is necessary to provide the service. Bundling unrelated
  data collection with service provision violates this principle.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Number of PIPEDA principles | 10 | All derived from CSA Model Code |
| Access request response time | 30 days | May extend with notice in limited cases |
| PIPEDA enacted | 2000 | Phased implementation; fully effective 2004 |
| Schedule containing principles | Schedule 1 | CSA Model Code incorporated by reference |
| Breach notification required | Yes | Since November 2018 amendments |
| Mandatory breach records retention | 24 months | Records of all breaches, not just reported ones |

## Related topics

- **CSA Model Code**: The Canadian Standards Association developed the 10
  principles in 1996; PIPEDA adopted them as Schedule 1
- **Substantially similar laws**: Provincial laws (Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, Quebec
  Law 25) may apply instead of PIPEDA for intra-provincial commercial activity
- **OECD Guidelines**: The 8 OECD principles influenced the CSA Model Code and by
  extension PIPEDA
- **Consent exceptions (PIPEDA s. 7)**: Specific situations where consent is not
  required - critical to know the major exceptions
- **Privacy Commissioner**: Oversight authority that investigates complaints and
  issues guidance on PIPEDA compliance
- **Breach notification**: Mandatory since 2018; report to Commissioner and notify
  affected individuals when there is a real risk of significant harm

### 2.2: Understand the scope of application of PIPEDA and substantially similar laws

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 2.2: Understand the scope of application of PIPEDA and substantially similar laws

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Key concepts

- **PIPEDA applies to commercial activities**: PIPEDA governs organizations that
  collect, use, or disclose personal information for commercial purposes.
  - This is the primary jurisdictional trigger for PIPEDA
  - Non-commercial activities (journalism, personal use) are excluded

- **Provincial private-sector laws**: Several provinces have enacted their own
  private-sector privacy legislation:
  - Alberta's Personal Information Protection Act (PIPA)
  - British Columbia's Personal Information Protection Act (PIPA)
  - Quebec's Act Respecting the Protection of Personal Information in the
    Private Sector

- **Substantially similar designation**: When a province enacts privacy
  legislation deemed "substantially similar" to PIPEDA, the provincial law
  applies to intra-provincial commercial activities instead of PIPEDA.
  - BC, Alberta, and Quebec have substantially similar private-sector laws
  - PIPEDA continues to apply to interprovincial and international activities

- **Body of knowledge focus**: The CIPP/C exam tests candidates on "when
  private-sector legislation is applicable instead of PIPEDA" (BOK item II.B).

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Applicable law | Why |
| -------- | -------------- | --- |
| Commercial activity in province with substantially similar law | Provincial law | Local commercial activity defers to provincial legislation |
| Commercial activity crossing provincial borders | PIPEDA | Interprovincial commerce remains under federal jurisdiction |
| Federally regulated business (banking, telecom) | PIPEDA | Federal industries always subject to PIPEDA regardless of province |

## Gotchas

- **Commercial purpose is key**: PIPEDA applies to commercial activities. The
  example question in the study guide tests whether candidates understand this
  fundamental scope requirement.

- **Substantially similar does not mean identical**: Provincial laws may have
  provisions that differ from PIPEDA while still providing comparable
  protections.

- **Dual compliance possible**: Organizations operating across provincial
  borders may need to comply with both PIPEDA (for interprovincial activities)
  and provincial law (for local activities).

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Provinces with substantially similar private-sector laws | 3 | BC, Alberta, Quebec |
| PIPEDA application | Commercial activities | Non-commercial activities excluded |
| Federal industries | Always PIPEDA | Banking, telecom, interprovincial transport |

## Related topics

- **PIPEDA principles**: The 10 principles in Schedule 1 that provincial laws
  must match to be deemed substantially similar
- **Provincial commissioners**: Oversight of provincial private-sector laws by
  provincial privacy commissioners
- **Federally regulated industries**: Banking, telecommunications, broadcasting,
  interprovincial transportation

---

Note: This extract is based on the IAPP Free Study Guide, which provides an
overview of exam topics. For comprehensive coverage, consult the full textbook
"Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the Practitioner."

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.2: Understand the scope of application of PIPEDA and substantially similar laws

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **PIPEDA's territorial scope**: PIPEDA applies to organizations that collect, use,
  or disclose personal information in the course of commercial activities across
  provincial or national borders, or to federally regulated businesses regardless
  of where they operate.
  - Federal works, undertakings, or businesses (banking, telecommunications,
    interprovincial transportation, broadcasting)
  - Commercial activities crossing provincial or international borders
  - Provinces without substantially similar legislation

- **Substantially similar designation**: The Governor in Council may exempt an
  organization or class of activities from PIPEDA if a province has enacted
  legislation deemed "substantially similar" to the federal law.
  - Provincial law must provide comparable protections
  - Office of the Privacy Commissioner of Canada provides recommendations
  - Once designated, provincial law applies to intra-provincial commercial
    activities instead of PIPEDA

- **Provinces with substantially similar private-sector laws**:
  - British Columbia: Personal Information Protection Act (PIPA BC) - designated
    January 1, 2004
  - Alberta: Personal Information Protection Act (PIPA Alberta) - designated
    January 1, 2004
  - Quebec: Act Respecting the Protection of Personal Information in the Private
    Sector (Law 25 modernization effective 2023-2024) - designated since 2003

- **When PIPEDA still applies despite provincial law**: Even in provinces with
  substantially similar legislation, PIPEDA continues to apply to:
  - Interprovincial and international commercial transactions
  - Federally regulated employers and their employee information
  - Federal works, undertakings, and businesses (airlines, banks, telecoms,
    railways, broadcasters)
  - Collection, use, or disclosure connected to federal jurisdiction

- **Dual application scenarios**: An organization may need to comply with both
  PIPEDA and provincial law depending on the nature of the activity:
  - Local retail operations follow provincial law
  - Online sales to customers in other provinces trigger PIPEDA
  - A single organization may have different obligations for different activities

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Applicable law | Why |
| -------- | -------------- | --- |
| BC retailer sells only within BC | BC PIPA | Intra-provincial commercial activity in substantially similar province |
| BC retailer sells online nationally | PIPEDA | Interprovincial commercial activity |
| Bank operating in Alberta | PIPEDA | Federally regulated industry regardless of province |
| Quebec employer (non-federal) | Quebec law | Employee information covered by provincial legislation |
| Telecom company in Ontario | PIPEDA | Federally regulated industry |
| Alberta company doing business with US clients | PIPEDA | International commercial activity |
| Ontario retailer (local only) | PIPEDA | Ontario lacks substantially similar private-sector law |

## Gotchas

- **Federal industries always under PIPEDA**: Even if a federally regulated company
  operates entirely within a province with substantially similar legislation,
  PIPEDA applies. The nature of the industry, not the location of activity,
  determines jurisdiction for federal works and undertakings.

- **Provincial law applies to local activities only**: The substantially similar
  exemption covers intra-provincial commercial activities. The moment a transaction
  crosses provincial or national borders, PIPEDA applies.

- **Employee information complexity**: Whether employee information falls under
  PIPEDA or provincial law depends on whether the employer is federally regulated.
  Non-federal employers in BC, Alberta, and Quebec follow provincial rules;
  Ontario employers may follow PIPEDA (though Ontario has no substantially similar
  private-sector law, making this moot for most cases).

- **Not all provinces have substantially similar laws**: Only BC, Alberta, and
  Quebec have private-sector laws deemed substantially similar. In all other
  provinces and territories, PIPEDA applies to all commercial activities, including
  purely local ones.

- **Health-sector overlap**: Some provinces have health-sector privacy laws deemed
  substantially similar. These are separate designations from private-sector laws.
  An organization handling both commercial and health information may face multiple
  regulatory frameworks.

- **Quebec Law 25 modernization**: Quebec's law underwent significant amendments
  effective 2023-2024, strengthening requirements. Organizations operating in
  Quebec must ensure compliance with the updated provisions while the substantially
  similar designation continues.

- **Organizational vs. activity-based analysis**: Determining which law applies
  requires analyzing specific activities, not just organizational characteristics.
  A single organization may be subject to different laws for different activities.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Provinces with substantially similar private-sector laws | 3 | BC, Alberta, Quebec |
| BC PIPA designation date | January 1, 2004 | Private-sector law |
| Alberta PIPA designation date | January 1, 2004 | Private-sector law |
| Quebec private-sector designation | 2003 | Modernized by Law 25 (2023-2024) |
| Provinces where PIPEDA applies to local commercial activity | 10 | All except BC, AB, QC |
| Federally regulated industries | Always under PIPEDA | Banking, telecom, airlines, etc. |

## Related topics

- **Federally regulated industries**: Banking, interprovincial and international
  transportation, telecommunications, broadcasting - always subject to PIPEDA
- **Provincial private-sector laws**: BC PIPA, Alberta PIPA, Quebec Law 25 - each
  has unique provisions beyond PIPEDA baseline
- **PIPEDA principles**: The 10 principles in Schedule 1 form the baseline that
  provincial laws must match to be deemed substantially similar
- **Health-sector substantially similar laws**: Separate designation process for
  provincial health privacy laws
- **Interprovincial and international commerce**: Cross-border commercial activity
  triggers PIPEDA regardless of substantially similar provincial legislation
- **Governor in Council designation**: The formal process by which provincial laws
  are recognized as substantially similar to PIPEDA

### 2.3: Know which private sector industries fall under federal vs provincial laws

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 2.3: Know which private sector industries fall under federal vs provincial laws

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Source limitation

The free study guide provides only introductory exam preparation information. It
does not contain substantive content on federal vs provincial jurisdiction over
private-sector industries. See the BOK extraction for detailed coverage.

## Key concepts

- **PIPEDA scope**: The study guide notes that PIPEDA applies to "any Canadian
  organization that collects, uses or discloses personal information for
  commercial purposes."
  - This establishes federal jurisdiction over commercial data handling
  - Provincial substantially similar laws (Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, Quebec) may
    apply instead for intra-provincial activity

- **Provincial private-sector laws**: Three provinces have their own
  private-sector legislation recognized as substantially similar to PIPEDA.
  - Alberta PIPA
  - British Columbia PIPA
  - Quebec's Act Respecting the Protection of Personal Information in the
    Private Sector

## Decision heuristics

| When | Do | Because |
| ---- | -- | ------- |
| Organization collects data for commercial purposes | Apply PIPEDA or provincial equivalent | PIPEDA governs commercial collection, use, and disclosure |
| Province has substantially similar legislation | Check if provincial law applies instead of PIPEDA | Alberta, BC, and Quebec have their own private-sector laws |

## Related topics

- **Body of Knowledge Domain II.B**: "Know when private-sector legislation is
  applicable instead of PIPEDA" - this objective directly addresses the
  federal/provincial jurisdiction question
- **Substantially similar legislation**: Provincial laws that exempt
  organizations from PIPEDA for intra-provincial commercial activity

## Recommended supplementary source

For exam preparation on this objective, consult:
- IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge extraction (detailed coverage)
- "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the Practitioner"
  (authoritative textbook)

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.3: Know which private sector industries fall under federal vs provincial laws

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Constitutional division of powers**: The Canadian Constitution divides
  legislative authority between federal and provincial governments. Privacy
  jurisdiction follows this division, with federally regulated industries
  governed by PIPEDA regardless of provincial substantially similar legislation.
  - Section 91: Federal powers (banking, navigation, interprovincial works)
  - Section 92: Provincial powers (property and civil rights, local matters)
  - Trade and commerce power enables federal privacy regulation of commercial
    activities crossing provincial boundaries

- **Federally regulated industries (always under PIPEDA)**: Certain industries
  fall under exclusive federal jurisdiction as "federal works, undertakings, or
  businesses" and remain subject to PIPEDA even in provinces with substantially
  similar legislation.
  - Banking and financial institutions (chartered banks, federally regulated
    credit unions)
  - Telecommunications (telephone companies, internet service providers,
    broadcasting)
  - Interprovincial and international transportation (airlines, railways,
    interprovincial trucking, marine shipping)
  - Broadcasting (radio, television, cable, satellite)
  - Navigation and shipping
  - Federal Crown corporations and enterprises
  - Uranium mining and atomic energy

- **Provincially regulated industries**: Industries falling under provincial
  jurisdiction are governed by provincial private-sector privacy laws (in BC,
  Alberta, Quebec) or PIPEDA (in provinces without substantially similar laws).
  - Retail and hospitality
  - Manufacturing (except interprovincial distribution)
  - Healthcare delivery (subject to health-specific privacy laws)
  - Real estate
  - Professional services (law, accounting, consulting)
  - Education (private institutions)
  - Insurance (provincially incorporated insurers)
  - Credit unions (provincially regulated)
  - Local transportation (taxis, municipal transit)
  - Construction and trades

- **Employee information jurisdiction**: Employee personal information follows
  the regulatory status of the employer.
  - Federally regulated employers: PIPEDA governs employee information
  - Provincially regulated employers in BC, Alberta, Quebec: Provincial law
    applies
  - Provincially regulated employers elsewhere: PIPEDA does not generally apply
    to employee information (regulatory gap in some provinces)

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Applicable law | Why |
| -------- | -------------- | --- |
| TD Bank branch in Quebec | PIPEDA | Chartered bank is federally regulated |
| Rogers customer in BC | PIPEDA | Telecommunications is federally regulated |
| Air Canada in any province | PIPEDA | Airlines are federally regulated |
| Provincially regulated credit union in Alberta | Alberta PIPA | Not a chartered bank; provincial entity |
| Local taxi company in Quebec | Quebec law | Local transportation is provincial |
| VIA Rail | PIPEDA | Interprovincial railway is federally regulated |
| Private hospital in Ontario | PIPEDA | Ontario lacks substantially similar private-sector law |
| Law firm in Vancouver | BC PIPA | Professional services are provincially regulated |
| Interprovincial trucking company | PIPEDA | Interprovincial transportation is federal |
| Local delivery service in Toronto | PIPEDA | Ontario lacks substantially similar law |

## Gotchas

- **Chartered banks vs. credit unions**: Chartered banks (TD, RBC, BMO) are
  federally regulated and always under PIPEDA. Credit unions are typically
  provincially regulated unless federally incorporated, making them subject to
  provincial law in BC, Alberta, and Quebec.

- **Insurance company complexity**: Federally incorporated insurers fall under
  PIPEDA; provincially incorporated insurers follow provincial law (where
  applicable). An organization must verify incorporation status, not just where
  it operates.

- **Telecommunications includes ISPs**: Internet service providers are part of
  the telecommunications sector and federally regulated. A BC-based ISP remains
  under PIPEDA, not BC PIPA.

- **Broadcasting is broad**: Cable companies, satellite providers, and streaming
  services with broadcasting licenses fall under federal jurisdiction and PIPEDA,
  even for local content delivery.

- **Subsidiary structure matters**: A subsidiary of a federal work may be
  provincially regulated if it operates independently in a provincial sector.
  The regulatory status of the parent does not automatically transfer.

- **Interprovincial vs. local transportation**: A trucking company operating
  only within one province falls under provincial regulation. The same company
  expanding to interprovincial routes becomes federally regulated for those
  activities.

- **Mixed activities**: An organization with both federally and provincially
  regulated activities may face dual compliance obligations. The analysis is
  activity-based, not entity-based.

- **Federal Crown corporations**: Organizations like Canada Post operate under
  PIPEDA regardless of where they conduct business or whether they compete with
  private-sector provincially regulated entities.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Key federally regulated sectors | 4 | Banking, telecom, transportation, broadcasting |
| Provinces with private-sector substantially similar laws | 3 | BC, Alberta, Quebec |
| Employee info under PIPEDA for federal employers | Yes | Always applies to federally regulated employers |
| Employee info under PIPEDA for provincial employers | No | Except in provinces relying on PIPEDA |
| PIPEDA application in non-substantially-similar provinces | All commercial activity | Includes local transactions |

## Related topics

- **Substantially similar legislation**: Provincial laws that exempt
  organizations from PIPEDA for intra-provincial commercial activity
- **Constitutional jurisdiction**: Section 91 and 92 of the Constitution Act
  determine federal vs. provincial regulatory authority
- **Federal works, undertakings, and businesses**: The constitutional category
  that determines federal jurisdiction over certain industries
- **Interprovincial commerce**: Commercial activity crossing provincial
  boundaries triggers PIPEDA application
- **Employee information**: Different treatment depending on employer's
  regulatory status and province of operation
- **Health-sector laws**: Separate framework of provincial health privacy laws
  with their own substantially similar designations

### 2.4: Understand what is and is not a commercial activity

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 2.4: Understand what is and is not a commercial activity

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Source limitation

The IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide is an introductory preparation document that
does not contain substantive content on this objective. The guide covers exam
format, preparation recommendations, and a high-level body of knowledge outline
only.

The only relevant content in this source is:

- "PIPEDA applies to any Canadian organization that collects, uses or discloses
  personal information for commercial purposes."

For comprehensive coverage of commercial activity definitions, exceptions, and
scenarios, refer to the authoritative textbook extract in
`books/iapp-cippc-bok/objectives/iapp-cippc-2.4.md`.

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.4: Understand what is and is not a commercial activity

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Commercial activity under PIPEDA**: PIPEDA applies to the collection, use,
  or disclosure of personal information in the course of commercial activities.
  Section 2(1) defines commercial activity as "any particular transaction, act
  or conduct or any regular course of conduct that is of a commercial character,
  including the selling, bartering or leasing of donor, membership or other
  fundraising lists."
  - Must involve a commercial character or commercial purpose
  - Includes regular business operations and one-time commercial transactions
  - Selling or leasing lists is explicitly commercial even for non-profits
  - Exchange of value (monetary or otherwise) indicates commercial character

- **Activities that are commercial**: Activities where PIPEDA applies because
  of their commercial character.
  - Sale of goods and services to consumers or businesses
  - Customer relationship management and marketing activities
  - Employee information in federally regulated organizations
  - Leasing or selling donor or membership lists
  - B2B transactions and commercial partnerships
  - E-commerce and online retail operations
  - Financial services and banking transactions
  - Commercial data processing and analytics services

- **Activities that are not commercial**: Certain activities fall outside
  PIPEDA's commercial activity scope entirely.
  - Personal or domestic purposes (individual acting in personal capacity)
  - Journalistic, artistic, or literary purposes
  - Federal government institutions (covered by Privacy Act)
  - Provincial government institutions (covered by provincial FOIPPAs)
  - Not-for-profit activities that lack commercial character
  - Political party activities (membership, donations, advocacy)
  - Employment relationships in provincially regulated organizations (in most
    provinces without substantially similar laws)

- **Non-profit organizations and commercial activity**: Non-profits present
  complex determinations. Their status as non-profit does not automatically
  exempt them from PIPEDA.
  - Commercial activities of non-profits are covered (selling goods, paid
    services, list rentals)
  - Non-commercial activities are not covered (volunteer coordination, member
    communications for non-commercial purposes)
  - Fundraising may or may not be commercial depending on the specific activity
  - Selling or leasing donor lists is explicitly commercial under the definition

- **Employee information exception**: PIPEDA's application to employee personal
  information depends on the employer's regulatory status, not the nature of the
  employment relationship.
  - Federally regulated employers: PIPEDA applies to employee information
  - Provincially regulated employers in provinces without substantially similar
    laws: PIPEDA generally does not apply to employee information (regulatory
    gap)
  - Provincially regulated employers in BC, Alberta, Quebec: Provincial law
    applies to employee information

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Commercial activity? | Why |
| -------- | -------------------- | --- |
| Retail store collecting customer purchase data | Yes | Direct sale of goods to consumers |
| Charity selling t-shirts to raise funds | Yes | Sale of merchandise is commercial |
| Non-profit renting its donor list | Yes | Explicitly included in statutory definition |
| Individual selling personal items online | No | Personal/domestic purpose exception |
| Journalist researching a story | No | Journalistic purpose exception |
| Political party collecting member data | No | Not of a commercial character |
| Hospital providing patient care | No | Not commercial; health legislation applies |
| Private clinic charging for services | Yes | Commercial provision of health services |
| Professional association providing paid courses | Yes | Sale of services is commercial |
| Volunteer coordinator managing volunteer data | No | Volunteer management lacks commercial character |
| Bank processing customer transactions | Yes | Financial services are commercial |
| University admissions office | No | Educational institution; provincial public sector law applies |
| Private tutoring company | Yes | Commercial provision of educational services |

## Gotchas

- **Non-profit does not mean non-commercial**: An organization's non-profit
  status is irrelevant to PIPEDA application. The test is whether the specific
  activity has a commercial character. A registered charity selling goods or
  renting lists engages in commercial activity subject to PIPEDA.

- **Mixed activities require separate analysis**: An organization may conduct
  both commercial and non-commercial activities. PIPEDA applies only to the
  commercial activities. A charity's fundraising dinner ticket sales are
  commercial; its volunteer appreciation event is not.

- **Personal purpose exception is narrow**: The personal or domestic purpose
  exception applies only to individuals acting in their personal capacity.
  Operating a side business from home is still commercial. The test is the
  nature of the activity, not the location.

- **Journalistic purpose has limits**: The exception protects journalistic,
  artistic, and literary purposes. It does not extend to an organization's
  marketing materials or corporate communications merely because they contain
  creative content.

- **Free services can be commercial**: The absence of direct monetary payment
  does not mean an activity is non-commercial. Services funded by advertising
  revenue or data monetization have a commercial character. The exchange of
  personal information for free services is itself commercial.

- **Employment relationship complexity**: In provinces without substantially
  similar private-sector laws (other than BC, Alberta, Quebec), PIPEDA does not
  generally cover employee personal information for provincially regulated
  employers. This creates a regulatory gap, not an indication that employment
  is non-commercial.

- **Government contractors**: Private organizations providing services under
  government contract remain subject to PIPEDA for their commercial activities,
  even though the government client would be subject to the Privacy Act.

- **Professional services are commercial**: Lawyers, accountants, and other
  professionals providing services for fees engage in commercial activities.
  Professional regulatory requirements do not displace PIPEDA obligations.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Statutory definition source | PIPEDA s. 2(1) | "Commercial activity" is defined in the Act |
| Explicit inclusion | Selling/leasing donor, membership, fundraising lists | Removes ambiguity for non-profit list activities |
| Personal purpose exception | Yes | Individuals acting for personal/domestic purposes |
| Journalistic/artistic/literary exception | Yes | Protects specified expressive purposes |
| Government institution exception | Yes | Federal: Privacy Act; Provincial: FOIPPAs |
| Employee information in federal sector | Covered | Always under PIPEDA for federally regulated employers |
| Employee information in provincial sector | Varies | Provincial law in BC, AB, QC; gap elsewhere |

## Related topics

- **Substantially similar legislation**: Provincial laws (BC, Alberta, Quebec)
  that apply instead of PIPEDA for intra-provincial commercial activities
- **Federally regulated industries**: Sectors always under federal jurisdiction
  regardless of provincial laws (banking, telecom, transportation, broadcasting)
- **Purpose limitation**: Identifying purposes for collection is required once
  commercial activity is established
- **Consent requirements**: Once PIPEDA applies, consent obligations follow for
  collection, use, and disclosure
- **Privacy Act**: Federal public sector privacy law that applies instead of
  PIPEDA for government institutions
- **Health sector privacy laws**: Provincial health legislation that applies to
  health information regardless of commercial character

### 2.5: Know that accountability remains with the organization including when using third parties

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 2.5: Know that accountability remains with the organization including when using third parties

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Key concepts

- **Accountability principle**: The foundational principle under PIPEDA and the
  CSA Model Code. An organization remains responsible for personal information
  under its control, including information transferred to third parties.
  - Accountability is the first of PIPEDA's 10 principles (Schedule 1)
  - Cannot be delegated or contracted away to service providers
  - Applies throughout the entire data lifecycle

- **Third-party processing**: When an organization transfers personal
  information to another party for processing, the original organization
  retains accountability.
  - Common scenarios: cloud providers, payroll processors, call centers
  - Original organization must ensure third parties provide comparable
    protection
  - Contractual agreements establish third-party obligations

- **Organizational responsibility**: Organizations must designate an individual
  responsible for compliance with privacy principles.
  - Required under PIPEDA Principle 1
  - Applies to all personal information the organization controls
  - Includes oversight of third-party handling

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Outsourcing data processing | Use contracts to ensure comparable protection; maintain oversight | Accountability stays with the original organization |
| Third party experiences a breach | Original organization fulfills breach notification duties | Cannot transfer accountability for the data |
| Vendor wants secondary use of data | Deny unless individuals consented to that purpose | Original purpose limitations still apply |
| Cross-border transfer to processor | Ensure adequate protections regardless of location | Accountability remains domestic |

## Gotchas

- **Contracts do not transfer accountability**: A contract creates obligations
  for the third party but does not shift the organization's fundamental
  responsibility to individuals and regulators.

- **Breach notification remains with you**: Even when a third party causes a
  breach, the original organization must notify affected individuals and the
  OPC as required under PIPEDA.

- **Ongoing oversight required**: Due diligence is not one-time. Organizations
  must monitor third-party compliance throughout the relationship.

- **Individuals complain to you**: Data subjects seeking access or filing
  complaints approach the original organization, not the third-party processor.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| PIPEDA principle number | 1 | First of the 10 principles |
| Can accountability be transferred | No | Remains with the organization |
| Comparable protection required | Yes | Third parties must match safeguards |
| Written contracts required | Best practice | Contractual means ensure protection |
| Designated individual required | Yes | Someone must be accountable for compliance |

## Related topics

- **PIPEDA Principle 1 - Accountability**: The statutory basis for
  organizational responsibility
- **Safeguards (Principle 7)**: Third parties must implement appropriate
  security measures
- **Cross-border data transfers**: Adds complexity but does not eliminate
  accountability
- **Breach notification**: Obligations persist when third parties cause
  breaches
- **Purpose limitation**: Third-party use must align with original consented
  purposes

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.5: Know that accountability remains with the organization including when using third parties

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Accountability principle**: The first principle of the CSA Model Code and
  PIPEDA Schedule 1. An organization is responsible for personal information
  under its control, including information transferred to third parties for
  processing.
  - Must designate an individual accountable for compliance
  - Accountability cannot be delegated or contracted away
  - Responsibility persists throughout the entire data lifecycle

- **Third-party processors**: External organizations that handle personal
  information on behalf of the collecting organization. Common examples include:
  - Cloud service providers and data hosting
  - Payroll processors and HR service providers
  - Marketing and analytics service providers
  - IT service providers and managed services
  - Call centers and customer service vendors

- **Contractual protections**: Organizations must use contractual means to
  provide comparable levels of protection when transferring information to
  third parties.
  - Written agreements specifying permitted uses and disclosures
  - Required safeguards matching the transferring organization's standards
  - Breach notification obligations
  - Audit and oversight provisions
  - Limitations on subcontracting

- **Due diligence requirements**: Before engaging a third party, organizations
  must assess the processor's ability to protect personal information.
  - Evaluate security controls and practices
  - Review privacy policies and procedures
  - Assess compliance history and reputation
  - Consider jurisdictional factors for cross-border transfers
  - Document the assessment process

- **Ongoing oversight**: Accountability does not end once a contract is signed.
  Organizations must maintain oversight throughout the relationship.
  - Monitor third-party compliance with contractual terms
  - Conduct periodic reviews or audits
  - Respond to incidents involving third-party handling
  - Update agreements as requirements change
  - Terminate relationships when standards are not met

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Outsourcing payroll processing | Contractually require the processor to protect employee information and limit use to payroll purposes | Accountability remains with the employer; processor is an extension of the organization |
| Cloud storage of customer data | Due diligence on provider's security practices plus contractual protections; understand where data is stored | Organization remains accountable regardless of where data is physically located |
| Third party experiences a breach | Original organization must respond as if the breach occurred internally; notify affected individuals | Accountability cannot be transferred to the third party |
| Vendor wants to use data for marketing | Refuse unless explicit consent was obtained from individuals for that purpose | Original consent was for processing only; new uses require new consent |
| Subcontractor hired by primary vendor | Primary organization must ensure contractual chain extends protections to subcontractors | Accountability flows back regardless of how many parties handle the data |
| Third party located in another country | Assess adequacy of protections in that jurisdiction; include contractual safeguards | Cross-border transfer does not eliminate accountability |

## Gotchas

- **Contracts do not transfer accountability**: While contracts create
  obligations for the third party, the original organization remains fully
  accountable to individuals and regulators. A well-drafted contract protects
  the organization through indemnification but does not shift the fundamental
  duty to protect personal information.

- **Breach liability persists**: When a third party suffers a breach involving
  the organization's personal information, the original organization must still
  fulfill breach notification requirements under PIPEDA. The organization cannot
  point to the third party as solely responsible.

- **Consent scope must be respected**: Third parties can only use personal
  information for the purposes the individual consented to. The original
  organization must prevent third parties from using data for their own
  purposes unless separate consent exists.

- **Due diligence is not one-time**: Organizations sometimes conduct due
  diligence before signing a contract but fail to maintain oversight. Ongoing
  monitoring is required to ensure continued compliance.

- **Verbal assurances are insufficient**: Third-party accountability must be
  documented in writing. Contractual protections provide evidence of
  appropriate safeguards and a legal basis for enforcement.

- **Cross-border transfers add complexity**: When personal information is
  transferred to third parties in other countries, the organization must
  consider both Canadian requirements and the legal environment of the
  destination jurisdiction. Accountability remains with the Canadian
  organization regardless of where data is processed.

- **Consumer complaints come to you**: Individuals seeking access to their
  information or complaining about mishandling will approach the original
  organization. The organization cannot redirect them to the third party.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| PIPEDA principle number for accountability | Principle 1 | First of the 10 principles in Schedule 1 |
| Requirement for designated individual | Yes | Must designate someone accountable for compliance |
| Can accountability be contracted away | No | Remains with the organization |
| Requirement for comparable protection | Yes | Third parties must provide equivalent safeguards |
| Written contract required | Yes (best practice) | Contractual means to ensure protection |
| Ongoing oversight required | Yes | Must monitor third-party compliance |

## Related topics

- **Principle 1 - Accountability**: The foundational PIPEDA principle that
  establishes organizational responsibility for personal information
- **Safeguards (Principle 7)**: Third parties must implement appropriate
  administrative, technical, and physical safeguards
- **Cross-border data transfers**: International transfers to third parties
  add jurisdictional complexity while accountability remains domestic
- **Breach notification**: Organizations must notify individuals and OPC even
  when a third party caused the breach
- **Consent and purpose limitation**: Third-party use must align with the
  original purposes individuals consented to
- **Due diligence**: Pre-contract and ongoing assessment of third-party
  privacy and security practices
- **Privacy Impact Assessments**: PIAs may be warranted when engaging new
  third-party processors, especially for sensitive data or cross-border
  transfers

### 2.6: Identify roles and responsibilities of stakeholders in compliance efforts

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.6: Identify roles and responsibilities of stakeholders in compliance efforts

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Privacy officer**: The designated individual accountable for an organization's
  privacy compliance under PIPEDA's Principle 1 (Accountability).
  - Must have authority to implement and oversee the privacy program
  - Serves as the primary contact for privacy inquiries and complaints
  - Reports to senior leadership on compliance status
  - May be a dedicated role or assigned to an existing position

- **Senior management/executive leadership**: Establishes the organizational
  commitment to privacy and provides resources for compliance.
  - Sets the tone from the top for privacy culture
  - Approves privacy policies and budgets
  - Bears ultimate organizational accountability
  - Ensures privacy is integrated into business strategy

- **Legal counsel**: Provides interpretation of privacy legislation and advises
  on compliance obligations.
  - Interprets PIPEDA and provincial privacy law requirements
  - Reviews contracts for third-party accountability provisions
  - Advises on consent language and policy wording
  - Supports response to complaints and regulatory inquiries

- **Information technology**: Implements technical safeguards and manages
  systems that process personal information.
  - Deploys encryption, access controls, and security measures
  - Maintains data inventories and system documentation
  - Implements privacy-enhancing technologies
  - Supports breach detection and response capabilities

- **Human resources**: Manages employee personal information and supports
  privacy training initiatives.
  - Handles employee data in compliance with applicable law
  - Coordinates privacy awareness training programs
  - Implements access controls for HR systems
  - Manages employee-related access requests

- **Business units/operational staff**: Front-line personnel who collect and
  handle personal information in daily operations.
  - Collect personal information at point of contact
  - Provide privacy notices and obtain consent
  - Handle individual inquiries about personal information
  - Report privacy incidents through proper channels

- **Information security**: Develops and maintains safeguards protecting
  personal information from unauthorized access.
  - Implements administrative, technical, and physical safeguards
  - Conducts risk assessments and vulnerability testing
  - Responds to security incidents
  - Maintains security policies and procedures

- **Third-party service providers**: External organizations processing personal
  information on behalf of the organization.
  - Must comply with contractual privacy obligations
  - Implement safeguards comparable to the organization's standards
  - Report breaches and incidents to the contracting organization
  - Subject to oversight and audit provisions

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Customer complains about data handling | Privacy officer receives and investigates complaint; coordinates with relevant departments | Privacy officer is the designated contact for compliance issues |
| New system processes personal information | IT implements technical safeguards; privacy officer reviews for compliance; legal advises on consent | Multiple stakeholders contribute expertise to ensure compliant implementation |
| Employee requests access to their file | HR coordinates with privacy officer to fulfill request within timelines | HR manages employee data; privacy officer ensures proper process |
