| Third party experiences a data breach | Privacy officer coordinates response; legal advises on notification; IT supports investigation | Organization remains accountable; stakeholders work together under privacy officer coordination |
| Board asks about privacy compliance | Privacy officer reports to senior management; senior management reports to board | Accountability flows upward; privacy officer provides operational visibility |
| Marketing wants to use customer data for new purpose | Privacy officer reviews against original consent; legal advises on requirements | Cross-functional consultation before expanding data use |

## Gotchas

- **Accountability cannot be delegated away**: While the privacy officer
  coordinates compliance, organizational accountability remains with the
  organization itself. Designating a privacy officer does not shield senior
  management from responsibility.

- **Privacy officer title is not mandatory**: PIPEDA requires an accountable
  individual, not a specific job title. The function matters more than the
  label, though "privacy officer" is common practice.

- **All employees share responsibility**: Privacy is not solely the privacy
  officer's job. Every employee who handles personal information has a role
  in compliance through proper data handling practices.

- **Third parties do not assume your accountability**: When outsourcing to
  service providers, the organization retains accountability. Contractual
  arrangements create obligations but do not transfer fundamental
  responsibility.

- **Privacy officer needs adequate authority**: A privacy officer without
  access to senior leadership or sufficient resources cannot effectively
  fulfill the accountability requirement. Authority must match responsibility.

- **IT and security are distinct from privacy**: While technical safeguards
  are essential, privacy compliance extends beyond security to include
  consent, purpose limitation, access rights, and transparency. These
  functions must work together.

- **Cross-functional coordination is required**: Privacy compliance cannot
  operate in a silo. Effective programs require coordination between privacy,
  legal, IT, HR, security, and business units.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Designated individual required | Yes | PIPEDA Principle 1 requires someone accountable |
| Specific job title required | No | Function matters, not title |
| Must report to CEO | Not specified | Should have appropriate authority and access |
| Training for staff required | Yes (implied) | Safeguards include administrative controls like training |
| Third-party oversight required | Yes | Must use contractual means for comparable protection |
| Privacy officer contact public | Yes | Part of openness requirement |

## Related topics

- **Principle 1 - Accountability**: Foundation for designating responsible
  individuals and maintaining organizational accountability
- **Safeguards (Principle 7)**: Technical, administrative, and physical
  controls require coordination among IT, security, and operations
- **Openness (Principle 8)**: Requires making policies and contact information
  available; privacy officer typically serves as the designated contact
- **Third-party accountability**: Organizations must ensure service providers
  protect personal information through contractual and oversight mechanisms
- **Complaint handling (Principle 10)**: Privacy officer typically coordinates
  the process for challenging compliance and addressing complaints
- **Privacy Impact Assessments**: Cross-functional activity requiring input
  from privacy, IT, security, legal, and business stakeholders

### 2.7: Identify the purposes for collecting personal information

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.7: Identify the purposes for collecting personal information

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Identifying Purposes Principle (PIPEDA Principle 2)**: Organizations must
  identify the purposes for which personal information is collected at or before
  the time of collection.
  - Derived from CSA Model Code Principle 4.2
  - Purposes must be documented and communicated to the individual
  - Foundation for all subsequent use, disclosure, and retention decisions
  - Links directly to consent: meaningful consent requires understanding of
    purpose

- **Timing of purpose identification**: Purposes must be identified before or at
  the time of collection, not retroactively.
  - Cannot collect first and determine purpose later
  - Prohibits "collect now, figure out use later" approaches
  - Must identify purposes before seeking consent
  - Late identification invalidates the consent basis

- **Specificity requirement**: Purposes must be sufficiently specific to be
  meaningful to the individual.
  - Vague purposes like "business purposes" or "to improve our services" are
    inadequate
  - Must explain what the organization will actually do with the information
  - Specific purposes enable meaningful consent
  - OPC guidance: purposes should answer "what will you do with my data?"

- **Documentation of purposes**: Organizations must document identified purposes
  internally and make them available to individuals.
  - Written record of each collection purpose required
  - Supports accountability and openness principles
  - Enables compliance verification and auditing
  - Privacy policies must reflect documented purposes

- **New purpose identification**: When an organization identifies a new purpose
  for previously collected information, fresh consent is required.
  - Cannot expand use beyond originally stated purposes without consent
  - Must notify individual of new purpose before use
  - Individual has right to refuse consent for new purpose
  - Original collection remains valid for original purposes

- **Relationship to other principles**: Purpose identification is foundational
  to PIPEDA compliance and directly enables other principles.
  - Limiting Collection: collection must relate to identified purposes
  - Consent: meaningful consent requires understanding of purposes
  - Use and Disclosure: limited to identified purposes
  - Retention: tied to purpose fulfillment
  - Accuracy: determined by what purposes require

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Collecting email for order confirmation | State: "We collect your email to send order updates and shipping notifications" | Specific purpose enables meaningful consent |
| Retailer wants to use purchase history for marketing | Identify marketing purpose at collection; if not, obtain new consent | Cannot use for unstated purposes |
| Job application collects references | State: "Reference contacts will be used to verify employment history" | Applicant must know purpose before providing information |
| Website collects browsing data | Identify purposes in privacy policy before collection begins | Purpose identification must precede collection |
| Organization wants to share data with partners | Must have identified third-party sharing as a purpose initially | Sharing is a use requiring prior purpose identification |
| Loyalty program collects purchase patterns | Explain all analysis purposes: "We analyze purchases to recommend products and provide personalized offers" | Specify each distinct use |
| Health club collects emergency contact | State: "Emergency contact used only in case of medical emergency during visit" | Narrow purpose limits future use |
| Bank collecting income information | Identify all purposes: credit decisions, regulatory compliance, product offerings | Multiple purposes require complete disclosure |

## Gotchas

- **Generic purposes are insufficient**: "To provide services" or "for business
  purposes" does not satisfy the specificity requirement. The OPC has found such
  statements inadequate because they do not inform individuals of actual uses.

- **Purpose creep invalidates consent**: If an organization uses information for
  purposes beyond those identified, the original consent does not cover the new
  use. This constitutes a PIPEDA violation even if the individual would have
  consented had they been asked.

- **Collection for potential future use is prohibited**: Collecting information
  because it "might be useful someday" without an identified current purpose
  violates the principle. Speculative collection is not permitted.

- **Third-party transfers require purpose identification**: If information will
  be shared with third parties, this must be identified as a purpose. The
  identity of recipients or categories of recipients should be specified.

- **Implied consent does not excuse purpose identification**: Even where implied
  consent is appropriate, the organization must still identify purposes. The
  consent mechanism differs, but the purpose identification requirement remains.

- **Bundled purposes require separate identification**: When information serves
  multiple purposes, each purpose must be separately identified. Individuals
  should understand the full scope of intended uses.

- **Oral identification is permitted but risky**: While PIPEDA does not require
  written notice in all cases, organizations should document that purposes were
  identified and how. Oral identification creates evidence challenges.

- **Statutory collection does not bypass identification**: When collection is
  required by law, the organization must still identify this purpose to the
  individual, even though consent may not be required.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| When purposes must be identified | At or before collection | Not retroactive |
| Standard for specificity | Meaningful to individual | Generic descriptions insufficient |
| New purpose requires | Fresh consent | Before new use begins |
| Documentation requirement | Written record | Internal and external (privacy policy) |
| Form of identification | Flexible | Written, oral, or implied by context |
| Privacy policy update | Before collection | Reflect purposes before gathering information |

## Related topics

- **Consent (Principle 3)**: Purpose identification is prerequisite to valid
  consent; individuals cannot consent meaningfully without knowing purposes
- **Limiting Collection (Principle 4)**: Collection must be necessary for
  identified purposes; purpose identification constrains what may be collected
- **Limiting Use and Disclosure (Principle 5)**: Information can only be used or
  disclosed for purposes identified at collection, absent new consent
- **Openness (Principle 8)**: Privacy policies must reflect identified purposes
  and be accessible to individuals
- **OPC guidance on consent**: Office of the Privacy Commissioner has issued
  detailed guidance on meaningful consent, emphasizing purpose clarity
- **Privacy notices**: Practical mechanism for communicating purposes to
  individuals at point of collection
- **Purpose limitation under provincial laws**: Alberta, BC, and Quebec private
  sector laws have similar purpose identification requirements with minor
  variations in language

### 2.8: Acquire meaningful and valid consent (reasonableness, opt-out mechanisms, new purposes)

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 2.8: Acquire meaningful and valid consent (reasonableness, opt-out mechanisms, new purposes)

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

> **Note**: This free study guide is a preparation overview document that does
> not contain detailed content on consent requirements. See the IAPP CIPP/C
> textbook "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the
> Practitioner" for comprehensive coverage.

## Key concepts

- **Meaningful consent**: Consent that an individual provides with genuine
  understanding of what they are agreeing to. Requires clear notice of purposes
  and how personal information will be handled.
- **Valid consent**: Consent obtained in compliance with PIPEDA and provincial
  private sector laws. Must meet form requirements (express or implied) based on
  sensitivity of information and reasonable expectations.
- **Form of consent**: The manner in which consent is obtained - express
  (explicit opt-in) or implied (reasonable inference from actions). Sensitivity
  of information determines which form is required.
- **Consent as a condition of service**: Organizations cannot require consent to
  collection, use, or disclosure beyond what is necessary for the stated
  purpose as a condition of providing a product or service.
- **Opt-out mechanisms**: Methods allowing individuals to withdraw consent for
  certain uses of their personal information. Must be clear, accessible, and
  easy to use.
- **New purposes**: When an organization wants to use personal information for a
  purpose not identified at the time of collection, it must obtain fresh consent
  before that new use.

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Collecting sensitive information (health, financial) | Obtain express consent | Sensitivity requires explicit agreement |
| Marketing to existing customers | Provide clear opt-out mechanism | Implied consent may exist but must be easy to withdraw |
| Changing how collected data will be used | Obtain new consent before new use | Original consent does not cover new purposes |
| Requiring consent for unrelated purposes | Do not tie consent to service provision | Cannot bundle unrelated consents |

## Gotchas

- **Trap: Assuming blanket consent covers all uses**: Original consent is
  limited to stated purposes. Any new purpose requires fresh consent even for
  existing data.
- **Trap: Relying on buried terms**: Consent is not meaningful if the individual
  cannot reasonably understand what they are agreeing to. Privacy policies must
  be clear and accessible.
- **Trap: Making opt-out difficult**: Opt-out mechanisms must be as easy as
  opting in. Complex or hidden withdrawal processes undermine valid consent.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Express consent requirement | Sensitive information | Health, financial, children's data |
| Implied consent acceptable | Non-sensitive, reasonable expectations | Routine commercial transactions |
| Consent withdrawal | Must be honoured | Subject to legal or contractual restrictions |

## Related topics

- **2.7 Identify purposes**: Purposes must be identified before or at collection
  to support meaningful consent
- **2.9 Consent exceptions**: Situations where consent is not required
- **2.10 Collection, use, disclosure rules**: Consent ties to permissible
  handling of personal information
- **1.4 CSA Model Code**: Consent is the third principle in the ten fair
  information principles

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.8: Acquire meaningful and valid consent

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Meaningful consent**: Consent is only valid when individuals understand what
  they are agreeing to.
  - Requires clear, plain language explanation of purposes
  - Individual must understand what personal information is collected
  - Must know how information will be used and disclosed
  - OPC emphasizes consent is not meaningful if buried in lengthy terms

- **Valid consent requirements**: PIPEDA Principle 3 establishes that knowledge
  and consent are required for collection, use, or disclosure.
  - Consent must be obtained before or at time of collection
  - Cannot be unduly vague or overly broad
  - Must be specific to identified purposes
  - Individual must have genuine ability to decline

- **Reasonableness standard**: Under PIPEDA section 5(3), consent is only valid
  if a reasonable person would consider the collection, use, or disclosure
  appropriate in the circumstances.
  - Balances organizational needs against individual privacy expectations
  - Sensitive information requires higher justification threshold
  - Context matters: what is reasonable for a bank differs from a retailer
  - OPC applies this standard when assessing consent validity

- **Forms of consent**: The appropriate form depends on sensitivity and
  reasonable expectations.
  - **Express consent**: Explicit affirmative action required for sensitive
    information, unexpected uses, or significant consequences
  - **Implied consent**: May be inferred from actions where purposes are obvious
    and information is less sensitive
  - **Opt-out consent**: Individual is notified and can decline; appropriate for
    certain less sensitive uses like marketing

- **Opt-out mechanisms**: When opt-out consent is used, organizations must
  provide accessible and effective withdrawal methods.
  - Must be simple and not require excessive effort
  - Explained at time consent is obtained
  - Must honor opt-out requests promptly
  - Cannot impose penalties for opting out
  - CASL has specific unsubscribe requirements for electronic messages

- **New purposes require fresh consent**: When an organization wants to use
  information for purposes not originally identified, new consent is required.
  - Cannot rely on original consent for expanded uses
  - Must notify individual of new purpose before use
  - Individual can refuse without affecting original consent
  - Exception: new purpose is clearly consistent with original

- **Consent as a condition of service**: Organizations cannot require consent
  beyond what is necessary to provide the product or service.
  - Cannot bundle unrelated data practices with service delivery
  - Required elements only: consent for purposes necessary to fulfill request
  - Optional elements must be separable and genuinely optional
  - Tying consent to unrelated marketing violates this principle

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Collecting SIN for employment | Express consent with explanation of purpose | SIN is highly sensitive; implied consent insufficient |
| Retailer wants to send promotional emails | Opt-out mechanism with clear notice at point of sale | Marketing is predictable use; opt-out is appropriate |
| Health app wants to share data with advertisers | Express consent with separate toggle; cannot be bundled | Sharing with third parties for advertising is unexpected |
| Bank using credit score for loan decision | Implied consent acceptable if disclosed in application | Credit assessment is expected in lending context |
| Website changing privacy policy to add new uses | Notify users and obtain fresh consent for material changes | New purposes require new consent |
| Loyalty program wants to sell data to partners | Cannot proceed; this was not an original purpose | Selling data fundamentally different from operating loyalty program |
| Free service requiring marketing consent | Must offer alternative or make marketing truly optional | Cannot tie unrelated consent to service access |
| Employee monitoring program | Express consent with detailed notice before implementation | Monitoring is intrusive; sensitivity demands express consent |

## Gotchas

- **Scroll-through consent is problematic**: Requiring individuals to scroll
  through lengthy terms before clicking "I agree" does not constitute meaningful
  consent if key terms are not highlighted or summarized.

- **Pre-checked boxes do not constitute express consent**: For express consent,
  the individual must take an affirmative action. Pre-selected options that
  require opting out are only valid where implied consent is appropriate.

- **Consent obtained through deception is void**: Misleading statements about
  purposes or uses invalidate consent entirely. Misrepresenting data practices
  to obtain consent is a PIPEDA violation.

- **Bundled consent may be invalid**: Requiring consent to multiple unrelated
  purposes as a single package undermines the ability to provide meaningful
  consent to each purpose.

- **Silence does not equal consent for sensitive uses**: While implied consent
  may be inferred from inaction in some circumstances, silence cannot establish
  consent for sensitive information or unexpected purposes.

- **Withdrawal must be easy**: If obtaining consent is simple but withdrawal
  requires excessive steps (phone calls, forms, waiting periods), the consent
  mechanism may not be valid.

- **Reasonable expectations matter**: Even with technical consent, if a
  reasonable person would not expect the use, the reasonableness standard under
  PIPEDA section 5(3) may invalidate it.

- **Third-party sharing requires specific consent**: General consent to an
  organization's practices does not extend to sharing with unnamed third
  parties for their own purposes.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Consent timing | At or before collection | Retroactive consent problematic |
| Sensitive information | Express consent required | Health, financial, biometric, children's data |
| Marketing communications | Opt-out permitted | Must be clear and accessible |
| New purposes | Fresh consent required | Before new use begins |
| Withdrawal timeline | Without unreasonable delay | No legislated maximum, but must be prompt |
| Condition of service | Limited to necessary purposes | Cannot require consent to unrelated uses |
| Standard for validity | Reasonable person test | PIPEDA section 5(3) |

## Related topics

- **Identifying Purposes (Principle 2)**: Meaningful consent depends on clear
  purpose identification; cannot consent to unknown uses
- **Limiting Collection (Principle 4)**: Collection must be limited to what is
  necessary for consented purposes
- **Limiting Use and Disclosure (Principle 5)**: Use and disclosure constrained
  by scope of consent obtained
- **OPC Guidelines for Obtaining Meaningful Consent**: Detailed guidance on
  consent practices, including seven guiding principles for valid consent
- **CASL consent requirements**: Canada's Anti-Spam Legislation has specific
  express and implied consent rules for commercial electronic messages
- **Provincial variations**: Quebec Law 25, Alberta PIPA, and BC PIPA have
  consent requirements that differ from PIPEDA in specific ways
- **Automated decision-making**: Emerging requirements for consent and
  transparency around profiling and algorithmic decisions

### 2.9: Recognize when consent requirements may have exceptions

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 2.9: Recognize when consent requirements may have exceptions

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Source limitation

The IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide is an introductory document that provides exam
structure and preparation guidance. It does not contain substantive legal
content on consent exceptions under PIPEDA or provincial private sector laws.

The guide explicitly notes: "Substantive legal content requires the official
textbook" (Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the
Practitioner).

## What the source covers

The free study guide identifies that Domain II (Private-Sector Laws and
Practices) includes:

- Privacy principles foundational to PIPEDA and provincial private sector laws
- When private-sector legislation applies instead of PIPEDA
- Canada's Anti-Spam Legislation (CASL)

It confirms that PIPEDA "applies to any Canadian organization that collects,
uses or discloses personal information for commercial purposes" but does not
elaborate on consent exceptions.

## Key concepts (from domain context)

Based on the body of knowledge outline, this objective falls within:

- **Domain II: Canadian Privacy Laws and Practices - Private Sector**
- **Weight**: 19% of exam content
- **Focus area**: PIPEDA principles and provincial private sector laws

The body of knowledge indicates candidates must "know the privacy principles
that are the foundation of PIPEDA and provincial private sector laws," which
includes understanding when consent is required and when exceptions apply.

## Recommended supplementary sources

For substantive content on consent exceptions, consult:

- IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge extract for objective 2.9
- "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the Practitioner"
  (official CIPP/C textbook)
- PIPEDA sections 7(1), 7(2), and 7(3) directly
- OPC guidance documents on consent exceptions

## Related topics

- **PIPEDA Principle 3 - Consent**: The default requiring knowledge and consent
- **Provincial private sector laws**: Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, and Quebec Law 25
  have their own consent exception frameworks
- **CASL**: Related but distinct consent requirements for commercial electronic
  messages

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.9: Recognize when consent requirements may have exceptions

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Consent as the default rule**: PIPEDA Principle 3 requires knowledge and
  consent for the collection, use, or disclosure of personal information.
  However, the Act recognizes situations where obtaining consent is
  impractical, inappropriate, or would undermine legitimate purposes.
  - Exceptions are enumerated in sections 7(1), 7(2), and 7(3) of PIPEDA
  - Exceptions exist separately for collection, use, and disclosure
  - Organizations must document which exception applies when relying on one

- **Collection without consent (s. 7(1))**: Personal information may be
  collected without consent in limited circumstances:
  - Collection is clearly in the interests of the individual and consent
    cannot be obtained in a timely way
  - Collection is necessary for investigating a breach of agreement or law
  - Collection is for journalistic, artistic, or literary purposes
  - Information is publicly available as specified in regulations
  - Collection is required by law

- **Use without consent (s. 7(2))**: Personal information may be used without
  consent when:
  - Used for purposes for which it was collected (and individual would
    reasonably expect such use)
  - Used for detecting and preventing fraud
  - Used for statistical or scholarly study or research (where consent is
    impractical and measures protect identity)
  - Used for an emergency threatening life, health, or security
  - Required by law

- **Disclosure without consent (s. 7(3))**: Personal information may be
  disclosed without consent in circumstances including:
  - Disclosure to a lawyer representing the organization
  - Disclosure to collect a debt
  - Disclosure required by law or court order
  - Disclosure to a government institution for law enforcement, national
    security, or administration of laws
  - Disclosure to comply with a subpoena or warrant
  - Disclosure for an emergency threatening life, health, or security
  - Disclosure for statistical or scholarly research (with safeguards)
  - Disclosure 20 years after death or 100 years after record creation for
    archival purposes

- **Business transaction exception**: PIPEDA allows disclosure and use of
  personal information without consent for prospective business transactions
  (mergers, acquisitions, asset sales) subject to conditions:
  - Information is necessary to determine whether to proceed
  - Confidentiality agreements are in place
  - Information is used only for transaction-related purposes
  - If transaction does not proceed, information must be returned or destroyed

- **Employment exception considerations**: While PIPEDA covers employee
  information for federally regulated works, undertakings, and businesses,
  the employment context may affect consent requirements:
  - Reasonable collection for employment relationship establishment
  - Investigation of workplace misconduct may qualify under breach exception
  - Provincial private sector laws may apply to provincially regulated
    employers (AB, BC, QC)

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Responding to a court order for customer records | Disclose without consent under s. 7(3) legal requirement exception | Law compels disclosure; consent not required |
| Investigating suspected employee fraud | Use existing records without additional consent under s. 7(2) investigation exception | Investigation of breach of agreement; consent could frustrate investigation |
| Collecting credit card information from a person in medical distress | Collect without consent under s. 7(1) individual interest exception | Collection clearly benefits individual; consent cannot be timely obtained |
| Sharing records with acquiring company during merger negotiations | Disclose under business transaction exception with confidentiality agreement | Transaction exception permits disclosure with safeguards |
| Police request for customer information (no warrant) | Evaluate whether s. 7(3) disclosure exception applies; may but not required to disclose | Exception permits but does not compel voluntary disclosure |
| Using customer data for internal fraud analytics | Use without consent under s. 7(2) fraud detection exception | Detecting and preventing fraud is an enumerated exception |
| Newspaper researching story involving business records | May collect and use under journalistic exception | Journalistic purposes are exempt from consent requirements |

## Gotchas

- **Exceptions permit but may not require disclosure**: Many disclosure
  exceptions are permissive ("may disclose") not mandatory. Organizations
  retain discretion to refuse voluntary disclosure even when an exception
  applies, except where legally compelled.

- **Police requests without warrant**: Law enforcement requests for personal
  information without a warrant may fall under s. 7(3) disclosure exceptions,
  but organizations are permitted, not required, to comply. Careful
  documentation of the request and rationale for response is essential.

- **Business transaction confidentiality is mandatory**: Relying on the
  business transaction exception requires confidentiality agreements limiting
  use to transaction purposes. Failure to obtain such agreements means the
  exception does not apply.

- **Legitimate business interests is not an exception**: Unlike some
  jurisdictions, PIPEDA does not include a broad "legitimate interests" basis
  for processing. Canadian organizations cannot rely on general business
  necessity as a consent exception.

- **Publicly available information has specific limits**: The regulations
  specify what qualifies as publicly available (telephone directories,
  professional registries, public records, publications). Not all information
  that appears public qualifies.

- **Statistical research requires safeguards**: The research exception for
  use and disclosure requires that obtaining consent would be impractical,
  the research cannot be done without the information, confidentiality is
  ensured, and consent is obtained if practicable before publication.

- **Emergency exception is narrow**: Threatening life, health, or security
  requires genuine urgency. Routine healthcare or security operations do not
  qualify as emergencies under this exception.

- **Documentation burden shifts to organization**: When relying on any
  exception, the organization bears the burden of demonstrating the exception
  applies. Document the specific exception, the circumstances, and the
  decision-making process.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| PIPEDA section for collection exceptions | s. 7(1) | Lists circumstances for collection without consent |
| PIPEDA section for use exceptions | s. 7(2) | Lists circumstances for use without consent |
| PIPEDA section for disclosure exceptions | s. 7(3) | Lists circumstances for disclosure without consent |
| Default rule | Consent required | Exceptions are departures from the norm |
| Publicly available information regulation | SOR/2001-7 | Specifies what qualifies as publicly available |
| Archival disclosure threshold | 20 years after death or 100 years after creation | Whichever comes first |
| Business transaction requirement | Confidentiality agreement | Must limit use to transaction purposes |
| Research exception requirement | Consent impractical + identity protection | Both conditions must be met |

## Related topics

- **Principle 3 - Consent**: The foundational PIPEDA principle requiring
  knowledge and consent, from which exceptions depart
- **Forms of consent**: Understanding when express vs. implied consent is
  appropriate helps distinguish situations where consent is required from
  exceptions
- **Provincial private sector laws**: Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, and Quebec Law
  25 have their own consent exception frameworks that may differ from PIPEDA
- **Public sector consent exceptions**: The Privacy Act (s. 8) has its own
  disclosure exception framework for government institutions
- **Health sector consent exceptions**: Provincial health privacy acts have
  distinct exceptions for PHI, including circle of care provisions
- **Breach notification**: Some disclosure exceptions relate to breach
  investigations and reporting requirements
- **Law enforcement cooperation**: Understanding the interplay between
  voluntary disclosure permissions and legal compulsion

### 2.10: Adhere to rules for collection, use, disclosure, retention, and deletion

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 2.10: Adhere to rules for collection, use, disclosure, retention, and deletion

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Source limitation

The IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide is an introductory preparation document that
does not contain substantive content on this objective. The guide covers exam
format, preparation recommendations, and a high-level body of knowledge outline
only.

The relevant content in this source is limited to:

- The body of knowledge outline mentions "privacy principles that are the
  foundation of PIPEDA and provincial private sector laws" without elaborating
  on specific collection, use, disclosure, retention, or deletion rules.
- A sample question references that "PIPEDA applies to any Canadian organization
  that collects, uses or discloses personal information for commercial
  purposes."

For comprehensive coverage of collection limitation, use and disclosure
restrictions, retention requirements, and deletion obligations under PIPEDA and
provincial private-sector laws, refer to the authoritative textbook extract in
`books/iapp-cippc-bok/objectives/iapp-cippc-2.10.md`.

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.10: Adhere to rules for collection, use, disclosure, retention, and deletion

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Limiting collection (Principle 4)**: Organizations shall limit collection of
  personal information to what is necessary for identified purposes.
  - Collection must be limited to what is required for the stated purposes
  - Cannot collect information "just in case" it might be useful later
  - Must use fair and lawful means of collection
  - Shall not collect by deception or misleading individuals

- **Limiting use, disclosure, and retention (Principle 5)**: Personal information
  shall not be used or disclosed for purposes other than those for which it was
  collected, except with consent or as required by law.
  - Use and disclosure limited to original stated purposes
  - New purposes require new consent before proceeding
  - Retention only for as long as necessary to fulfill the purpose
  - Must establish retention schedules and deletion procedures

- **Consent exceptions**: PIPEDA permits collection, use, or disclosure without
  consent in specific circumstances.
  - Required by law (subpoena, court order, statutory obligation)
  - Emergency threatening life, health, or security
  - Debt collection activities
  - Journalistic, artistic, or literary purposes
  - Publicly available information as defined in regulations
  - Fraud investigation or prevention

- **Purpose limitation doctrine**: The stated purposes at collection define the
  boundaries of permissible use.
  - Purposes must be identified before or at time of collection
  - Cannot retroactively expand purposes without new consent
  - Reasonable person standard: would individual expect this use?
  - Related purposes may be permitted if reasonably expected

- **Retention requirements**: Organizations must establish schedules for how long
  personal information is kept.
  - Retain only as long as necessary for identified purposes
  - Consider legal requirements (tax records, employment records)
  - Document retention periods in policies
  - Destroy or anonymize when no longer needed

- **Deletion and destruction**: Personal information must be disposed of securely
  when retention period ends.
  - Secure destruction to prevent unauthorized access
  - Physical records: shredding, incineration
  - Electronic records: secure erasure, degaussing, destruction
  - Extends to copies held by third parties

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Customer provides email for purchase receipt | Use only for receipt delivery; do not add to marketing list without consent | Use must align with purpose stated at collection |
| Marketing wants to use transaction data for profiling | Obtain new consent before using data for analytics not disclosed at collection | New purpose requires new consent |
| Legal hold issued during litigation | Suspend normal deletion; retain relevant records until hold lifted | Legal requirements override standard retention |
| Individual asks why information is still held | Must be able to explain retention based on legitimate ongoing purpose | Retention requires ongoing justification |
| Employee leaves the organization | Retain employment records per legal requirements; delete discretionary data | Balance legal retention with minimization |
| Third party requests customer list for marketing | Refuse unless customers consented to disclosure for that purpose | Disclosure limited to original purposes |
| Old backup tapes contain personal information | Include in destruction schedule; securely destroy when retention period ends | Deletion obligations extend to all copies |

## Gotchas

- **Collection creep**: Organizations often expand data collection over time
  without reassessing necessity. Each new field requires justification tied to
  a specific, identified purpose.

- **Implied consent has limits**: While implied consent may permit certain uses,
  it does not authorize uses a reasonable person would not expect. Sensitive
  information or unexpected uses require express consent.

- **Retention is not indefinite**: "We might need it someday" is not a valid
  retention justification. Keeping information beyond its useful life increases
  risk and violates the limiting principle.

- **Exceptions are narrow**: Consent exceptions exist for specific situations.
  Organizations cannot broadly invoke exceptions to avoid obtaining proper
  consent. Each exception has defined criteria.

- **Related purposes are limited**: While PIPEDA allows use for purposes a
  reasonable person would consider appropriate, this is not a blanket
  authorization. The connection must be logical and expected.

- **Deletion must be verifiable**: Simply marking records for deletion is
  insufficient. Organizations must confirm information is actually destroyed
  across all systems, backups, and third-party processors.

- **Third parties inherit restrictions**: When sharing information with third
  parties, the original purpose limitations follow. Third parties cannot use
  data for their own purposes without separate consent.

- **Legal holds override schedules**: When litigation is anticipated or
  underway, normal deletion must stop. Destroying evidence after a legal hold
  creates serious liability.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| PIPEDA principle for limiting collection | Principle 4 | Schedule 1, Section 4.4 |
| PIPEDA principle for limiting use/disclosure/retention | Principle 5 | Schedule 1, Section 4.5 |
| Default position on consent | Required | Exceptions must be specifically justified |
| Maximum retention period | Not specified | As long as necessary for purpose |
| Retention schedule required | Yes (implied) | Must have documented retention periods |
| Secure destruction required | Yes | Prevent unauthorized access to disposed data |
| Third-party deletion required | Yes | Must ensure processors also delete |

## Related topics

- **Identifying purposes (Principle 2)**: Purposes must be identified before
  collection; collection rules depend on clear purpose statements
- **Consent (Principle 3)**: Consent scope defines permissible uses; new uses
  require new consent
- **Accuracy (Principle 6)**: Information kept for ongoing use must remain
  accurate for the purpose
- **Safeguards (Principle 7)**: Protection requirements continue until secure
  destruction occurs
- **Openness (Principle 8)**: Policies should explain retention practices and
  purpose limitations
- **Third-party accountability**: Retention and deletion obligations extend to
  processors handling information on behalf of the organization
- **Breach notification**: Retaining unnecessary data increases breach impact;
  minimization reduces risk exposure

### 2.11: Maintain openness in policies concerning personal information collection

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 2.11: Maintain openness in policies concerning personal information collection

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Key concepts

- **Openness principle**: The study guide identifies "private-sector privacy laws
  such as PIPEDA" as a key subject matter area. PIPEDA Schedule 1 includes the
  openness principle (Principle 4.8), which requires organizations to make their
  privacy policies and practices readily available to individuals.
  - Transparency in how personal information is collected, used, and disclosed
  - Policies must be understandable and accessible
  - Links to CSA Model Code and GAPP frameworks for privacy compliance

- **CSA Model Code relevance**: The study guide lists "Canadian model codes for
  privacy compliance such as the CSA and GAPP frameworks" as key knowledge
  areas. The CSA Model Code includes openness as a core principle that was
  incorporated into PIPEDA.
  - CSA Principle 8 covers openness requirements
  - Organizations must be open about policies concerning personal information
  - Individuals have a right to know about organizational privacy practices

- **Body of knowledge coverage**: The CIPP/C body of knowledge outline specifies
  that candidates must "Know the privacy principles that are the foundation of
  the Personal Information Protection and Electronic Documents Act (PIPEDA)."
  Openness is one of these foundational principles.

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Preparing for CIPP/C exam | Study PIPEDA Schedule 1 principles | Openness is a testable principle |
| Understanding CSA Model Code | Review all ten principles including openness | Foundation of Canadian privacy law |
| Reviewing provincial laws | Check BC PIPA, Alberta PIPA, Quebec Law 25 | Each has openness/transparency requirements |

## Gotchas

- **Exam scope note**: The free study guide provides only high-level topics. The
  official textbook "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the
  Practitioner" contains detailed content on the openness principle.

- **Provincial variations exist**: The study guide identifies BC PIPA, Alberta
  PIPA, and Quebec's private sector law as key areas. Each has distinct
  transparency and openness requirements that may appear on the exam.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| PIPEDA principle number | 8 | Schedule 1, Principle 4.8 |
| Exam domain | II. Canadian Privacy Laws and Practices - Private-Sector | 19% of exam |
| Primary textbook | Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy | Required for detailed study |

## Related topics

- **PIPEDA principles**: Openness is principle 8 of 10 in Schedule 1
- **Provincial private sector laws**: BC PIPA, Alberta PIPA, Quebec Law 25 each
  contain openness or transparency requirements
- **CSA Model Code**: Source of the ten privacy principles adopted by PIPEDA
- **GAPP framework**: Alternative privacy framework also covering transparency

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.11: Maintain openness in policies concerning personal information collection

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Openness Principle (Principle 8)**: PIPEDA Schedule 1 requires organizations to
  make information about their privacy policies and practices readily available to
  individuals. This principle ensures transparency in how personal information is
  handled.
  - Policies must be understandable by the average person
  - Information must be accessible without unreasonable effort
  - Cannot use legal jargon or technical language that obscures meaning
  - Applies to all stages of the information lifecycle

- **Required disclosure elements**: Organizations must make the following
  information available to individuals:
  - Identity and contact information of the person accountable for privacy
    compliance (typically the Privacy Officer or Chief Privacy Officer)
  - How to access personal information held by the organization
  - How to challenge compliance with privacy principles
  - Types of personal information collected and purposes for collection
  - Description of how personal information is used
  - Parties to whom personal information may be disclosed

- **Point of collection requirements**: Privacy information must be available at
  the time personal information is collected, not just afterward.
  - Notice must precede or accompany collection
  - Must inform individuals of purposes before or at time of collection
  - Collection forms should include privacy notices or links to full policies
  - Verbal collection (call centers) requires scripted privacy statements

- **Accessibility standards**: Policies must be "readily available," which means:
  - Posted prominently on websites (typically footer link to privacy policy)
  - Available in physical locations where information is collected
  - Provided upon request without charge
  - Available in formats accessible to persons with disabilities when required
  - Translated into appropriate languages for the customer base served

- **Plain language requirement**: The openness principle requires policies to be
  understandable, not merely available.
  - Avoid legal jargon and technical terms
  - Use clear, simple sentences
  - Organize information logically with headings
  - Layered notices (short summary with link to full policy) are acceptable
  - Privacy Commissioner guidance emphasizes readability

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Launching new website | Post privacy policy with prominent link | Openness requires readily available policies |
| Customer asks who handles complaints | Provide name/contact of privacy officer | Must identify accountable individual |
| Using complex legal terms in policy | Rewrite in plain language | Policies must be understandable |
| Collecting data at trade show | Provide privacy notice at booth | Point of collection disclosure required |
| Policy buried in terms of service | Create standalone privacy policy link | Must be readily accessible, not hidden |
| Customer cannot find privacy info | Review and improve accessibility | Failure of openness obligation |
| Updating privacy practices | Update policy and notify customers | Ongoing openness obligation |

## Gotchas

- **"Readily available" is active, not passive**: Organizations cannot satisfy
  openness by having a policy exist somewhere - it must be easy to find. A policy
  buried in fine print or requiring multiple clicks fails the standard.

- **Layered notices are acceptable**: A short summary with a link to the full
  policy satisfies openness requirements. You do not need to present the entire
  policy at every collection point.

- **Openness is not consent**: Making a policy available does not equal obtaining
  consent. Openness informs; consent authorizes. Both are required but serve
  different functions.

- **Updates require communication**: When policies change, organizations should
  notify individuals of material changes. Posting a new policy without notification
  may not satisfy openness for existing customers.

- **Internal policies count**: Openness applies to policies about information
  handling, not just public-facing privacy statements. Employees and third-party
  processors should understand the organization's privacy practices.

- **Policies must be current**: Outdated policies that do not reflect actual
  practices violate openness. Regular policy reviews are necessary.

- **Provincial variations**: Quebec Law 25 has additional transparency requirements
  including publishing specific information about automated decision-making and
  biometric data. BC and Alberta PIPA also require transparency but with slightly
  different formulations.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Principle number in PIPEDA | 8 (Openness) | Schedule 1, Principle 4.8 |
| Policy must identify | Accountable person | Name or title and contact information |
| Format requirement | Readily available | Generally understood as website posting |
| Language requirement | Understandable | Plain language, not legal jargon |
| Point of collection | At or before | Cannot delay disclosure until after |
| Fee for policy access | None | Must be available without charge |

## Related topics

- **Accountability (Principle 1)**: Openness requires identifying the accountable
  individual, linking these two principles
- **Consent (Principle 3)**: Informed consent depends on openness - individuals
  cannot consent meaningfully without understanding practices
- **Individual Access (Principle 9)**: Openness requires explaining how individuals
  can exercise access rights
- **Challenging Compliance (Principle 10)**: Openness requires explaining complaint
  procedures
- **Privacy Impact Assessments**: PIAs often identify openness gaps in new programs
- **Quebec Law 25**: Enhanced transparency requirements including AI and biometric
  disclosures
- **OPC Guidance**: Privacy Commissioner has issued guidance on effective privacy
  policies and plain language requirements

### 2.12: Respond to individuals seeking access to personal information

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.12: Respond to individuals seeking access to personal information

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Individual Access Principle (Principle 9)**: PIPEDA Schedule 1 establishes that
  individuals have the right to access personal information held about them by an
  organization. This includes being informed of the existence, use, and disclosure
  of their personal information, and being given access to that information upon
  request.
  - The right is not absolute; specific exemptions apply
  - Applies to personal information in any form (electronic, paper, recordings)
  - Includes the right to challenge accuracy and completeness

- **Identity verification**: Organizations must verify the identity of individuals
  making access requests before disclosing personal information.
  - Verification methods must be reasonable and proportionate
  - Cannot require verification beyond what is necessary
  - Common methods include government-issued ID, security questions, or account
    credentials
  - Must balance security against accessibility of the right
  - Over-verification can itself be a barrier to access rights

- **Response timeline under PIPEDA**: Organizations must respond to access
  requests within 30 days of receiving the request.
  - The clock starts when the request is received, not acknowledged
  - Extensions are permitted in limited circumstances (e.g., meeting deadline
    would unreasonably interfere with operations, or consultation is required)
  - Must notify the requester of any extension and their right to complain to
    the Privacy Commissioner

- **Information that must be provided**: Upon a valid access request,
  organizations must disclose:
  - The personal information held about the individual
  - How that information has been used
  - Third parties to whom the information has been disclosed
  - Sufficient detail for the individual to understand and challenge accuracy

- **Manner of access**: Organizations must provide personal information in a form
  that is generally understandable.
  - Must explain abbreviations, codes, and technical terms
  - Provide access in the format requested if reasonably possible
  - May provide a copy rather than allowing direct examination
  - Fee must be minimal and not a barrier to access

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Customer requests all data held about them | Verify identity, compile records, respond within 30 days | PIPEDA Principle 9 access right |
| Unable to locate all records in 30 days | Notify requester of extension with reason | Extension permitted but must notify |
| Request includes third-party information | Redact third-party personal information before disclosure | Cannot disclose others' personal information |
| Requester cannot verify identity | Offer alternative verification methods | Must balance access against security |
| Request is vague or unclear | Seek clarification while preserving timeline | Cannot use ambiguity to delay indefinitely |
| Information contains technical codes | Provide legend or explanation with response | Must be generally understandable |
| Large volume of information requested | May charge reasonable fee for reproduction | Fee cannot be prohibitive |
| Requester disputes accuracy after access | Provide correction or annotate records | Access linked to correction rights |

## Gotchas

- **30 days is the maximum, not the standard**: Organizations should aim to
  respond faster when possible. Routinely taking the full 30 days may attract
  Commissioner scrutiny.

- **Acknowledging receipt is not responding**: Sending an acknowledgment does not
  satisfy the response obligation. The substantive response with information or a
  denial must occur within the timeline.

- **Cannot charge for access itself**: While organizations may charge a minimal
  fee for reproduction costs, they cannot charge for the act of searching for or
  compiling the information. Fees must not discourage access.

- **Partial disclosure is acceptable**: If some information is exempt, disclose
  what can be disclosed and explain what is withheld and why. Do not refuse the
  entire request because part is exempt.

- **Denials require reasons**: If access is denied in whole or in part, the
  organization must explain why and inform the individual of their right to
  complain to the Privacy Commissioner.

- **Third-party information vs. information about third parties**: Information
  about an individual's transactions with third parties (e.g., purchase history)
  is the requester's personal information. Information that would reveal another
  person's personal information must be withheld or redacted.

- **Provincial variations matter**: Quebec Law 25 requires a response within 30
  days but permits a 10-day extension. BC PIPA also uses 30 days. Alberta PIPA
  allows 45 days. Know which timeline applies.

- **Employee vs. customer access**: PIPEDA applies to employee information only
  for federally regulated employers. For provincially regulated employers, the
  applicable provincial legislation governs access rights.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| PIPEDA response timeline | 30 days | From receipt of request |
| Privacy Act timeline | 30 days | For public sector access requests |
| Alberta PIPA timeline | 45 days | Longer than federal standard |
| Quebec Law 25 timeline | 30 days + 10 days extension | Extension available |
| BC PIPA timeline | 30 days | May extend to 60 days |
| Principle number in PIPEDA | 9 (Individual Access) | Schedule 1, Principle 4.9 |
| Fee for access | Minimal or none | Cannot be prohibitive |
| Information format | Generally understandable | Explain codes and abbreviations |
| Identity verification | Reasonable and proportionate | Not overly burdensome |

## Related topics

- **Accuracy (Principle 6)**: Access enables individuals to challenge and correct
  inaccurate information
- **Openness (Principle 8)**: Organizations must explain how to exercise access
  rights in their privacy policies
- **Challenging Compliance (Principle 10)**: Individuals may complain to the
  Privacy Commissioner if access is denied
- **Consent exceptions**: Some exceptions to consent may also limit access (e.g.,
  solicitor-client privilege)
- **Breach notification**: Individuals may request access after receiving breach
  notification to understand the scope of exposure
- **Privacy Impact Assessments**: PIAs should consider how access requests will
  be handled for new data collections
- **Provincial private sector laws**: Access timelines and procedures vary between
  PIPEDA, Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, and Quebec Law 25

### 2.13: Understand identity verification protocols for access requests

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.13: Understand identity verification protocols for access requests

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Purpose of identity verification**: Organizations must confirm the identity of
  individuals making access requests before disclosing personal information. This
  prevents unauthorized disclosure to impostors while preserving the individual's
  right of access under PIPEDA Principle 9.
  - Primary goal is preventing disclosure to wrong person
  - Secondary goal is maintaining audit trail of who accessed what
  - Must not create barriers that effectively deny access rights

- **Proportionality principle**: Verification requirements must be proportionate
  to the sensitivity of the information and the risk of harm from unauthorized
  disclosure.
  - Higher sensitivity (financial, health, SIN) warrants stronger verification
  - Lower sensitivity (contact preferences) permits simpler verification
  - Risk assessment should consider both likelihood and severity of harm
  - Organizations cannot apply maximum verification to all requests

- **Reasonable verification methods**: Organizations have discretion in choosing
  verification methods, but they must be reasonable in the circumstances.
  - Account credentials (username/password) for existing customers
  - Knowledge-based authentication (security questions, transaction history)
  - Government-issued identification (driver's license, passport)
  - Biometric verification where already collected for service delivery
  - Multi-factor authentication combining methods

- **Barriers to access**: Overly burdensome verification requirements can
  themselves constitute a violation of access rights.
  - Requiring in-person attendance when not necessary
  - Demanding original documents when copies suffice
  - Asking for information the organization does not already hold
  - Creating verification processes that exceed the organization's own
    security practices for service delivery
  - Imposing costs on verification that deter legitimate requests

- **Authorized representatives**: Individuals may authorize another person to
  make access requests on their behalf.
  - Written authorization from the individual is typically required
  - Power of attorney or legal guardianship for incapacity situations
  - Parent or guardian for minor children
  - Organization must verify both the representative's identity and authority
  - Cannot unreasonably refuse representative requests

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Existing customer with active account | Verify via account login credentials | Already authenticated for service |
| Former customer without active account | Request ID document or security questions | Must establish identity without account |
| Request by email without prior relationship | Request photo ID and verify email ownership | Higher verification for unknown requesters |
| Requester cannot provide standard ID | Offer alternatives (attestation, statutory declaration) | Must accommodate accessibility |
| Lawyer requests on behalf of client | Verify lawyer identity and obtain client authorization | Confirm both identity and authority |
| Parent requests child's information | Verify parental relationship and child's age | Authority depends on minor status |
| Request for highly sensitive information | Apply multi-factor verification | Risk-proportionate approach |
| Request for basic contact preferences | Simple verification via email confirmation | Low sensitivity permits simpler process |

## Gotchas

- **Cannot require SIN for verification**: Organizations should not use Social
  Insurance Numbers as verification credentials unless the SIN was legitimately
  collected for a statutory purpose (taxation, employment records). Using SIN as
  identity proof creates security risks.

- **Verification requirements cannot exceed service standards**: If an
  organization accepts simple credentials for financial transactions, it cannot
  demand more rigorous verification for access requests. The access process
  should not be harder than the service itself.

- **Failure to verify is not consent to deny**: If an individual cannot meet
  verification requirements, the organization should offer reasonable
  alternatives rather than simply denying access. Blanket denials for
  verification failure may attract Commissioner scrutiny.

- **Third-party verification services**: Organizations may use third-party
  identity verification services, but they remain responsible for ensuring the
  process is proportionate and does not create barriers. The organization cannot
  outsource its privacy obligations.

- **Documentation of verification**: Organizations should maintain records of how
  identity was verified for each access request. This creates an audit trail and
  demonstrates compliance with both access and security obligations.

- **Provincial variations in authorized representatives**: Quebec Law 25
  specifically addresses access by mandataries and legal representatives.
  Alberta PIPA includes provisions for access on behalf of deceased individuals.
  Know which provincial rules apply.

- **Time spent on verification counts against deadline**: The 30-day response
  timeline runs from receipt of the request, not from completion of
  verification. Lengthy verification processes can consume the response window.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| PIPEDA response deadline | 30 days | Verification must occur within this window |
| Minimum verification for low-risk requests | Single factor | Email confirmation, account login |
| Verification for sensitive information | Multi-factor recommended | Proportionate to risk |
| SIN as verification credential | Not recommended | Creates security risk, not intended purpose |
| Documentation retention | Retain verification records | Part of access request file |
| Representative authorization | Written preferred | Verbal may suffice in some circumstances |
| Alternative verification | Must offer | When standard methods inaccessible |

## Related topics

- **Individual access (Principle 9)**: Verification is the gateway to exercising
  access rights; excessive verification defeats the right
- **Safeguards (Principle 7)**: Verification is itself a safeguard preventing
  unauthorized disclosure
- **Limiting collection (Principle 4)**: Cannot collect additional personal
  information beyond what is needed for verification
- **Accountability (Principle 1)**: Organization must designate who handles
  verification and ensure consistent application
- **Provincial private sector laws**: Verification requirements may differ under
  Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, and Quebec Law 25
- **Health sector access**: Provincial health privacy laws often have specific
  verification requirements for access to personal health information
- **Breach response**: Post-breach access requests may require enhanced
  verification if authentication credentials were compromised

### 2.14: Safeguard personal information (standards, controls, PETs, cybersecurity)

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.14: Safeguard personal information (standards, controls, PETs, cybersecurity)

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Safeguards Principle (PIPEDA Principle 7)**: Organizations must protect
  personal information with security safeguards appropriate to the sensitivity
  of the information.
  - Derived from CSA Model Code Principle 4.7
  - Safeguards must protect against loss, theft, unauthorized access,
    disclosure, copying, use, or modification
  - Level of protection must correspond to sensitivity of information
  - Applies throughout the information lifecycle

- **Categories of safeguards**: PIPEDA requires organizations to implement
  safeguards across three categories.
  - Administrative safeguards: policies, procedures, training, access
    management, incident response
  - Technical safeguards: encryption, access controls, logging, network
    security, intrusion detection
  - Physical safeguards: secure storage, facility access controls, disposal
    procedures, device security

- **Proportionality requirement**: Safeguards must be proportionate to the
  sensitivity of the information and the harm that could result from a breach.
  - Highly sensitive information (health, financial, biometric) requires
    stronger controls
  - Volume of data affects risk level and required safeguards
  - Context matters: employee data may require different controls than customer
    data
  - Risk assessment informs safeguard selection

- **Privacy-Enhancing Technologies (PETs)**: Technical measures that minimize
  personal information processing while enabling functionality.
  - Data minimization tools: collect only what is necessary
  - Pseudonymization: replace identifying information with artificial
    identifiers
  - Anonymization: render data non-identifiable (irreversible)
  - Encryption: protect data at rest and in transit
  - Access controls: limit data access to authorized personnel
  - Differential privacy: add noise to datasets to prevent re-identification

- **Third-party accountability**: Organizations remain accountable for personal
  information handled by third parties and must ensure comparable safeguards.
  - Contractual provisions must require adequate protection
  - Due diligence required when selecting service providers
  - Ongoing monitoring of third-party compliance
  - Data processing agreements must address security requirements
  - Breach notification obligations extend to third-party incidents

- **Information lifecycle protection**: Safeguards apply from collection through
  disposal.
  - Collection: secure channels, encrypted transmission
  - Storage: access controls, encryption, physical security
  - Use: role-based access, audit logging
  - Transfer: secure transmission, recipient verification
  - Retention: time-limited storage, purpose fulfillment
  - Disposal: secure destruction, media sanitization

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Storing customer credit card data | Encrypt at rest and in transit; comply with PCI DSS; limit access to payment staff | Financial data is highly sensitive; industry standards apply |
| Employee working remotely | Require VPN; encrypt device storage; implement MFA; establish clear use policies | Extends physical perimeter; maintains equivalent protection |
| Sharing data with cloud provider | Execute data processing agreement; verify provider certifications; specify security requirements | Accountability remains with organization |
| Disposing of old computers | Secure wipe or physical destruction; maintain disposal records; verify completion | Prevents data recovery from discarded media |
| Collecting data via website | Use HTTPS/TLS; implement input validation; secure session management | Protects data in transit; prevents unauthorized access |
| Health information at clinic | Implement access controls by role; encrypt electronic records; secure physical files | PHI requires enhanced protection |
| Transferring data internationally | Ensure receiving jurisdiction provides adequate protection; use contractual safeguards | Safeguards must follow data across borders |
| Detecting a security incident | Activate incident response plan; contain breach; assess scope; notify as required | Timely response limits harm |

## Gotchas

- **Sensitivity is contextual, not categorical**: The same type of information
  may require different safeguards depending on context. A phone number may be
  low-sensitivity for a business contact but high-sensitivity for a domestic
  violence survivor.

- **Anonymization may not be permanent**: Data once considered anonymized may
  become re-identifiable as technology advances or when combined with other
  datasets. Organizations should reassess anonymization effectiveness
  periodically.

- **Encryption is not sufficient alone**: Encryption protects against some
  threats but does not address all risks. Access controls, logging, and
  procedural safeguards remain necessary.

- **Employee training is a safeguard**: Human error causes many breaches.
  Training on security practices, phishing recognition, and data handling
  procedures is an essential administrative safeguard.

- **Consent does not reduce safeguard obligations**: Even when individuals
  consent to data collection, the organization must still implement appropriate
  safeguards. Consent addresses lawfulness, not security.

- **Safeguards must evolve with threats**: Security measures that were adequate
  five years ago may be insufficient today. Organizations must monitor the
  threat landscape and update controls accordingly.

- **Third-party breaches are your problem**: When a service provider suffers a
  breach affecting your data, you remain accountable to affected individuals.
  Contracts should address liability and notification obligations.

- **Physical security still matters**: Despite focus on cybersecurity, physical
  controls remain essential. Unlocked offices, visible screens, and unsecured
  printouts create vulnerabilities.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Standard for adequacy | Proportionate to sensitivity | Risk-based approach |
| Categories of safeguards | Administrative, technical, physical | All three required |
| Third-party requirements | Comparable protection | Contractually enforceable |
| Lifecycle coverage | Collection through disposal | No gaps permitted |
| Review frequency | Ongoing | Adapt to evolving threats |
| Breach notification (PIPEDA) | Real risk of significant harm | Mandatory since Nov 2018 |
| Record retention | As long as purpose requires | Secure disposal when complete |
| Access logging | Recommended practice | Supports accountability |

## Related topics

- **Accountability (Principle 1)**: Organization is responsible for all
  information under its control, including safeguarding; accountability extends
  to third parties
- **Limiting Collection (Principle 4)**: Collecting only necessary information
  reduces safeguarding burden; data minimization is both privacy and security
  measure
- **Retention (Principle 5)**: Secure disposal when purpose fulfilled;
  retention limits reduce risk exposure
- **Breach notification (2.15)**: Safeguard failures trigger notification
  obligations; incident response is part of safeguarding
- **Provincial variations**: Quebec Law 25 requires PIAs for high-risk
  processing; BC and Alberta PIPAs have similar safeguard requirements
- **Industry standards**: PCI DSS for payment data; SOC 2 for service
  providers; ISO 27001 for information security management
- **OPC guidance**: Office of the Privacy Commissioner publishes guidance on
  security safeguards and breach response

### 2.15: Understand data breach notification procedures and requirements

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 2.15: Understand data breach notification procedures and requirements

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide (2025)

> **Note**: This source is an introductory study guide that does not cover breach
> notification specifics. The content below synthesizes what can be inferred
> from the Body of Knowledge and related exam context. For comprehensive
> coverage, consult the official textbook.

## Key concepts

- **Breach of security safeguards**: Under PIPEDA, a breach occurs when personal
  information is lost, accessed without authorization, or disclosed without
  authorization in circumstances where a reasonable person would consider there
  is a real risk of significant harm to an individual.
- **Mandatory notification**: PIPEDA requires organizations to notify affected
  individuals and the Privacy Commissioner of Canada when a breach creates a
  real risk of significant harm.
- **Record-keeping requirement**: Organizations must maintain records of all
  breaches of security safeguards, regardless of whether notification was
  required.
- **Provincial variations**: Alberta, BC, and Quebec have their own breach
  notification requirements that may differ from PIPEDA in timing, thresholds,
  or content requirements.

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Breach with real risk of significant harm | Notify OPC and affected individuals as soon as feasible | PIPEDA mandatory notification threshold met |
| Breach with no real risk of significant harm | No notification required, but must record the breach | Record-keeping obligation applies to all breaches |
| Third party experiences breach of your data | Original organization fulfills notification duties | Accountability remains with the data controller |
| Province has substantially similar law | Follow provincial requirements where applicable | Provincial law may supersede PIPEDA |

## Gotchas

- **All breaches must be recorded**: Even when notification is not required,
  organizations must document every breach in a breach record.
- **"As soon as feasible" is not a fixed timeline**: Unlike some provincial
  laws, PIPEDA does not specify an exact number of days for notification.
- **Significant harm includes more than financial**: Harm can include bodily
  harm, humiliation, damage to reputation, or damage to relationships.
- **Third-party processors do not eliminate your obligations**: When a service
  provider causes a breach, the original organization retains notification
  responsibilities.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| PIPEDA notification timeline | "As soon as feasible" | No fixed day count |
| Record retention | Minimum 24 months | Must maintain breach records |
| Real risk threshold | Reasonable person standard | Whether harm is likely |
| Quebec Law 25 timeline | 72 hours | Provincial requirement differs from PIPEDA |

## Related topics

- **2.5 Accountability with third parties**: Breach notification remains with
  the organization even when third parties cause breaches
- **2.14 Safeguarding personal information**: Prevention measures reduce breach
  likelihood
- **1.7 Office of the Privacy Commissioner**: OPC receives breach reports and
  may investigate
- **1.8 Complaint and investigation process**: Breaches may trigger
  investigations
- **2.19 Provincial private sector laws**: Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, and Quebec
  Law 25 have distinct breach notification rules

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.15: Understand data breach notification procedures and requirements

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Breach of security safeguards (PIPEDA definition)**: The loss of,
  unauthorized access to, or unauthorized disclosure of personal information
  resulting from a breach of an organization's security safeguards or from a
  failure to establish those safeguards.
  - Includes both external attacks and internal failures
  - Covers accidental loss, not just malicious access
  - Failure to establish safeguards is itself a breach trigger
  - Applies to both paper and electronic records

- **Real Risk of Significant Harm (RROSH)**: The threshold test for mandatory
  breach notification under PIPEDA. Organizations must notify when a breach
  creates a real risk of significant harm to affected individuals.
  - "Significant harm" includes: bodily harm, humiliation, damage to reputation,
    loss of employment, financial loss, identity theft, negative effects on
    credit record, damage to property
  - "Real risk" means more than speculative; must be a genuine possibility
  - Assessment must consider both sensitivity of information and probability of
    misuse
  - Encryption and other mitigation factors can reduce risk below threshold

- **Sensitivity factors**: Determining whether information poses RROSH requires
  evaluating its sensitivity in context.
  - Financial account numbers, credit card data: high sensitivity
  - Government-issued identifiers (SIN, health card): high sensitivity
  - Passwords, authentication credentials: high sensitivity
  - Health information: high sensitivity
  - Children's information: heightened concern
  - Combination of data elements increases sensitivity
  - Volume of affected individuals is a factor

- **Probability factors**: Assessing likelihood that breach will cause harm.
  - Was data encrypted? Encryption significantly reduces probability
  - Was access targeted or incidental?
  - What is known about who accessed the data?
  - Has data been recovered or contained?
  - Is there evidence of misuse?
  - How long was data exposed?

- **Notification to the Privacy Commissioner**: Mandatory when RROSH threshold
  is met.
  - Must be made "as soon as feasible"
  - Must include prescribed information about the breach
  - Must describe circumstances, information involved, timeline
  - Must outline steps taken to reduce harm and prevent recurrence
  - Failure to report is an offense under PIPEDA

- **Notification to affected individuals**: Mandatory when RROSH threshold is
  met.
  - Must be made "as soon as feasible" after determining RROSH exists
  - Must contain prescribed content elements
  - Direct notification preferred; indirect permitted in limited circumstances
  - Must enable individuals to understand risk and protect themselves
  - Cannot be used for marketing or other purposes

- **Notification to third parties**: May be required when third parties can
  reduce risk of harm.
  - Organizations should notify when third party can mitigate harm
  - Examples: law enforcement, credit bureaus, financial institutions
  - This is a "should consider" rather than mandatory requirement
  - Assessment based on whether notification would reduce risk

- **Record-keeping requirements**: Organizations must maintain breach records
  regardless of RROSH determination.
  - All breaches must be recorded, not just reportable ones
  - Records must be kept for 24 months after breach determination
  - Commissioner may request access to records
  - Must contain sufficient information to verify compliance
  - Records must show RROSH assessment rationale

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
