| Laptop stolen containing encrypted customer data | Assess RROSH; strong encryption may reduce risk below threshold; document assessment | Encryption is key factor; still requires formal assessment and documentation |
| Email sent to wrong recipient with SIN numbers | Notify OPC and affected individuals; contact recipient to delete | SIN creates high sensitivity; direct disclosure occurred |
| Ransomware attack on customer database | Assess if data was exfiltrated or only encrypted; notify if access occurred | Access alone may trigger RROSH even without exfiltration evidence |
| Vendor breach affecting your customers' data | You remain accountable; coordinate notification; assess your own obligations | Accountability extends to third-party handling |
| Employee snooping on co-worker records | Internal breach still counts; assess RROSH based on sensitivity | Unauthorized access includes internal unauthorized access |
| Breach discovered but contained immediately | Still assess RROSH; document containment as factor reducing probability | Speed of containment reduces but does not eliminate RROSH consideration |
| Mass mailing with visible email addresses | Assess sensitivity; names alone may not meet threshold | Context matters; email-only exposure typically lower sensitivity |
| Physical file theft from office | Paper records included; assess what information was in files | PIPEDA breach provisions cover all formats |

## Gotchas

- **All breaches require records, not just RROSH breaches**: Organizations must
  maintain records of every breach for 24 months, even if no notification was
  required. The Commissioner can request these records.

- **"As soon as feasible" is not "immediately"**: Organizations may take time
  to investigate and contain a breach before notifying, but delays must be
  justified. Excessive delay can itself constitute non-compliance.

- **Encryption quality matters**: Not all encryption provides equivalent
  protection. Weak encryption or compromised keys may not reduce RROSH below
  threshold.

- **Assessment must be documented**: Even when determining no notification is
  required, the organization must document its reasoning. This documentation
  may be reviewed by the Commissioner.

- **Third-party breaches do not transfer accountability**: When a service
  provider experiences a breach affecting your data, you remain accountable
  for notification obligations to your customers.

- **Provincial variations exist**: Quebec, Alberta, and British Columbia have
  their own breach notification requirements that may differ from PIPEDA.
  Organizations operating in multiple jurisdictions must comply with all
  applicable laws.

- **Notification cannot wait for investigation completion**: If RROSH is
  evident, notification must proceed even if investigation continues. Updated
  notifications can be sent as more information becomes available.

- **Combining data elements changes sensitivity**: Information that seems
  innocuous alone (name, date of birth) may create RROSH when combined with
  other elements in the breach.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Notification threshold | Real Risk of Significant Harm (RROSH) | Mandatory trigger |
| Notification timing | As soon as feasible | No fixed deadline |
| Record retention | 24 months | From date of RROSH determination |
| Records required for | All breaches | Not just RROSH breaches |
| Breach notification mandatory date | November 1, 2018 | When PIPEDA amendments took effect |
| Maximum penalty | $100,000 per offense | For knowing failure to report |
| Prescribed content | Yes | Regulations specify required elements |
| Direct notification | Preferred | Indirect only in limited cases |

## Related topics

- **Safeguards (2.14)**: Breach notification is triggered by safeguard failures;
  strong safeguards reduce breach likelihood and RROSH probability
- **Accountability (2.2)**: Organization remains accountable for breaches at
  third parties; accountability includes having breach response procedures
- **Provincial private sector laws (2.16)**: Quebec Law 25, Alberta PIPA, and
  BC PIPA have distinct breach notification requirements and thresholds
- **OPC complaints and investigations (1.3)**: Commissioner may investigate
  breach handling; failure to notify is an offense
- **Health sector breaches (Domain IV)**: Provincial health privacy acts have
  separate breach notification requirements for PHI
- **Record-keeping obligations**: Breach records are part of broader
  accountability requirements under PIPEDA Principle 1

### 2.16: Know transborder data flow regulations and requirements

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.16: Know transborder data flow regulations and requirements

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Transborder data flow**: The transfer of personal information from Canada to
  another country for processing, storage, or access. PIPEDA does not prohibit
  cross-border transfers but requires organizations to maintain accountability.
  - No adequacy requirement under PIPEDA (unlike GDPR)
  - Accountability principle applies regardless of where data is transferred
  - Organizations must use contractual and other means to provide comparable
    protection
  - Both the transfer itself and the foreign processing must comply

- **Accountability for third-party transfers**: Under PIPEDA Principle 1, an
  organization remains responsible for personal information transferred to a
  third party for processing, including foreign processors.
  - Written contracts must specify permitted uses and disclosures
  - Comparable level of protection must be ensured
  - Organization cannot delegate away its accountability
  - Due diligence required before transferring

- **Transparency requirements**: Organizations must be open about their
  transborder data practices. The OPC expects organizations to inform
  individuals that their information may be transferred outside Canada.
  - Privacy policies should disclose cross-border transfers
  - Should identify countries where data may be processed
  - Should explain implications for individual rights
  - Notice at or before collection is best practice

- **Consent considerations**: Individuals should be made aware of transborder
  transfers when providing consent. The OPC has indicated that meaningful
  consent may require disclosure of foreign transfer.
  - Express consent not always required for transfers
  - Implied consent may suffice when transfer is reasonably expected
  - Must inform about transfers to fulfill openness requirement
  - Consent obtained for original purpose extends to processing in another
    jurisdiction if for same purpose

- **Foreign law access**: When personal information is transferred to another
  country, it may become subject to that country's laws, including government
  access provisions.
  - USA PATRIOT Act concerns have been prominent in Canadian context
  - CLOUD Act enables US access to data held by US companies globally
  - Organizations should assess foreign legal environment
  - Risk assessment and mitigation may be required
  - Some sectors have specific restrictions (e.g., health information)

- **Provincial variations**: Quebec's Law 25 (Private Sector Act amendments)
  introduced stricter transborder transfer requirements than PIPEDA.
  - Quebec requires privacy impact assessment before transfer
  - Must determine receiving jurisdiction provides adequate protection
  - If inadequate, must use contractual or other safeguards
  - More prescriptive framework than PIPEDA's accountability approach

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Storing customer data in US cloud service | Due diligence on provider; contract requiring comparable protection; disclose in privacy policy | Accountability remains; must ensure protection continues abroad |
| Customer asks where their data is stored | Provide information about countries where data is transferred | Openness principle requires transparency about data locations |
| Provincial health data to foreign processor | Check provincial health privacy laws; may prohibit or restrict | Health information often has stricter cross-border requirements |
| Quebec organization using foreign service | Conduct PIA; assess adequacy of foreign jurisdiction; document safeguards | Law 25 has explicit transborder assessment requirements |
| Foreign government requests data held abroad | Organization must understand and disclose this possibility; no PIPEDA exemption | Foreign law applies where data is located; transparency required |
| Call center in another country | Contract for comparable protection; train on Canadian requirements; audit compliance | Processing is still for the Canadian organization; accountability persists |

## Gotchas

- **PIPEDA does not require adequacy determination**: Unlike GDPR, PIPEDA does
  not prohibit transfers to countries lacking adequate protection. The
  organization must ensure protection through contractual means, not rely on
  government adequacy findings.

- **Disclosure in privacy policy is not enough**: Organizations must actively
  ensure comparable protection, not merely inform individuals about the
  transfer. Transparency is necessary but not sufficient.

- **Foreign law access is a real risk**: The OPC has recognized that data
  transferred to other countries becomes subject to those countries' laws. This
  is a factor organizations should consider and may need to disclose.

- **Provincial laws may be stricter**: Quebec's Law 25 imposes explicit
  assessment requirements for transborder transfers. Organizations subject to
  Quebec law must conduct PIAs and document adequacy analysis.

- **Sector-specific restrictions exist**: Some provincial health privacy laws
  restrict or prohibit cross-border transfers of health information. Always
  check sector-specific legislation.

- **Cloud does not eliminate responsibility**: Using a cloud provider in another
  country does not transfer accountability. The organization remains responsible
  regardless of where or how data is stored.

- **Subcontractors extend the chain**: If a foreign processor uses further
  subcontractors, the original organization must ensure protection extends
  through the entire chain.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| PIPEDA adequacy requirement | None | No formal adequacy determination required |
| Accountability for foreign processing | Yes | Organization remains fully accountable |
| Transparency requirement | Yes | Must disclose cross-border transfers |
| Quebec PIA requirement | Yes | Required before transfer under Law 25 |
| Express consent required | No | Not specifically required for transfers |
| Contract required for third parties | Yes (best practice) | Must use contractual means for protection |
| Sector restrictions | Varies | Health and other sectors may have prohibitions |

## Related topics

- **Accountability (2.5)**: Accountability principle is the foundation for
  transborder transfer requirements; organizations cannot contract away
  responsibility by transferring data abroad
- **Third-party processing**: Transborder transfers often involve third-party
  processors; same accountability requirements apply
- **Consent (2.8)**: Meaningful consent may require disclosure of foreign
  transfers as part of notice at collection
- **Safeguards (2.14)**: Organizations must ensure foreign recipients implement
  appropriate safeguards for the information
- **Provincial private sector laws (2.19)**: Quebec Law 25 has stricter
  transborder requirements than PIPEDA
- **Openness (2.11)**: Privacy policies should disclose cross-border data
  practices to fulfill openness principle

### 2.17: Understand Canada's Anti-Spam Legislation (CASL)

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 2.17: Understand Canada's Anti-Spam Legislation (CASL)

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Key concepts

- **CASL scope**: Canada's Anti-Spam Legislation governs commercial electronic
  messages (CEMs) sent to or from Canada.
  - Part of Domain II: Canadian Privacy Laws and Practices - Private Sector
  - Distinct from PIPEDA but related to commercial privacy practices
  - Focuses on consent, identification, and unsubscribe mechanisms

- **Consent requirements**: CASL establishes rules for obtaining consent before
  sending commercial electronic messages.
  - Express consent: Explicit permission obtained from the recipient
  - Implied consent: Permitted in specific circumstances (existing business
    relationship, conspicuous publication of address)
  - Consent must be obtained before sending CEMs

- **Identification requirements**: Senders must clearly identify themselves in
  commercial electronic messages.
  - Include sender's name and contact information
  - Identify the person on whose behalf the message is sent (if applicable)

- **Unsubscribe mechanisms**: CASL mandates functional opt-out mechanisms in
  all CEMs.
  - Must be clearly presented in every message
  - Must remain functional for at least 60 days after message is sent
  - Unsubscribe requests must be processed within 10 business days

- **Computer programs**: CASL regulates the installation of computer programs
  on devices.
  - Consent required before installing programs on another person's computer
  - Applies to automatic downloads and software installations
  - Covers malware, spyware, and other unauthorized software

## Common scenarios

| Scenario                                   | Correct approach                          | Why                                        |
| ------------------------------------------ | ----------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------ |
| Sending promotional email to new contact   | Obtain express consent first              | No prior relationship means no implied consent |
| Existing customer relationship             | Implied consent may apply                 | Business relationship exception exists     |
| Recipient requests unsubscribe             | Process within 10 business days           | CASL mandates timely processing            |
| Installing software on user's device       | Obtain consent before installation        | CASL covers computer program installation  |
| Contact address published on website       | May qualify for implied consent           | Conspicuous publication exception          |

## Gotchas

- **CASL vs. PIPEDA distinction**: CASL specifically targets commercial
  electronic messages and computer programs, while PIPEDA covers broader
  personal information handling - they are complementary but distinct laws
- **Geographic scope**: CASL applies to messages sent to or from Canada,
  capturing organizations outside Canada that send to Canadian recipients
- **Implied consent limits**: Implied consent has time limits and specific
  conditions - it does not provide indefinite permission
- **Computer program consent**: Consent requirements extend beyond email to
  software installation, including updates and bundled programs

## Limits and defaults

| Item                          | Value              | Notes                                      |
| ----------------------------- | ------------------ | ------------------------------------------ |
| Unsubscribe mechanism active  | 60 days minimum    | Must work for 60 days after sending        |
| Unsubscribe processing        | 10 business days   | Maximum time to honor opt-out request      |
| Implied consent (inquiry)     | 6 months           | From date of inquiry if no transaction     |
| Implied consent (transaction) | 2 years            | From date of purchase or contract          |

## Penalties

- **Administrative monetary penalties**: CASL allows significant penalties for
  non-compliance.
  - Up to CAD 1 million per violation for individuals
  - Up to CAD 10 million per violation for corporations
  - CRTC has authority to issue penalties
- **Private right of action**: Individuals and organizations can sue for CASL
  violations (implementation suspended)

## Related topics

- Objective 2.1: PIPEDA privacy principles (related consent frameworks)
- Objective 2.9: PIPEDA consent requirements (complementary consent rules)
- Domain II: Private-sector privacy laws and practices
- CRTC enforcement authority for CASL compliance

---

**Note**: This source provides only high-level exam preparation guidance. For
comprehensive coverage of CASL provisions, consent requirements, and
enforcement mechanisms, consult the official CIPP/C textbook "Canadian Privacy:
Data Protection Law and Policy for the Practitioner."

### 2.18: Understand CASL penalties for non-compliance

### 2.19: Know provincial private sector laws (Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, Quebec Law 25)

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 2.19: Know provincial private sector laws (Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, Quebec Law 25)

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Substantially similar designation**: Provincial private sector privacy laws
  may be deemed "substantially similar" to PIPEDA by federal order-in-council.
  When designated, the provincial law applies instead of PIPEDA for
  intra-provincial commercial activities.
  - Three provinces have substantially similar private sector laws: Alberta, BC,
    Quebec
  - PIPEDA still applies to federally regulated organizations and
    interprovincial/international commercial activities
  - Designation is formal legal process, not automatic

- **Alberta Personal Information Protection Act (PIPA)**: Enacted 2004, deemed
  substantially similar to PIPEDA. Governs collection, use, and disclosure of
  personal information by private sector organizations in Alberta.
  - Applies to provincially regulated organizations for intra-provincial
    activities
  - Contains 8 principles similar to PIPEDA's 10
  - Mandatory breach notification to Commissioner and affected individuals
  - Commissioner can order compliance and impose administrative penalties
  - Employee information covered (unlike some jurisdictions)
  - Includes provisions for automated decision-making transparency

- **BC Personal Information Protection Act (PIPA)**: Enacted 2004, deemed
  substantially similar to PIPEDA. Governs private sector privacy in British
  Columbia.
  - Similar structure to Alberta PIPA
  - Mandatory breach notification requirements
  - Commissioner can make binding orders
  - Employee information is covered
  - Includes provisions requiring explanation of automated decisions that
    significantly affect individuals
  - Whistleblower protection provisions

- **Quebec Law 25 (Act Respecting the Protection of Personal Information in the
  Private Sector, as amended)**: Quebec's private sector law was substantially
  amended by Bill 64 (2021), with provisions phasing in through 2024. Known as
  "Law 25" or "Quebec Private Sector Act."
  - Original law dates to 1994; major amendments effective 2022-2024
  - Strongest provincial private sector privacy law in Canada
  - Explicit requirements for privacy impact assessments (PIAs)
  - Mandatory privacy officer designation
  - Strict transborder transfer requirements with adequacy assessment
  - Broader individual rights including portability and erasure
  - Significant administrative monetary penalties (up to $25M or 4% of worldwide
    turnover)
  - Profiling and automated decision-making provisions with opt-out rights
  - Biometric information specific protections
  - Explicit consent required for disclosure to third parties

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Alberta business processes customer data | Apply Alberta PIPA for intra-provincial activities | Substantially similar law applies instead of PIPEDA |
| BC company has breach affecting customers | Notify BC Commissioner and affected individuals per BC PIPA | Provincial breach notification requirements apply |
| Quebec business uses automated profiling | Provide transparency about profiling; offer opt-out mechanism | Law 25 requires disclosure and withdrawal rights for profiling |
| Alberta organization transfers data to processor in Ontario | PIPEDA applies to interprovincial transfer | Provincial law only covers intra-provincial activities |
| Quebec business transferring data abroad | Conduct PIA; assess adequacy of receiving jurisdiction; document safeguards | Law 25 has explicit transborder assessment requirements |
| Federally regulated bank in BC | Apply PIPEDA, not BC PIPA | Federal industries remain under PIPEDA regardless of province |
| Quebec business collecting biometric data | Obtain express consent; inform of purposes; provide destruction timeline | Law 25 has specific biometric requirements |

## Gotchas

- **Provincial laws only apply intra-provincially**: If data crosses provincial
  or national borders in the commercial activity, PIPEDA applies. A BC company
  selling to Alberta customers is subject to PIPEDA for that transaction.

- **Federally regulated industries remain under PIPEDA**: Banks, airlines,
  telecommunications, and other federally regulated businesses are subject to
  PIPEDA even for activities within a province with substantially similar law.

- **Quebec Law 25 is significantly stricter than PIPEDA**: Organizations
  operating in Quebec face higher compliance burdens including mandatory PIAs,
  stricter consent requirements, and substantial penalties.

- **Provincial breach notification differs**: Each province has its own breach
  notification thresholds and requirements. Know the specific triggers for each
  jurisdiction.

- **Employee information treatment varies**: All three provincial laws cover
  employee personal information, but specific rules differ. Quebec has explicit
  employee monitoring provisions.

- **Automated decision-making provisions**: BC and Quebec both have transparency
  requirements for automated decisions. Quebec Law 25 provides opt-out rights
  from automated decisions.

- **Substantially similar does not mean identical**: Each provincial law has
  unique provisions. Compliance with PIPEDA does not guarantee compliance with
  provincial laws.

- **Quebec penalties are GDPR-scale**: Law 25 introduced administrative monetary
  penalties up to $25M or 4% of worldwide turnover, significantly higher than
  other Canadian jurisdictions.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Provinces with substantially similar private sector laws | 3 | Alberta, BC, Quebec |
| Alberta PIPA enacted | 2004 | Deemed substantially similar same year |
| BC PIPA enacted | 2004 | Deemed substantially similar same year |
| Quebec Private Sector Act original | 1994 | Predates PIPEDA |
| Quebec Law 25 amendments | 2021-2024 | Phased implementation through September 2024 |
| Quebec maximum administrative penalty | $25M or 4% turnover | Whichever is greater |
| Quebec PIA requirement | Mandatory | Required before new projects involving PI |
| Quebec privacy officer | Mandatory | Must designate and publish contact info |
| Breach notification | Mandatory (all three) | Specific thresholds vary by province |

## Related topics

- **PIPEDA principles (2.1)**: Provincial laws share core principles but differ
  in specifics and enforcement
- **Substantially similar determination (2.17)**: Formal federal process for
  designating provincial laws
- **Breach notification (2.12)**: Each province has distinct breach notification
  requirements
- **Consent requirements (2.8)**: Quebec Law 25 requires explicit consent for
  disclosure to third parties
- **Transborder transfers (2.16)**: Quebec has stricter cross-border transfer
  requirements than PIPEDA
- **Automated decision-making**: BC and Quebec have specific transparency and
  opt-out provisions
- **Privacy impact assessments (3.5)**: Quebec mandates PIAs for private sector;
  Alberta and BC encourage them

---

## Domain 3: Canadian Privacy Laws and Practices - Public Sector

**Weight: 12%**

### 3.1: Know the privacy principles that are the foundation of the Privacy Act

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 3.1: Know the privacy principles that are the foundation of the Privacy Act

Source: CIPP/C Free Study Guide (IAPP)

> **Note**: This source is a preparation overview, not comprehensive content.
> The authoritative reference is "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and
> Policy for the Practitioner" (IAPP).

## Key concepts

- **Privacy Act scope**: Federal public-sector legislation governing how
  government institutions collect, use, and disclose personal information
- **Access rights**: Individuals have the right to request access to personal
  information held by government institutions
- **Response timeline**: Government institutions must respond to access requests
  within 30 days

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Individual requests personal information from federal agency | Agency must respond within 30 days | Privacy Act mandates this timeline |

## Gotchas

- **30 days, not 45**: The Privacy Act requires a 30-day response window for
  access requests, which differs from some provincial timelines

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Access request response time | 30 days | From receipt of request |

## Related topics

- Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy Acts (provincial/territorial):
  Parallel public-sector legislation at provincial level (objective 3.3)
- Privacy Impact Assessments: Required process for federal government
  initiatives (objective 3.2)
- PIPEDA: Federal private-sector counterpart with different principles
  (Domain II)

---

*This extract is limited by the source material. The free study guide provides
exam structure and sample questions but does not detail the Privacy Act's
foundational principles. Consult the official CIPP/C textbook for comprehensive
coverage of: purpose limitation, consent requirements, collection limitations,
accuracy obligations, retention limits, security safeguards, openness, and
individual access rights under the Privacy Act.*

### 3.2: Understand the scope and application of the Privacy Act

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 3.2: Understand the scope and application of the Privacy Act

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Key concepts

- **Privacy Act**: Federal legislation governing personal information held by
  government institutions
  - Applies to federal government institutions, not private sector
  - Part of the public-sector privacy framework in Canada
  - Works alongside provincial Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy
    Acts at the provincial/territorial level

- **Access rights**: Individuals have the right to request access to their
  personal information held by government institutions
  - Government institutions must respond within 30 days (exam-tested timeframe)

- **Oversight**: The Privacy Commissioner of Canada oversees compliance with the
  Privacy Act
  - In provinces without a dedicated privacy commissioner, the Ombudsman handles
    privacy oversight

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Individual requests access to personal information from federal government | Process request and respond within 30 days | Privacy Act mandates this response timeline |
| Determining oversight body in province without privacy commissioner | Look to the Ombudsman | Default oversight structure in those jurisdictions |
| Organization collects data for commercial purposes | Apply PIPEDA, not Privacy Act | Privacy Act covers government, not commercial activity |

## Gotchas

- **Privacy Act vs. PIPEDA**: The Privacy Act applies only to federal government
  institutions. Commercial activities fall under PIPEDA or provincial
  private-sector laws, not the Privacy Act.

- **Response timeline**: The 30-day response requirement is a specific,
  exam-tested detail. Do not confuse with other timelines in Canadian privacy
  law.

- **Provincial variation**: Provincial and territorial governments are covered
  by their own Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy Acts, not the
  federal Privacy Act.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Access request response time | 30 days | Federal government institutions under Privacy Act |

## Related topics

- 3.1 Know the privacy principles that are the foundation of the Privacy Act:
  The Privacy Act embeds foundational privacy principles that guide how
  government institutions handle personal information
- 3.3 Conduct Privacy Impact Assessments: PIAs are required for federal
  government programs under the Privacy Act framework
- Provincial FOIPP Acts: Similar legislation at provincial/territorial level
  with their own scope and application rules

---

Note: This source is an introductory study guide. For detailed provisions,
exceptions, and case law related to the Privacy Act, consult the authoritative
CIPP/C textbook "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the
Practitioner."

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 3.2: Understand the scope and application of the Privacy Act

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Privacy Act (R.S.C., 1985, c. P-21)**: Federal legislation governing how
  government institutions collect, use, disclose, retain, and dispose of
  personal information
  - Enacted in 1983, came into force July 1, 1983
  - Applies exclusively to federal government institutions listed in the
    Schedule to the Act
  - Does not apply to private sector organizations (covered by PIPEDA) or
    provincial/territorial public bodies

- **Personal information under the Privacy Act**: Information about an
  identifiable individual that is recorded in any form
  - Includes name, address, age, education, employment history, financial
    information, medical history, criminal record
  - Excludes information about federal employees related to their position or
    functions (e.g., job title, business contact information)

- **Government institution**: Any department or ministry of state of the
  Government of Canada, or any body or office listed in the Schedule to the
  Privacy Act
  - Includes departments, agencies, Crown corporations, and other federal
    bodies
  - The Schedule is periodically updated to add or remove institutions

- **Privacy Commissioner of Canada**: Independent Officer of Parliament who
  oversees compliance with the Privacy Act
  - Investigates complaints from individuals
  - Conducts audits and reviews
  - Makes recommendations (not binding orders) to government institutions
  - Can apply to the Federal Court in certain circumstances

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Individual requests access to their personal information | Respond within 30 days of receipt | Privacy Act section 14 mandates this timeline; extensions possible in limited circumstances |
| New federal program collects personal information | Complete Privacy Impact Assessment before implementation | TBS Directive requires PIAs for new or substantially modified programs |
| Third party requests disclosure of personal information | Assess against specific exemptions in sections 7-8 | Disclosure without consent permitted only under enumerated exceptions |
| Personal information no longer needed for original purpose | Dispose of according to retention schedules | Section 6(3) requires disposal when no longer required |

## Gotchas

- **Privacy Act vs. PIPEDA**: The Privacy Act applies only to federal
  government institutions, not private sector organizations. A common exam trap
  conflates these two regimes. PIPEDA governs commercial activities; the
  Privacy Act governs federal public sector.

- **30-day response timeline**: The Privacy Act requires response within 30
  calendar days, not business days. This differs from some provincial
  legislation. Extensions are permitted but require notification.

- **Recommendations vs. orders**: The Privacy Commissioner can only make
  recommendations under the Privacy Act, not binding orders. This contrasts
  with some provincial commissioners who have order-making power.

- **Institutions covered**: Not all federal entities are covered. The Schedule
  to the Act must be checked to confirm coverage. Some entities may be added or
  removed over time.

- **Employee information exception**: Information about federal employees
  related to their position or functions is not considered personal
  information for Access to Information Act purposes, but this exception is
  narrower under the Privacy Act.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Response time for access requests | 30 days | From date of receipt; calendar days |
| Time extension with notice | 30 additional days | For consultations or voluminous requests |
| Complaint filing deadline | 1 year | From refusal or failure to respond |
| Records retention | Per institution schedules | Must follow Treasury Board guidelines |
| Federal Court application deadline | 45 days | After receiving Commissioner's report |

## Related topics

- **Treasury Board Secretariat Directive on Privacy Practices**: Operational
  requirements for implementing the Privacy Act across government institutions
- **Privacy Impact Assessments (PIAs)**: Required for new or modified programs
  that raise privacy risks; covered in objective 3.4
- **Access to Information Act**: Companion legislation for access to government
  records; often administered together with Privacy Act
- **Provincial FOIP legislation**: Provincial public bodies are covered by
  their own freedom of information and protection of privacy acts, not the
  federal Privacy Act

### 3.3: Know how PIPEDA and Privacy Act differ in scope and enforcement

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 3.3: Know how PIPEDA and Privacy Act differ in scope and enforcement

Source: CIPP/C Free Study Guide (IAPP)

> **Note**: This source is a preparation overview, not comprehensive content.
> The authoritative reference is "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and
> Policy for the Practitioner" (IAPP).

## Key concepts

- **PIPEDA scope**: Federal private-sector legislation applying to organizations
  that collect, use, or disclose personal information in the course of
  commercial activities
- **Privacy Act scope**: Federal public-sector legislation governing how
  government institutions handle personal information
- **Sector distinction**: PIPEDA regulates private-sector commercial activities;
  Privacy Act regulates federal government institutions
- **Commercial activity requirement**: PIPEDA applies only to commercial
  purposes, not journalistic, artistic, literary, or personal use

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Applicable law | Why |
| -------- | -------------- | --- |
| Private company collecting customer data for sales | PIPEDA | Commercial activity in private sector |
| Federal department maintaining citizen records | Privacy Act | Federal government institution |
| Provincial government health agency | Neither (provincial law applies) | Falls under provincial public-sector legislation |
| Private company collecting data for internal HR | May be exempt from PIPEDA | Employee records in provincially-regulated workplaces excluded |

## Gotchas

- **Commercial purpose is key for PIPEDA**: The exam tests whether you can
  identify that PIPEDA applies to "commercial purposes" specifically, not all
  organizational activities
- **Provincial carve-outs**: Organizations in provinces with "substantially
  similar" legislation (Quebec, Alberta, BC) may be subject to provincial
  private-sector laws instead of PIPEDA for intra-provincial activities
- **Federal vs provincial jurisdiction**: Federally-regulated industries
  (banking, telecommunications, transportation) remain under PIPEDA regardless
  of province

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Privacy Act response time | 30 days | For access requests to federal institutions |
| PIPEDA enforcement | OPC recommendations | Commissioner issues findings, not binding orders |
| Privacy Act oversight | Privacy Commissioner of Canada | Same commissioner oversees both acts |

## Related topics

- Substantially similar laws: Provincial private-sector laws that can displace
  PIPEDA for intra-provincial commercial activities (objective 2.2)
- Provincial and territorial public-sector laws: FOIPP acts that parallel the
  Privacy Act at provincial level (objective 3.11)
- OPC role: Privacy Commissioner of Canada oversees both PIPEDA and Privacy Act
  (objective 1.7)
- Federal vs provincial jurisdiction: Determines which private-sector law
  applies (objective 2.3)

---

*This extract is limited by the source material. The free study guide provides
exam structure and sample questions but does not detail the enforcement
mechanisms, remedy differences, or procedural distinctions between PIPEDA and
the Privacy Act. Consult the official CIPP/C textbook for comprehensive
coverage of: enforcement powers and limitations, complaint processes,
investigative authority, binding vs non-binding decisions, Federal Court
review, damages and penalties, and the historical evolution of both statutes.*

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 3.3: Know how PIPEDA and Privacy Act differ in scope and enforcement

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Fundamental distinction**: PIPEDA and the Privacy Act regulate entirely
  different sectors of Canadian society
  - PIPEDA: Private-sector organizations engaged in commercial activities
  - Privacy Act: Federal government institutions
  - No overlap in coverage; the laws are mutually exclusive in application

- **PIPEDA scope of application**: Governs collection, use, and disclosure of
  personal information in the course of commercial activities
  - Private-sector organizations conducting commercial activity
  - Federally regulated industries (banking, telecommunications, airlines,
    broadcasting, railways) regardless of location
  - Interprovincial and international commercial transactions
  - Does not apply to government institutions or non-commercial activities

- **Privacy Act scope of application**: Governs personal information held by
  federal government institutions
  - Federal departments, agencies, and Crown corporations listed in the
    Schedule to the Act
  - Applies to information collected for program administration, service
    delivery, and internal operations
  - Does not apply to provincial or territorial governments (covered by
    provincial FOIPPAs)
  - Does not apply to private-sector commercial activities

- **Oversight authority**: Both laws are overseen by the Privacy Commissioner of
  Canada, but with different powers
  - Same office handles complaints under both regimes
  - Commissioner's enforcement powers differ significantly between the two laws
  - Federal Court plays different roles under each statute

- **Enforcement model - PIPEDA**: Ombudsman model with enhanced powers
  - Commissioner investigates complaints and issues findings and recommendations
  - After November 2018 amendments, Commissioner can seek binding orders from
    Federal Court for serious violations
  - Compliance agreements available as alternative to court proceedings
  - Mandatory breach notification to Commissioner when real risk of significant
    harm exists
  - No direct fines or penalties under PIPEDA itself (but CASL has separate
    penalties)

- **Enforcement model - Privacy Act**: Pure ombudsman model without order-making
  authority
  - Commissioner investigates complaints and makes recommendations only
  - Recommendations are not binding on government institutions
  - No compliance agreement mechanism
  - No mandatory breach notification provisions in the Act itself (though TBS
    directives require breach reporting)
  - Individual can apply to Federal Court if institution does not comply with
    recommendations

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Applicable law | Enforcement path |
| -------- | -------------- | ---------------- |
| Bank refuses customer access to records | PIPEDA | Commissioner investigates; may seek Federal Court order |
| Federal department denies access request | Privacy Act | Commissioner investigates and recommends; individual may apply to Federal Court |
| Private company suffers data breach with risk of harm | PIPEDA | Must notify Commissioner and affected individuals; non-compliance is an offence |
| Federal institution suffers data breach | Privacy Act | TBS directive requires reporting; no statutory breach notification requirement |
| Telecom company misuses customer data | PIPEDA | Commissioner can initiate inquiry or investigate complaint; may seek court orders |
| Canada Revenue Agency discloses taxpayer information improperly | Privacy Act | Commissioner investigates complaint; recommends remedial action |

## Gotchas

- **Same Commissioner, different powers**: A common exam trap assumes the
  Commissioner has identical authority under both laws. Under PIPEDA, the
  Commissioner can seek binding Federal Court orders. Under the Privacy Act,
  the Commissioner can only make non-binding recommendations.

- **Mandatory breach notification**: PIPEDA requires breach notification to the
  Commissioner and affected individuals when there is a real risk of
  significant harm. The Privacy Act has no equivalent statutory requirement -
  breach reporting follows Treasury Board Secretariat directives, not
  legislation.

- **Federal Court role differs**: Under PIPEDA, the Commissioner can apply to
  the Federal Court for orders. Under the Privacy Act, the individual (not the
  Commissioner) must apply to court if the institution fails to act on
  recommendations.

- **No direct fines under either law**: Neither PIPEDA nor the Privacy Act
  imposes direct monetary penalties for privacy violations. PIPEDA creates
  offences for specific violations (e.g., obstruction, retaliation) with fines
  up to $100,000, but these are criminal sanctions, not administrative
  penalties. The Privacy Act has no comparable provisions.

- **Crown corporations can be complex**: Some Crown corporations are listed in
  the Privacy Act Schedule and subject to that law. Others may engage in
  commercial activities subject to PIPEDA. Determining which law applies
  requires checking the Schedule and analyzing the nature of the activity.

- **Provincial government employees**: The Privacy Act does not cover provincial
  or territorial public bodies - these are governed by provincial FOIPPAs. Only
  federal employees working for listed institutions fall under the Privacy Act.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Privacy Act | PIPEDA | Notes |
| ---- | ----------- | ------ | ----- |
| Entities covered | ~260 federal institutions | Private-sector orgs | Schedule lists covered institutions |
| Commissioner's orders | Non-binding recommendations | Can seek court orders | Key enforcement difference |
| Access request deadline | 30 days | 30 days | Same timeline, different laws |
| Breach notification | Not statutory | Mandatory when RROSH | PIPEDA amended 2018; TBS directive for federal |
| Federal Court application | By individual | By Commissioner | Different standing under each law |
| Offences and penalties | None | Up to $100,000 | PIPEDA s. 28 creates specific offences |
| Compliance agreements | Not available | Available | PIPEDA provides voluntary compliance mechanism |

## Related topics

- **Treasury Board Secretariat directives**: Operational policies that
  supplement the Privacy Act, including breach notification requirements that
  are not in the statute itself
- **Federal Court jurisdiction**: Both laws involve Federal Court review, but
  the paths to court and the available remedies differ significantly
- **Provincial FOIPPA legislation**: Provincial and territorial public-sector
  privacy laws cover provincial governments, municipalities, school boards, and
  health authorities
- **PIPEDA's substantially similar provisions**: Some provinces have
  private-sector laws that apply instead of PIPEDA for local commercial
  activities - a feature with no parallel in the Privacy Act
- **Access to Information Act**: Companion to the Privacy Act for access to
  government records; often administered together by the same officials

### 3.4: Understand consent expectations for collection, use, and disclosure

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 3.4: Understand consent expectations for collection, use, and disclosure

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Source limitation

The IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide is an introductory preparation document that
provides exam logistics, certification structure, and high-level domain
outlines. It explicitly notes that substantive legal content requires the
official textbook: "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the
Practitioner."

The guide does not contain specific content on consent expectations for public
sector collection, use, and disclosure under the Privacy Act or provincial
FOIPPAs.

- Privacy principles foundational to the Privacy Act
- Privacy Impact Assessments
- Applicability of provincial/territorial Freedom of Information and Protection
  of Privacy Acts
