*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 3.4: Understand consent expectations for collection, use, and disclosure

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Consent model in public sector differs from private sector**: The Privacy
  Act does not operate on a consent-based model like PIPEDA
  - Government institutions do not typically seek individual consent before
    collecting, using, or disclosing personal information
  - Authority flows from statutory mandates, not individual consent
  - Personal information handling must be authorized by law or necessary for
    program delivery

- **Collection authority under the Privacy Act**: Section 4 limits collection to
  information that relates directly to an operating program or activity
  - Collection must be necessary for the program or activity
  - No explicit consent requirement; authority is statutory
  - Individuals must be informed of the purpose at or before collection (section
    5)

- **Use limitations**: Personal information can only be used for the purpose for
  which it was collected, or a consistent use
  - Consistent use: a use that has a reasonable and direct connection to the
    original purpose
  - Individual consent is not the primary control mechanism
  - Uses are governed by statutory authority and consistency requirements

- **Disclosure provisions (sections 7-8)**: Personal information may be
  disclosed without consent under enumerated circumstances
  - Section 8(2) lists over 15 specific permitted disclosures
  - Consent is only one of many permitted grounds for disclosure
  - Other grounds include law enforcement, legal proceedings, audits, and
    agreements with provincial governments

- **Notice requirements**: While consent is not required, notice is mandatory
  - Individuals must be informed of the purpose of collection (section 5)
  - Personal Information Banks (PIBs) must be published in Info Source
  - Transparency replaces consent as the primary accountability mechanism

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Collecting information for a new program | Ensure collection relates directly to operating program; provide notice of purpose | Section 4 requires direct relation to program; section 5 requires purpose notification |
| Using collected information for related purpose | Assess whether use is consistent with original purpose | Consistent uses permitted without new consent; must document reasoning |
| External agency requests disclosure | Check section 8(2) for applicable exception | Disclosure permitted only under enumerated exceptions; consent is just one option |
| Individual objects to use of their information | Explain statutory authority and consistent use provisions | Privacy Act does not give individuals a general right to refuse uses authorized by law |

## Gotchas

- **No meaningful consent model**: Unlike PIPEDA, the Privacy Act does not
  require consent for collection, use, or disclosure. Exam questions may test
  whether candidates understand this fundamental difference. Expecting "implied"
  or "express" consent analysis in public sector contexts is incorrect.

- **Notice does not equal consent**: The Privacy Act requires informing
  individuals of collection purposes (section 5), but this is a notice
  requirement, not a consent requirement. Information can still be collected
  regardless of the individual's preference.

- **Consistent use is not a consent exception**: In private sector law, using
  information beyond the original purpose requires consent or an exception.
  Under the Privacy Act, consistent uses are permitted without any consent
  analysis - they are authorized uses, not exceptions.

- **Section 8(2)(a) allows consent-based disclosure**: Consent is one of the
  enumerated disclosure grounds under section 8(2)(a), but it is not required.
  Government institutions may obtain consent for disclosure but are not obligated
  to do so if another exception applies.

- **Provincial public sector laws may differ**: Some provincial FOIPPAs
  incorporate consent elements more similar to private-sector models. The
  Privacy Act's approach is federal-specific. Exam questions may test whether
  candidates can distinguish federal from provincial public-sector requirements.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Collection authority | Must relate directly to operating program | Section 4; no consent requirement |
| Notice timing | At or before collection | Section 5; must state purpose |
| Consistent use test | Reasonable and direct connection | TBS guidance defines interpretation |
| Disclosure exceptions | 15+ enumerated grounds | Section 8(2); consent is only one option |
| Individual objection right | No general right to refuse | Differs from private sector consent withdrawal |

## Related topics

- **Section 8(2) disclosure exceptions**: Detailed enumeration of when personal
  information may be disclosed without consent; critical for exam questions
  about public sector disclosure authority
- **Privacy Impact Assessments**: Required when programs present privacy risks;
  must assess whether collection authority exists and notice is adequate
- **Info Source and Personal Information Banks**: Publication requirements that
  support transparency as an alternative to individual consent
- **PIPEDA consent requirements**: Contrasting model where consent is the
  default legal basis for personal information handling
- **Objective 3.5**: Specific exceptions permitting collection, use, or
  disclosure without consent - the detailed application of these concepts

### 3.5: Recognize when collection, use, or disclosure is permitted without consent

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 3.5: Recognize when collection, use, or disclosure is permitted without consent

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Privacy Act operates without a consent model**: Unlike PIPEDA, the Privacy
  Act authorizes collection, use, and disclosure through statutory provisions
  rather than individual consent
  - Consent is one of many permitted grounds for disclosure, not a default
    requirement
  - Authority derives from statutory mandates and program necessity
  - Understanding the specific exceptions is critical for exam questions

- **Collection without consent (section 4)**: Personal information may be
  collected if it relates directly to an operating program or activity
  - No consent requirement; collection is authorized by institutional mandate
  - Collection limited to information directly related to program purposes
  - Indirect collection permitted only with individual's consent or where
    authorized under section 4
  - Notification required under section 5, but notification is not consent

- **Use without consent - consistent use doctrine**: Information may be used
  for purposes beyond the original collection purpose if the use is consistent
  - Consistent use defined as use with a reasonable and direct connection to
    the original purpose
  - Treasury Board Secretariat (TBS) guidance provides interpretation framework
  - Institution must document rationale for determining consistency
  - Individuals cannot refuse authorized uses, unlike private-sector consent
    withdrawal

- **Section 7 - internal use exceptions**: Personal information under control
  of a government institution may be used for purposes it was obtained or
  compiled for, or a consistent use
  - Subsection (a): Use for the purpose collected or a consistent use
  - Subsection (b): Use for which information may be disclosed under section 8

- **Section 8(2) - disclosure exceptions**: The critical enumeration of
  permitted disclosure grounds without consent
  - Contains over 15 specific exceptions covering diverse situations
  - Each exception represents an independent legal authority to disclose
  - Consent under 8(2)(a) is one option, not a prerequisite

## Section 8(2) disclosure exceptions (exam-critical)

| Exception | Authority | Scope |
| --------- | --------- | ----- |
| 8(2)(a) - Consent | Individual consents | Optional, not required |
| 8(2)(b) - Consistent use | Same as collection purpose | Must document connection |
| 8(2)(c) - MP acting for individual | Member of Parliament | Constituent assistance |
| 8(2)(d) - Subpoena, court order | Legal proceedings | Compliance mandatory |
| 8(2)(e) - Law enforcement investigation | Canadian peace officer | Criminal investigation |
| 8(2)(f) - Federal-provincial agreement | Specified by agreement | Enforcement of law |
| 8(2)(g) - MP or Senator oversight | Members of Parliament | Government accountability |
| 8(2)(h) - Internal audit | Audit of government | Accountability purposes |
| 8(2)(i) - National Archives | Library and Archives Canada | Preservation mandate |
| 8(2)(j) - Research or statistics | Research purposes | Strict controls apply |
| 8(2)(k) - Aboriginal organization | First Nations, Inuit, Mtis | Specific agreements |
| 8(2)(l) - Any person or body | Public interest | Head of institution discretion |
| 8(2)(m) - Benefit to individual | Disclosed for individual's benefit | Compassionate grounds |

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Applicable exception | Key consideration |
| -------- | -------------------- | ----------------- |
| RCMP requests employee records for criminal investigation | 8(2)(e) - Law enforcement | Must be investigation of specific offence; investigative body must be Canadian |
| Court issues subpoena for personal information | 8(2)(d) - Legal proceedings | Compliance is mandatory; document the disclosure |
| Researcher requests anonymized health data | 8(2)(j) - Research | Must ensure appropriate safeguards; may require ethics review |
| MP contacts department on behalf of constituent | 8(2)(c) - MP for individual | MP must be acting on individual's behalf; document request |
| Sharing personal info between federal departments | 8(2)(b) or (f) | If consistent use, use (b); if formal agreement exists, use (f) |
| Individual's family member seeks emergency disclosure | 8(2)(m) - Benefit to individual | Must clearly benefit the individual; compassionate circumstances |
| Audit of program by internal auditor | 8(2)(h) - Internal audit | Authorized for government accountability purposes |

## Gotchas

- **Consent is optional, not required**: Section 8(2)(a) permits disclosure
  with consent, but does not require it. If another exception applies, consent
  is unnecessary. Exam questions may test whether candidates incorrectly
  believe consent must always be sought first.

- **Law enforcement exception has limits**: Section 8(2)(e) applies only to
  investigations by Canadian peace officers into specific offences. Fishing
  expeditions or foreign law enforcement requests do not qualify under this
  provision.

- **Research exception requires safeguards**: Disclosure for research under
  8(2)(j) is not automatic. The head of the institution must be satisfied that
  the research purpose cannot be achieved without the personal information and
  that appropriate confidentiality protections exist.

- **Consistent use is not unlimited**: The consistent use exception under
  8(2)(b) requires a reasonable and direct connection to the original purpose.
  Remote or tangential connections do not qualify. TBS guidance emphasizes
  documentation of the consistency analysis.

- **Head of institution discretion under 8(2)(l)**: The public interest
  exception gives the head of the institution discretion to disclose to "any
  person or body" where clearly in the public interest. This broad authority
  requires careful exercise and documentation.

- **Provincial public sector laws differ**: Section 8(2) applies to federal
  institutions under the Privacy Act. Provincial FOIPPAs have their own
  disclosure provisions with different exception structures. Exam questions
  may distinguish federal from provincial exceptions.

- **Disclosure requires documentation**: All disclosures under section 8(2)
  must be recorded. Section 8(4) requires that requests for disclosure be
  recorded in the same manner as the information itself.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Consent requirement | Not required | One of many disclosure grounds |
| Section 8(2) exceptions | 15+ enumerated grounds | Each is independent authority |
| Consistent use standard | Reasonable and direct connection | TBS guidance applies |
| Law enforcement scope | Canadian peace officers | Investigation of specific offence |
| Research disclosure | Head of institution discretion | Safeguards required |
| Disclosure record keeping | Mandatory | Section 8(4) requirement |
| Public interest disclosure | Head of institution discretion | Must be clearly in public interest |

## Related topics

- **Objective 3.4 - Consent expectations**: Foundation for understanding why
  consent is not the primary basis for personal information handling in the
  public sector
- **Privacy Impact Assessments**: Must assess whether collection and disclosure
  authority exists under statutory provisions
- **Info Source and Personal Information Banks**: Publication requirements that
  support transparency as a substitute for consent-based controls
- **PIPEDA consent exceptions**: Private-sector parallel with different
  structure; sections 7(1), 7(2), 7(3) of PIPEDA enumerate separate exceptions
  for collection, use, and disclosure
- **Treasury Board Secretariat directives**: Operational guidance interpreting
  Privacy Act provisions, including consistent use doctrine

### 3.6: Understand the individual's right of access to their personal information

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 3.6: Understand the individual's right of access to their personal information

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Key concepts

- **Right of access under the Privacy Act**: Individuals may request access to
  personal information held about them by federal government institutions
  - This is a fundamental right within the public-sector privacy framework
  - Access requests are a core component of Domain III (Public-Sector Laws and
    Practices) on the CIPP/C exam

- **30-day response timeline**: Government institutions must respond to access
  requests within 30 calendar days
  - This is explicitly tested on the exam (see example question in study guide)
  - The timeline applies to federal institutions under the Privacy Act
  - Provincial FOIPPAs may have different response periods

- **Privacy Act as foundation**: The Privacy Act establishes privacy principles
  for the federal public sector, including access rights
  - One of three main components of Domain III alongside PIAs and provincial
    FOIPPAs
  - Candidates must distinguish between federal Privacy Act requirements and
    provincial legislation

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Individual requests access to personal information from government institution | Respond within 30 days | Privacy Act requirement; explicitly tested on exam |
| Question asks about response timeline for federal access request | Select 30 days (not 10, 15, or 45) | Study guide example question confirms this value |
| Determining which law governs access request | Identify whether federal or provincial institution | Privacy Act applies to federal; provincial FOIPPAs apply to provincial bodies |

## Gotchas

- **30 days is the federal standard**: The specific 30-day timeline applies to
  the federal Privacy Act. Provincial and territorial Freedom of Information
  and Protection of Privacy Acts may specify different response periods. Exam
  questions may test the distinction between federal and provincial
  requirements.

- **Access rights differ by sector**: The right of access under the Privacy Act
  (public sector) operates separately from access provisions under PIPEDA
  (private sector) or provincial health privacy laws. Candidates must identify
  the correct legal framework based on the institution type.

- **Response timeline is a maximum**: The 30 days represents the deadline for
  response, not a standard processing time.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Response deadline | 30 calendar days | Federal Privacy Act requirement |
| Governing law | Privacy Act | For federal government institutions |
| Provincial equivalent | FOIPPAs | May have different timelines |

## Related topics

- **Objective 3.1 - Privacy Act principles**: Foundation for understanding
  access rights within the public-sector framework
- **Objective 3.2 - Scope of the Privacy Act**: Determines when federal access
  rights apply vs provincial legislation
- **Objective 3.7 - Right of correction**: Paired with access; individuals may
  request correction after obtaining access
- **Objective 3.11 - Provincial/territorial public sector legislation**:
  Distinct access provisions under FOIPPAs

---

Note: The CIPP/C Free Study Guide provides limited coverage of this objective.
The 30-day response timeline is confirmed via the example exam question. For
detailed treatment of access request procedures, exemptions, identity
verification, and information format requirements, refer to the official CIPP/C
textbook "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the
Practitioner."

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 3.6: Understand the individual's right of access to their personal information

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Right of access under the Privacy Act (section 12)**: Individuals have a
  statutory right to request access to personal information about themselves
  held by federal government institutions
  - Right applies to Canadian citizens, permanent residents, and any individual
    present in Canada
  - Request must be made in writing to the appropriate government institution
  - Institutions must provide access unless a specific exemption applies
  - Access right is fundamental to transparency and accountability in the
    public sector

- **Personal Information Banks (PIBs)**: Government institutions maintain
  registered collections of personal information organized by program
  - Info Source publication describes PIBs and how to access them
  - Individuals should identify the relevant PIB when making access requests
  - PIBs help requesters locate their personal information across government
  - Institutions must describe their PIBs in publicly accessible directories

- **Response timeline - 30 days**: Government institutions must respond to
  access requests within 30 calendar days of receipt
  - Extensions permitted where searching records would unreasonably interfere
    with operations
  - Extensions permitted for consultation with other institutions or third
    parties
  - Requester must be notified of any extension and provided a new deadline
  - Privacy Commissioner may review complaints about delayed responses

- **Identity verification**: Institutions must verify the requester's identity
  before disclosing personal information
  - Reasonable steps to confirm identity required to prevent unauthorized
    disclosure
  - Methods may include government-issued identification, knowledge-based
    verification, or in-person attendance
  - Verification must balance security with accessibility
  - Over-verification can itself be a barrier to access rights

- **Right to information in intelligible form**: Information must be provided
  in a form the individual can understand
  - Where information is in coded format, institution must provide explanation
  - Technical or specialized terminology should be clarified
  - Translations may be required for official languages

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Individual requests their employment records from a federal department | Accept request in writing; respond within 30 days; verify identity before disclosure | Standard access request procedure under section 12 |
| Requester provides insufficient identification | Request additional verification; do not deny access solely on this basis | Verification is procedural, not a bar to the access right |
| Records contain both requester's and third party's personal information | Sever third party information before disclosure; provide requester's information | Section 19 exemption for third party information; access right applies to requester's own information |
| Response requires consultation with another department | Extend timeline; notify requester of extension and new deadline | Consultation extension permitted under section 9 |
| Requester alleges delayed response | Privacy Commissioner may investigate complaint | Commissioner oversight ensures compliance with timelines |
| Individual cannot locate appropriate PIB | Assist in identifying relevant information holdings; Info Source is the starting point | Institutions have duty to assist requesters |

## Gotchas

- **30 days is a maximum, not a target**: The Privacy Act requires response
  within 30 days. Institutions should respond as quickly as possible; 30 days
  is the outer limit absent a valid extension. Exam questions may test whether
  candidates understand this is a ceiling, not a standard processing time.

- **Extensions are not automatic**: Extensions must be justified by specific
  circumstances (volume of records, consultation requirements). An institution
  cannot routinely extend every request. The Privacy Commissioner scrutinizes
  extension practices.

- **Third party information must be severed, not refused entirely**: When
  records contain both the requester's and a third party's personal
  information, the institution must provide the requester's information after
  removing the third party content. Complete refusal is not appropriate.

- **Access is to personal information, not documents**: The right is to
  personal information about the requester, not necessarily complete documents.
  Non-personal information or exempt portions may be removed from disclosed
  records.

- **Provincial public bodies follow different timelines**: The 30-day timeline
  applies to federal institutions under the Privacy Act. Provincial FOIPPAs
  may specify different response periods. Exam questions may distinguish
  between federal and provincial requirements.

- **Oral requests are insufficient**: The Privacy Act requires written requests.
  Institutions may have duty to assist individuals in formulating requests,
  but the formal request must be in writing.

- **Fee prohibition for personal information**: Unlike access to general
  government records under the Access to Information Act, individuals cannot
  be charged fees for accessing their own personal information under the
  Privacy Act.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Response deadline | 30 calendar days | From receipt of request |
| Extension authority | Permitted with justification | Must notify requester |
| Request format | Written | Oral requests insufficient |
| Fees | Prohibited | No charge for personal information access |
| Third party information | Severed before disclosure | Section 19 exemption |
| Identity verification | Required | Reasonable steps before disclosure |
| Form of access | Intelligible | Must provide explanation of codes/terms |
| Oversight | Privacy Commissioner | May investigate complaints |

## Related topics

- **Objective 3.7 - Right of correction**: Paired with access right; individual
  may request correction after obtaining access to inaccurate information
- **Objective 3.5 - Collection and disclosure without consent**: Access rights
  operate alongside non-consent-based information handling
- **Section 19 exemptions for third party information**: Limits on access where
  disclosure would reveal third party personal information
- **Privacy Impact Assessments**: Should address how access requests will be
  handled for new programs
- **Info Source**: Published directory of government information holdings that
  supports access right exercise
- **PIPEDA access rights (Principle 9)**: Private-sector parallel with different
  procedural requirements; 30-day timeline also applies under PIPEDA

### 3.7: Understand the individual's right of correction to their personal information

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 3.7: Understand the individual's right of correction to their personal information

Source: CIPP/C Free Study Guide (IAPP 2025)

---

**Note**: The IAPP Free Study Guide is a preparation overview document that does not contain substantive content about the right of correction. This extract summarizes the objective scope based on the Body of Knowledge. For comprehensive exam preparation, consult the official textbook "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the Practitioner."

---

## Key concepts

- **Right of correction**: The individual's entitlement under public-sector privacy legislation to request that a government institution correct inaccurate or incomplete personal information held about them
- **Privacy Act foundation**: The right of correction is a core privacy principle under the federal Privacy Act, complementing the right of access
  - Individuals can request corrections to personal information held by federal government institutions
  - Institutions must respond to correction requests within prescribed timelines
- **Provincial/territorial equivalents**: Provincial Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy (FOIPP) Acts contain parallel correction rights for provincially-regulated public bodies

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Individual discovers factual error in government records | Submit formal correction request to the institution | Right of correction enables individuals to maintain accurate records |
| Institution disagrees with requested correction | Document the disagreement; individual may have recourse to Privacy Commissioner | Correction rights include mechanisms for dispute resolution |
| Correction request relates to opinion vs. fact | Distinguish between objective errors and subjective assessments | Right typically applies to factual inaccuracies, not differences of opinion |

## Gotchas

- **Correction vs. deletion**: The right of correction addresses inaccuracies; it is distinct from rights to have information deleted or erased
- **Scope limitations**: Correction rights may be denied in specific circumstances defined by statute
- **Burden of proof**: Individual typically must demonstrate the information is inaccurate or incomplete
- **Notation rights**: Where correction is refused, individuals may have the right to attach a notation or statement of disagreement to their file

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Response timeline | Varies by jurisdiction | Privacy Act specifies 30 days for access requests; correction timelines may differ |
| Exam weight | Domain III covers 11-15 questions | Right of correction is one component of competency III.A |

## Related topics

- **Right of access (3.7)**: Correction rights are typically exercised after accessing personal information to identify inaccuracies
- **Privacy Act principles (III.A)**: Correction is one of the foundational principles alongside consent, access, and data management
- **Commissioner oversight**: Privacy Commissioners may investigate complaints regarding denied correction requests
- **Provincial FOIPP Acts (III.C)**: Provincial legislation contains equivalent correction provisions for provincially-regulated public bodies

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 3.7: Understand the individual's right of correction to their personal information

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Right of correction under the Privacy Act (section 12(2))**: Individuals who
  have been granted access to their personal information may request correction
  of errors or omissions
  - Right is derivative of the access right; must first obtain access to
    identify inaccuracies
  - Applies to personal information held by federal government institutions
  - Purpose is to ensure government records about individuals are accurate and
    complete
  - Supports the accuracy principle in fair information practices

- **Scope of correction requests**: Corrections may address factual errors or
  incomplete information
  - Errors of fact: incorrect dates, misspelled names, wrong addresses
  - Omissions: missing information that provides necessary context
  - Does not extend to opinions, assessments, or subjective judgments
  - Government is not required to change professional opinions or evaluations
    simply because the individual disagrees

- **Annotation when correction is refused**: If a government institution refuses
  to make a requested correction, the individual may require a notation be
  attached to the information
  - Notation records that a correction was requested and refused
  - Notation must be included whenever the information is subsequently used or
    disclosed
  - Ensures third parties are aware the individual disputes the accuracy
  - Preserves the individual's perspective in the record

- **Response timeline - 30 days**: Same timeline as access requests under
  section 14
  - Institution must respond within 30 calendar days of receiving the
    correction request
  - Extensions may apply in limited circumstances
  - Requester must be notified if an extension is taken

- **Notification of third parties**: When a correction is made, the institution
  must notify third parties to whom the information was disclosed within the
  previous year
  - Ensures corrected information propagates to other users
  - Applies where disclosure occurred within the year prior to correction
  - Third parties should update their own records accordingly

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Individual discovers their date of birth is incorrect in government records | Accept correction request; update the record; notify third parties who received the information | Factual error must be corrected under section 12(2) |
| Individual disagrees with a performance evaluation in their personnel file | Refuse correction; offer annotation of dispute | Opinions and professional assessments are not subject to correction |
| Institution refuses correction and individual disputes the refusal | Attach notation to the record; include notation in future uses/disclosures | Annotation preserves individual's position when correction is refused |
| Correction is made to information previously shared with another department | Notify the other department of the correction | Third-party notification required for disclosures within prior year |
| Individual requests correction but has not yet obtained access | Advise individual to first submit access request | Correction right is derivative of access right; must first see the information |
| Correction request concerns information disclosed two years ago | Make the correction but no third-party notification required | Notification obligation only covers disclosures within prior year |

## Gotchas

- **Correction right requires prior access**: An individual cannot request
  correction of information they have not seen. The correction right under
  section 12(2) is triggered after access has been provided. Exam questions may
  test whether candidates understand this sequencing.

- **Opinions are not correctable**: The right of correction applies to factual
  errors and omissions, not to opinions, assessments, or evaluations. An
  individual who disagrees with a supervisor's performance review cannot demand
  the opinion be changed--only that an annotation be attached.

- **Annotation is not deletion**: When correction is refused, the individual's
  recourse is annotation, not removal of the disputed information. The original
  information remains, but with a notation indicating the individual's dispute.

- **30-day timeline applies to correction requests**: The same 30-day response
  period that applies to access requests also applies to correction requests.
  This is often tested in exam questions comparing different timelines.

- **Third-party notification has a one-year lookback**: The obligation to notify
  third parties applies only to disclosures made within the year prior to the
  correction. Disclosures older than one year do not trigger this notification
  requirement.

- **Provincial FOIPPAs may differ**: Provincial freedom of information and
  protection of privacy acts may have different correction procedures,
  timelines, or annotation requirements. Exam questions may distinguish federal
  from provincial regimes.

- **Correction includes addition of missing information**: The right covers
  omissions, not just errors. If relevant information is missing and its absence
  creates an inaccurate impression, the individual may request it be added.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Response deadline | 30 calendar days | Same as access request timeline |
| Prior access | Required | Must have obtained access before requesting correction |
| Correctable content | Facts and omissions | Opinions and assessments excluded |
| Remedy when refused | Annotation | Notation attached to disputed information |
| Annotation inclusion | All subsequent uses/disclosures | Notation must accompany information |
| Third-party notification | Required | For disclosures within prior year |
| Notification lookback | 1 year | Disclosures older than one year not covered |
| Oversight | Privacy Commissioner | May investigate complaints about refused corrections |

## Related topics

- **Objective 3.6 - Right of access**: Access is prerequisite to correction;
  individual must first see information before requesting changes
- **Accuracy principle (CSA Model Code Principle 6)**: Foundational principle
  that personal information should be accurate, complete, and up to date
- **Privacy Act section 6**: Government institutions must take reasonable steps
  to ensure personal information is accurate and complete before using it for
  administrative decisions
- **PIPEDA Principle 9 (Individual Access)**: Private-sector parallel includes
  right to challenge accuracy and have information amended
- **Provincial FOIPPA correction rights**: Provincial public-sector laws contain
  similar correction provisions with potential procedural differences
- **Personal Information Banks**: PIBs where corrections would be recorded and
  annotated if disputed

### 3.8: Follow storage, retention, and destruction requirements

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 3.8: Follow storage, retention, and destruction requirements

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Storage requirements under the Privacy Act (section 6(2))**: Personal
  information used for administrative purposes must be retained long enough for
  individuals to exercise their access and correction rights
  - Two-year minimum retention period after last use for administrative decisions
  - Applies to personal information used to make decisions about identifiable
    individuals
  - Purpose is to ensure individuals can challenge decisions based on their
    personal information
  - Storage location must be in Canada for information under federal control

- **Personal Information Banks (PIBs) and retention schedules**: Government
  institutions must maintain retention and disposal schedules for PIBs
  - Library and Archives Canada approves records disposition authorities (RDAs)
  - Each PIB must have an associated retention period and disposition action
  - Disposition may be destruction or transfer to Library and Archives Canada
  - Schedules ensure systematic management of personal information lifecycle

- **Security safeguards during storage (section 6(3))**: Government institutions
  must protect personal information during storage
  - Administrative, technical, and physical safeguards required
  - Protection level must be commensurate with sensitivity of information
  - Applies throughout the retention period until proper destruction
  - Treasury Board policies provide detailed security standards

- **Destruction requirements**: Personal information must be destroyed securely
  when retention period expires and no legal hold applies
  - Destruction must be complete and irreversible
  - Methods include shredding, secure electronic deletion, degaussing
  - Must ensure information cannot be reconstructed or retrieved
  - Documentation of destruction may be required for audit purposes

- **Accuracy principle (section 6(1))**: Personal information used for
  administrative purposes must be as accurate, up to date, and complete as
  possible
  - Accuracy requirement continues throughout retention period
  - Institutions must take reasonable steps to verify accuracy before use
  - Connects to individual's right of correction under section 12(2)

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Employment file used to deny promotion | Retain for minimum two years after decision | Section 6(2) requires retention to allow access and challenge |
| Benefit application processed and granted | Retain according to RDA for the program; minimum two years if used for decisions | Must allow individual to access and verify information used |
| Paper records containing personal information to be disposed | Use cross-cut shredding or secure destruction service | Physical destruction must prevent reconstruction |
| Electronic database records past retention period | Apply secure deletion methods; document destruction | Electronic destruction must be complete and verifiable |
| Individual requests access 18 months after decision | Provide access; records must still be available | Within mandatory two-year retention period |
| Legal hold issued due to litigation | Suspend destruction regardless of retention schedule | Legal obligations override normal disposition |
| Personal information transferred to contractor | Ensure contract requires equivalent protection and proper destruction | Accountability extends to third-party handling |
| Archives-worthy records with personal information | Transfer to Library and Archives Canada per RDA | Some records have historical value and are preserved |

## Gotchas

- **Two years is a minimum, not a maximum**: Section 6(2) requires at least two
  years of retention after information is used for administrative purposes.
  Program-specific requirements or records disposition authorities may mandate
  longer periods. Exam questions may test whether candidates understand this is
  a floor, not a ceiling.

- **"Administrative purpose" is the trigger**: The two-year retention
  requirement applies when personal information is used to make decisions about
  an identifiable individual. Information collected but not used for decisions
  may have different retention requirements.

- **Retention starts from last use, not collection**: The two-year clock begins
  when the information is last used for an administrative purpose, not when it
  was originally collected. Ongoing use resets the retention period.

- **Legal holds override retention schedules**: When litigation is anticipated
  or commenced, normal destruction schedules must be suspended. Destroying
  information subject to a legal hold is spoliation and may result in adverse
  inferences or sanctions.

- **Provincial public sector laws vary**: Federal Privacy Act requirements apply
  to federal institutions. Provincial and territorial FOIPPAs may specify
  different retention periods. Alberta, British Columbia, and other provinces
  have their own public sector retention frameworks.

- **Destruction must be complete**: Simply deleting files or placing paper in
  regular waste is insufficient. Secure destruction means the information cannot
  be recovered. Forensic recovery of improperly destroyed data can lead to
  privacy breaches.

- **Third-party storage requires equivalent protection**: When contractors or
  cloud providers store personal information on behalf of government, contracts
  must specify security standards, retention requirements, and proper
  destruction obligations. Accountability remains with the government
  institution.

- **Info Source must reflect accurate retention information**: The published
  description of PIBs in Info Source should include retention periods. Outdated
  or inaccurate Info Source entries can mislead individuals about their access
  rights.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Minimum retention for administrative use | 2 years | From last use for decision-making |
| Records disposition authority | Required | Library and Archives Canada approval |
| Storage location | Canada | Federal information under federal control |
| Security standard | Commensurate with sensitivity | Treasury Board policy guidance |
| Destruction method | Secure and irreversible | Shredding, degaussing, secure deletion |
| Third-party obligations | Contractual requirements | Must match government standards |
| Legal hold | Suspends destruction | Preservation required during litigation |
| Oversight | Privacy Commissioner | May audit retention and destruction practices |

## Related topics

- **Objective 3.6 - Right of access**: Retention ensures information remains
  available for access requests during the mandatory period
- **Objective 3.7 - Right of correction**: Individuals can only correct
  information that has been retained; destruction ends correction opportunity
- **Objective 3.9 - Privacy Impact Assessments**: PIAs should address planned
  retention periods and destruction methods for new programs
- **Section 6(1) accuracy requirement**: Stored information must remain accurate
  for its intended purpose throughout retention
- **Treasury Board Policy on Privacy Protection**: Detailed guidance on storage,
  retention, and disposal for federal institutions
- **Library and Archives Canada**: Approves records disposition authorities;
  may receive transfers of archival records
- **Provincial FOIPPA retention requirements**: Provincial public bodies follow
  provincial legislation which may differ from federal standards

### 3.9: Understand how and when to complete a Privacy Impact Assessment (PIA)

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 3.9: Understand how and when to complete a Privacy Impact Assessment (PIA)

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Privacy Impact Assessment (PIA) definition**: A systematic process to identify
  and evaluate the privacy risks of a program, system, or activity involving
  personal information
  - Proactive tool used before implementation, not after
  - Documents privacy implications and planned mitigations
  - Required for federal government institutions under Treasury Board policy
  - Provincial and territorial public bodies have similar requirements under
    FOIPPAs

- **When a PIA is required**: Treasury Board Directive on Privacy Impact
  Assessment mandates PIAs for new or substantially modified programs and
  activities
  - New programs or activities involving personal information
  - Substantial modifications to existing programs that change privacy impact
  - Implementation of new systems or technologies handling personal information
  - Contracting out activities involving personal information
  - Data matching or data sharing initiatives
  - Not required for routine administrative activities with no change in privacy
    risk

- **Timing of PIAs**: Must be completed early in the planning and design phase
  - Before personal information is collected or used
  - Early enough to influence design decisions
  - Updated when significant changes occur to the program or system
  - Allows privacy-by-design principles to be incorporated
  - Late PIAs lose the opportunity to prevent privacy issues

- **Treasury Board Directive on Privacy Impact Assessment**: Core federal policy
  governing PIA requirements
  - Applies to all federal government institutions subject to the Privacy Act
  - Requires submission of PIA summaries to Treasury Board Secretariat and OPC
  - Mandates publication of completed PIA summaries
  - Institutions must maintain PIA documentation
  - Deputy heads are accountable for ensuring PIAs are conducted

- **Provincial PIA requirements**: Most provincial and territorial FOIPPAs require
  or recommend PIAs for public bodies
  - Alberta FOIP Act has mandatory PIA requirements for common or integrated
    programs
  - British Columbia requires PIAs for data-linking initiatives
  - Ontario IPC provides PIA guidance for provincial institutions
  - Requirements vary; some jurisdictions mandate submission to Privacy
    Commissioner

- **Role of the Privacy Commissioner**: OPC reviews federal PIAs and provides
  guidance
  - Receives PIA summaries from federal institutions
  - May comment on privacy risks and recommend additional mitigations
  - Does not formally approve or reject PIAs
  - Provides guidance materials on conducting effective PIAs
  - Provincial commissioners have varying roles in PIA review

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| New digital service portal collecting citizen data | Conduct PIA before launch; submit summary to TBS and OPC | New program involving personal information requires PIA |
| Upgrading an existing HR system with new analytics features | Assess if changes substantially modify privacy impact; conduct PIA if so | Substantial modifications trigger PIA requirement |
| Contracting cloud provider to process benefit applications | Conduct PIA addressing third-party handling, data location, safeguards | Contracting out personal information processing requires PIA |
| Routine server maintenance on existing system | No new PIA required | No change to privacy impact; routine technical work |
| Data matching initiative between two departments | Conduct PIA covering both institutions' data, matching criteria, retention | Data matching is specifically identified as PIA trigger |
| Program launched without PIA identified during audit | Complete PIA immediately; document gaps and remediation | Retroactive PIA required even though opportunity for design input was lost |
| Expanding an existing program to new provinces | Assess if expansion changes privacy impact; update PIA if needed | Geographic expansion may change risk profile |

## Gotchas

- **PIAs are proactive, not reactive**: The primary purpose of a PIA is to
  identify privacy risks before they materialize. Conducting a PIA after
  implementation misses the opportunity to incorporate privacy-by-design. Exam
  questions may present scenarios where candidates must recognize that early PIA
  timing is essential.

- **Substantial modification is the trigger, not any modification**: Not every
  change requires a new PIA. The test is whether the modification substantially
  changes the privacy impact. Minor updates that do not affect what information
  is collected, how it is used, or who has access typically do not require a new
  PIA.

- **PIA summaries must be submitted and published**: Federal institutions must
  submit PIA summaries to both Treasury Board Secretariat and the OPC. Summaries
  are also published for transparency. Failure to submit or publish is a
  compliance gap.

- **OPC does not approve PIAs**: The Office of the Privacy Commissioner reviews
  and may comment on PIAs but does not formally approve or reject them.
  Institutions are responsible for their own PIA decisions. Comments from OPC
  are advisory.

- **Provincial requirements differ from federal**: Each province and territory has
  its own PIA framework for public bodies. Some mandate PIA submission to the
  provincial commissioner; others provide guidance without mandatory submission.
  Exam questions may test knowledge of these differences.

- **Third-party involvement requires PIA coverage**: When contractors or service
  providers will handle personal information, the PIA must address these
  arrangements. Accountability remains with the government institution even when
  processing is outsourced.

- **Data location matters**: PIAs should address where personal information will
  be stored and processed. If cloud services or foreign data centers are
  involved, the PIA must evaluate associated risks and any applicable data
  residency requirements.

- **PIAs are living documents**: When programs change significantly, PIAs should
  be updated. A PIA completed at program launch may become outdated as the
  program evolves. Periodic review ensures continued relevance.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Federal PIA authority | Treasury Board Directive on PIA | Mandatory for federal institutions |
| Timing | Before implementation | Early in planning/design phase |
| Submission | TBS and OPC | PIA summaries required |
| Publication | Required | Transparency obligation |
| OPC role | Advisory | Reviews but does not approve |
| Threshold | Substantial modification | Triggers new or updated PIA |
| Third-party coverage | Required | Contractor handling must be addressed |
| Provincial requirements | Vary by jurisdiction | Some mandatory, some guidance-based |
| Accountability | Deputy head | Institutional responsibility |

## Related topics

- **Objective 3.8 - Storage, retention, and destruction**: PIAs should address
  planned retention periods and destruction methods for new programs
- **Privacy by design**: PIA process operationalizes privacy-by-design principles
  by embedding privacy analysis in early development stages
- **Treasury Board Policy on Privacy Protection**: Overarching policy framework
  within which PIA directive operates
- **Personal Information Banks (PIBs)**: New programs requiring PIAs often result
  in new or modified PIB registrations
- **Provincial FOIPPA requirements**: Provincial public bodies follow provincial
  PIA frameworks which may differ from federal approach
- **Objective 3.10 - PIA report contents**: Companion objective covering what
  should be included in the PIA report itself

### 3.10: Know required contents of a PIA report

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 3.10: Know required contents of a PIA report

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Key concepts

- **Privacy Impact Assessment (PIA)**: A systematic evaluation of privacy risks
  for a government program, system, or activity
  - Falls under Domain III: Canadian Privacy Laws and Practices - Public Sector
  - Part of the "Conduct privacy impact assessments" competency area
  - Applies to federal government institutions under the Privacy Act framework

- **PIA as a public sector requirement**: The body of knowledge positions PIAs
  as a core public-sector practice
  - Distinct from private-sector privacy management approaches
  - Linked to Privacy Act principles (Objective 3.1)
  - Part of the 12% weighting for the Public-Sector domain

- **Report vs process distinction**: Objective 3.10 specifically addresses
  report contents, not the broader PIA process
  - When to complete a PIA is covered separately (Objective 3.9)
  - Report contents focus on what must be documented
  - Exam questions may test specific required elements

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Question asks what must be included in a PIA report | Identify core required elements: program description, PI inventory, risk analysis, mitigations | Report must document complete privacy assessment |
| Question distinguishes PIA process from PIA report | Focus on documentation requirements for report questions | Process (when/how) vs contents (what) are separate objectives |
| Federal vs private sector privacy assessment | PIA requirements apply to federal public sector | Private sector may do privacy assessments but federal PIA has specific requirements |

## Gotchas

- **PIA reports have specific required contents**: The objective tests knowledge
  of what elements must be documented in a PIA report. Generic "privacy
  assessment" knowledge is insufficient; candidates must know the specific
  components required for federal government PIAs.

- **Public sector focus**: This objective falls under Domain III (Public
  Sector). PIA report requirements derive from federal government policy,
  primarily the Treasury Board Directive on Privacy Impact Assessment. Do not
  confuse with private-sector privacy assessments under PIPEDA.

- **Companion to Objective 3.9**: Objectives 3.9 (how and when to complete a
  PIA) and 3.10 (required contents) are related but distinct. Exam questions
  may require distinguishing between process/timing questions and content
  requirements.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Domain | III - Public Sector | 12% exam weight |
| Governing legislation | Privacy Act | Federal public sector |
| Policy framework | Treasury Board directives | Defines PIA requirements |
| Related objective | 3.9 - How and when to complete a PIA | Process companion |

## Related topics

- **Objective 3.1 - Privacy Act principles**: Foundation for understanding what
  PIAs must assess compliance against
- **Objective 3.9 - How and when to complete a PIA**: Companion objective
  covering timing and triggers
- **Objective 3.8 - Storage, retention, and destruction of personal
  information**: PIA reports must address data lifecycle
- **Domain II - Private-Sector Laws**: Contrast federal PIA requirements with
  private-sector privacy practices

---

Note: The CIPP/C Free Study Guide provides minimal coverage of this objective,
listing only "Conduct privacy impact assessments" as a body of knowledge item
without elaboration. For detailed treatment of required PIA report contents,
including specific elements mandated by Treasury Board policy (program
description, personal information inventory, privacy analysis, risk assessment,
mitigation measures, retention provisions, access procedures, and governance
accountability), refer to the official CIPP/C textbook "Canadian Privacy: Data
Protection Law and Policy for the Practitioner."

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 3.10: Know required contents of a PIA report

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **PIA report purpose**: Documents the privacy analysis and risk assessment for a
  program, system, or activity
  - Provides a structured record of privacy considerations
  - Serves as evidence of due diligence and compliance
  - Informs stakeholders of privacy risks and mitigations
  - Supports accountability under the Privacy Act
  - Published summary promotes transparency with the public

- **Program or activity description**: Foundational section describing what is being
  assessed
  - Name and purpose of the program, system, or activity
  - Legal authority for the program (enabling legislation or policy)
  - Business objectives and intended outcomes
  - Description of personal information flows
  - Stakeholders involved (internal and external)

- **Personal information inventory**: Documents what personal information is
  involved
  - Types and categories of personal information collected
  - Sources of personal information (individuals, other institutions, third parties)
  - Volume and sensitivity of information
  - Personal Information Banks (PIBs) created or modified
  - Data elements mapped to collection purposes

- **Privacy analysis**: Core assessment of privacy implications
  - Necessity and proportionality of collection (minimum collection principle)
  - Legal basis for collection, use, and disclosure
  - Consistency with the purpose stated at time of collection
  - Alignment with the 10 CSA privacy principles
  - Compliance with Privacy Act requirements
  - Whether consent applies or exceptions are invoked

- **Risk identification and assessment**: Systematic identification of privacy risks
  - Risks to individuals if personal information is mishandled
  - Likelihood and severity of potential harms
  - Risks from data sharing, matching, or integration with other systems
  - Risks from third-party access or outsourced processing
  - Data residency and cross-border transfer risks
  - Emerging technology risks (AI, analytics, cloud)

- **Mitigation measures**: Planned controls and safeguards to address identified
  risks
  - Administrative safeguards (policies, procedures, training)
  - Technical safeguards (encryption, access controls, logging)
  - Physical safeguards (secure facilities, access restrictions)
  - Contractual protections with third parties
  - Privacy-enhancing technologies (PETs) where applicable
  - Residual risk after mitigations applied

- **Retention and disposal**: How long personal information will be kept and how it
  will be destroyed
  - Retention period justified by program requirements
  - Alignment with institutional retention schedules
  - Secure disposal methods (destruction, anonymization)
  - Third-party data return or destruction requirements

- **Access and correction procedures**: How individuals can exercise their rights
  - Procedures for individuals to access their personal information
  - Process for requesting corrections
  - Response timelines compliant with Privacy Act (30 days)
  - Notification of access and correction rights

- **Governance and accountability**: Who is responsible for privacy compliance
  - Designated officials accountable for the program
  - Role of ATIP coordinator or privacy office
  - Ongoing monitoring and review mechanisms
  - Complaint handling procedures
  - Reporting to senior management and deputy head

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| New benefit program collecting SIN, income data | PIA must identify sensitivity, justify necessity, document strong safeguards | Sensitive data requires thorough risk assessment |
| Cloud-based system with US data center | PIA must address data residency, foreign access risks, contractual protections | Cross-border storage creates additional privacy risks |
| Program sharing data with provincial agencies | PIA documents information sharing agreements, legal basis, limits on use | Data sharing requires explicit risk analysis |
| System using automated decision-making | PIA addresses transparency, accuracy, bias risks, human review options | AI/automation introduces specific privacy considerations |
| Contractor will process personal information | PIA includes third-party risks, contractual safeguards, oversight mechanisms | Third-party processing requires accountability coverage |
| Program involves data matching from multiple sources | PIA documents matching criteria, data quality, accuracy verification | Data matching increases re-identification and error risks |

## Gotchas

- **PIA report is not just a checklist**: A complete PIA requires substantive
  analysis, not merely filling in template fields. Exam questions may present
  scenarios where superficial compliance (e.g., listing data elements without
  analyzing sensitivity) is insufficient.

- **Legal authority must be documented**: The PIA must identify the legal authority
  for collecting personal information. Collection without proper authority is a
  fundamental compliance failure regardless of how well the PIA addresses other
  elements.

- **Mitigations must be specific and actionable**: Vague statements like "appropriate
  security measures will be implemented" are insufficient. The PIA should specify
  what controls will be used (e.g., AES-256 encryption, role-based access control).

- **Third-party arrangements require detail**: When contractors or service providers
  handle personal information, the PIA must address their obligations, oversight
  mechanisms, and contractual protections. Simply stating "contractor will protect
  data" is inadequate.

- **Data residency must be addressed**: If personal information will be stored or
  processed outside Canada, the PIA must evaluate foreign access risks and document
  any data residency requirements or exemptions.

- **Retention justification is required**: The PIA must justify retention periods
  based on program necessity, not just state a number of years. Retaining data
  longer than necessary is a privacy risk.

- **PIA summary differs from full PIA**: Federal institutions must submit a summary
  to TBS and OPC and publish it. The summary is a condensed version; the full PIA
  with detailed analysis is maintained internally.

- **PIBs must be addressed**: If the program creates new Personal Information Banks
  or modifies existing ones, the PIA should document this and ensure PIB
  registration is completed.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Required for | Federal government institutions | Under Treasury Board Directive |
| Core sections | Description, inventory, analysis, risks, mitigations | Minimum required elements |
| Submission | PIA summary to TBS and OPC | Summary, not full PIA |
| Publication | PIA summaries published | Transparency requirement |
| Retention schedule | Must align with institutional schedules | Library and Archives Canada requirements |
| Access timeline | 30 days | Privacy Act requirement |
| Third-party coverage | Must be included | If contractors handle PI |
| PIB documentation | Required | If new or modified PIBs |
| Review cycle | Ongoing | Update when program changes |

## Related topics

- **Objective 3.9 - How and when to complete a PIA**: Companion objective covering
  PIA timing, triggers, and process; 3.10 focuses on report contents
- **Personal Information Banks (PIBs)**: PIA often results in new or updated PIB
  registrations; PIA should document PIB implications
- **Treasury Board Directive on Privacy Impact Assessment**: Core policy governing
  federal PIA requirements and report contents
- **CSA Model Code 10 principles**: PIA analysis should assess compliance with all
  10 privacy principles
- **Objective 3.8 - Storage, retention, and destruction**: PIA retention section
  must align with these requirements
- **Privacy Act access and correction rights**: PIA must document procedures for
  individuals to exercise these rights

# 3.11: Know provincial and territorial public sector privacy legislation

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Key concepts

- **Provincial/territorial public sector privacy coverage**: The CIPP/C exam covers
  the applicability of Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy Acts across
  provinces and territories
  - Listed as objective III.C in the body of knowledge
  - Falls within Domain III: Canadian Privacy Laws and Practices - Public-Sector
  - Exam questions may address any topic within this domain

  not provide substantive coverage
  - Detailed statutory content requires the official textbook: "Canadian Privacy:
    Data Protection Law and Policy for the Practitioner"
  - The guide explicitly notes it does not cover specific provisions of each statute

## Common scenarios

| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Need to understand provincial FIPPA details | Consult official CIPP/C textbook | Free study guide is introductory only |
| Identifying which province's law applies | Determine which level of government controls the institution | Jurisdiction based on constitutional division of powers |

- **Textbook required**: The guide explicitly recommends studying "Canadian Privacy:
  Data Protection Law and Policy for the Practitioner" for comprehensive coverage.

  alongside provincial legislation in Domain III
