  with public sector institutions

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 3.11: Know provincial and territorial public sector privacy legislation

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge

## Key concepts

- **Provincial/territorial jurisdiction over public sector privacy**: Each province and
  territory has enacted its own public sector privacy legislation governing provincial
  and territorial government institutions
  - Authority derives from constitutional division of powers
  - Provincial governments have jurisdiction over their own institutions
  - Federal Privacy Act only applies to federal government institutions
  - Provincial acts typically combine access to information and privacy protection

- **Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy (FIPPA/FOIPPA) model**: Common
  legislative framework used across most provinces
  - British Columbia: Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy Act (FIPPA)
  - Alberta: Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy Act (FOIP Act)
  - Ontario: Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy Act (FIPPA)
  - Manitoba: Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy Act (FIPPA)
  - Saskatchewan: Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy Act (FOIP)
  - New Brunswick: Right to Information and Protection of Privacy Act (RTIPPA)
  - Nova Scotia: Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy Act (FOIPOP)
  - Prince Edward Island: Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy Act (FOIPP)
  - Newfoundland and Labrador: Access to Information and Protection of Privacy Act
    (ATIPPA, 2015)
  - Quebec: Act respecting Access to documents held by public bodies and the Protection
    of personal information
  - Yukon: Access to Information and Protection of Privacy Act (ATIPP)
  - Northwest Territories: Access to Information and Protection of Privacy Act (ATIPP)
  - Nunavut: Access to Information and Protection of Privacy Act (ATIPP)

- **Public bodies covered by provincial legislation**: Types of organizations subject
  to provincial public sector privacy laws
  - Provincial government ministries and departments
  - Provincial agencies, boards, and commissions
  - Crown corporations (provincial)
  - Municipalities and local governments
  - School boards and educational institutions
  - Public health authorities and hospitals (in some provinces)
  - Police services (provincial and municipal)
  - Universities and colleges (in most provinces)
  - Professional regulatory bodies (varies by province)

- **Key differences from federal Privacy Act**: Provincial legislation differs in
  structure and requirements
  - Often combines access to information with privacy protection in single statute
  - May have different timelines for responding to access requests
  - Different oversight models (commissioner powers vary)
  - May include additional rights not in federal Act (e.g., correction, annotation)
  - Some provinces have binding order-making power for commissioners
  - Breach notification requirements vary by province

- **Oversight models across provinces**: How privacy enforcement is structured
  - Some provinces have dedicated Privacy Commissioner
  - Some provinces have combined Information and Privacy Commissioner
  - Provinces without separate commissioner use Ombudsman model
  - Commissioner powers vary (recommendations vs. binding orders)
  - Alberta, British Columbia, and Ontario commissioners can issue binding orders
  - Some commissioners have audit and investigation powers

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| University handling student records in Ontario | FIPPA applies, not PIPEDA | Provincial public sector law governs public institutions |
| Municipal government in Alberta collecting resident data | Alberta FOIP Act applies | Municipalities are public bodies under provincial legislation |
| Federal agency operating in BC | Federal Privacy Act applies | Federal institution remains under federal jurisdiction |
| Provincial Crown corporation processing customer data | Provincial public sector law applies | Crown corporations are typically covered by provincial acts |
| Hospital in Ontario handling patient records | PHIPA (health) may apply alongside FIPPA | Health-specific legislation may take precedence or complement |
| Police service collecting personal information | Provincial act applies unless RCMP (federal) | Municipal/provincial police under provincial law |

## Gotchas

- **Federal vs. provincial jurisdiction confusion**: A public institution's
  jurisdiction depends on which level of government created and controls it, not where
  it operates. An RCMP officer in Ontario is under federal Privacy Act; a Toronto
  police officer is under Ontario FIPPA.

- **Universities are often provincial**: Despite receiving federal research funding,
  most universities are provincial public institutions governed by provincial privacy
  legislation, not PIPEDA.

- **Municipal governments are provincial**: Municipalities are creatures of provincial
  legislation and are governed by provincial public sector privacy laws, not federal
  law.

- **Commissioner powers vary significantly**: Not all provincial commissioners have
  the same enforcement powers. Some can only recommend; others (Alberta, BC, Ontario)
  can issue binding orders. Know which model applies.

- **Health sector may have separate legislation**: In provinces with specific health
  privacy legislation (Ontario PHIPA, Alberta HIA, etc.), health custodians may be
  governed by health-specific laws rather than or in addition to general public sector
  laws.

- **Crown corporations require careful analysis**: Whether a Crown corporation is
  federal or provincial determines which privacy law applies. Some Crown corporations
  also engage in commercial activities subject to private sector laws.

- **Ombudsman model differs from commissioner model**: In provinces using an Ombudsman
  for privacy oversight, the enforcement approach and available remedies may differ
  from dedicated commissioner offices.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Provinces with FIPPA/FOIPPA-style legislation | All 10 provinces | Common framework, different names |
| Territories with public sector privacy laws | All 3 | Yukon, NWT, Nunavut each have ATIPP |
| Commissioners with binding order power | AB, BC, ON | Others have recommendation-only authority |
| Provinces using Ombudsman model | Varies | No dedicated privacy commissioner |
| Access request response time | Typically 30 days | Varies by province, extensions may apply |
| Municipal coverage | All provinces | Municipalities under provincial legislation |
| University coverage | Most provinces | Generally treated as public bodies |

## Related topics

- **Objective 3.1 - Federal Privacy Act**: The federal counterpart; understanding
  differences between federal and provincial public sector regimes is essential
- **Objective 3.12 - Which public bodies fall under Privacy Act vs. provincial law**:
  Companion objective on jurisdictional determination
- **Division of powers**: Constitutional basis for provincial vs. federal jurisdiction
- **Provincial private sector laws**: Some provinces have both public and private
  sector privacy legislation (AB, BC, QC)
- **Health sector legislation**: Provinces with health-specific laws that may overlap
  with or supersede general public sector laws

---

## Domain 4: Canadian Privacy Laws and Practices - Health Sector

**Weight: 11%**

### 4.1: Know what defines personal health information (PHI)

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 4.1: Know what defines personal health information (PHI)

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Personal health information (PHI)**: Information about an identifiable
  individual that relates to their physical or mental health, health care
  history, health services provided, or health care payments. PHI receives
  heightened protection under provincial health privacy legislation due to its
  sensitive nature.

- **Core elements of PHI across provincial health acts**:
  - Information about the individual's physical or mental health condition
  - Health care history and health services provided to the individual
  - Identification of a health care provider who provided services
  - Payments or eligibility for health care coverage
  - Donation of body parts or bodily substances
  - Health care registration information
  - Health numbers or other health identifiers

- **Provincial health privacy acts**: Canada has no federal health privacy law.
  Each province has enacted its own health privacy legislation:
  - Ontario: Personal Health Information Protection Act (PHIPA)
  - Alberta: Health Information Act (HIA)
  - Manitoba: Personal Health Information Act (PHIA)
  - Saskatchewan: Health Information Protection Act (HIPA)
  - New Brunswick: Personal Health Information Privacy and Access Act (PHIPAA)
  - Newfoundland and Labrador: Personal Health Information Act (PHIA)
  - Nova Scotia: Personal Health Information Act (PHIA)

- **Health information custodian**: The entity responsible for PHI under
  provincial health acts. Custodians include:
  - Health care practitioners (physicians, nurses, pharmacists)
  - Hospitals and health facilities
  - Laboratories
  - Pharmacies
  - Long-term care facilities
  - Health care agencies and government health ministries

- **Identifiability requirement**: Information qualifies as PHI only when it
  relates to an identifiable individual. De-identified or anonymized health
  data that cannot reasonably be linked back to an individual is not PHI.

- **Distinction from general personal information**: PHI is a subset of personal
  information with specific statutory treatment. While PIPEDA or provincial
  private sector laws govern general personal information, health privacy acts
  apply specifically to PHI held by health information custodians.

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Hospital records containing diagnosis and treatment | Treat as PHI under applicable provincial health act | Medical records are core PHI regardless of format |
| Employer obtains doctor's note for sick leave | General personal information under PIPEDA or provincial private sector law | Employer is not a health information custodian |
| Pharmacy prescription records | PHI under provincial health act | Pharmacies are health information custodians; prescriptions reveal health conditions |
| Health insurer claim records | May be PHI if insurer is designated custodian; otherwise governed by insurance privacy rules | Varies by province; some include insurers as custodians |
| Research using de-identified health data | Not PHI if properly de-identified | Identifiability is required for PHI status |
| Fitness tracker data held by technology company | Not PHI; general personal information | Technology company is not a health information custodian |
| Mental health counseling records | PHI under provincial health act | Mental health is explicitly included in PHI definitions |

## Gotchas

- **Not all health-related information is PHI.** Information only qualifies as
  PHI when held by a health information custodian in the health care context.
  The same information (e.g., a diagnosis) held by an employer or insurer may
  be governed by different legislation.

- **Provincial definitions vary in scope.** Each provincial health act has its
  own PHI definition. Some are broader (including billing and payment
  information) while others are narrower. Know the specific elements covered by
  the applicable provincial act.

- **Health care providers vs. health information custodians.** Not all health
  care providers are custodians under every provincial act. The designation
  depends on the provincial legislation and can affect which law applies.

- **Registration information is PHI.** Information collected when an individual
  registers for health services (name, address, health card number) is PHI
  because it reveals the individual's interaction with the health system.

- **Genetic information receives special treatment.** Most provincial health
  acts explicitly include genetic information as PHI, and some impose
  additional restrictions on its collection, use, and disclosure.

- **Deceased individuals may be covered.** Some provincial health acts protect
  PHI of deceased individuals for a specified period. The protection may pass
  to executors or personal representatives.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Federal health privacy law | None | Health privacy is provincially regulated |
| Ontario health privacy act | PHIPA | Personal Health Information Protection Act, 2004 |
| Alberta health privacy act | HIA | Health Information Act |
| Manitoba health privacy act | PHIA | Personal Health Information Act |
| Saskatchewan health privacy act | HIPA | Health Information Protection Act |
| Health number as PHI | Yes | Provincial health card numbers are explicitly PHI |
| Mental health records | PHI | Included in all provincial PHI definitions |
| Genetic information | PHI | Explicitly included in most provincial acts |

## Related topics

- **Circle of care and implied consent**: PHI can flow within the circle of care
  based on implied consent, which differs from express consent requirements for
  other disclosures (Objective 4.6)
- **Substantially similar health privacy laws**: Provinces with health acts
  deemed substantially similar to PIPEDA for health sector purposes
  (Objective 4.0)
- **Collection, use, and disclosure of PHI**: Understanding what constitutes PHI
  is prerequisite to applying rules for handling it (Objective 4.2)
- **Safeguarding and breach protocols**: PHI breach notification requirements
  are specific to health privacy legislation (Objective 4.7)

### 4.2: Understand Ontario's Personal Health Information Protection Act (PHIPA)

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 4.2: Understand Ontario's Personal Health Information Protection Act (PHIPA)

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Personal Health Information Protection Act (PHIPA)**: Ontario's health privacy
  legislation, enacted in 2004. PHIPA governs the collection, use, and disclosure
  of personal health information (PHI) by health information custodians in
  Ontario. It was the first comprehensive health privacy statute in Canada.

- **Health information custodian (HIC)**: The entity accountable for PHI under
  PHIPA. Includes:
  - Health care practitioners (physicians, nurses, pharmacists, dentists)
  - Hospitals, psychiatric facilities, and independent health facilities
  - Long-term care homes and retirement homes providing care services
  - Community care access corporations and community health centers
  - Laboratories and specimen collection centers
  - Pharmacies
  - Ambulance services
  - The Ministry of Health and Long-Term Care

- **Agent**: A person authorized by a HIC to collect, use, or disclose PHI on the
  custodian's behalf. Agents must comply with PHIPA and the custodian's policies.
  The custodian remains accountable for agents' actions.

- **Circle of care**: Health care providers involved in an individual's treatment.
  PHI can flow within the circle of care based on implied consent unless the
  individual has expressly withheld or withdrawn consent (lock box).

- **Lock box provision**: An individual's right to expressly withhold or withdraw
  consent for disclosure of PHI within the circle of care. Once invoked, other
  providers cannot access that information without express consent, even for
  treatment purposes.

- **Substantially similar status**: PHIPA is deemed substantially similar to
  PIPEDA for health information in Ontario, meaning PHIPA applies instead of
  PIPEDA to Ontario health information custodians.

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Family physician refers patient to specialist | Implied consent permits disclosure | Both are within circle of care for treatment |
| Patient invokes lock box for psychiatric records | Require express consent before disclosure | Lock box overrides implied consent |
| Hospital discloses PHI for quality improvement | Permitted without consent if de-identified or necessary for specific purpose | Section 37 permits certain internal uses |
| Researcher requests access to PHI | Require REB approval and consent or REB waiver with strict conditions | Research use requires ethics approval |
| Patient requests copy of medical records | Provide access within 30 days | Individuals have right of access under s. 52 |
| Third-party company requests PHI for marketing | Do not disclose without express consent | Marketing is outside permitted purposes |
| Electronic health record vendor accesses PHI | Vendor acts as electronic service provider; must comply with PHIPA requirements | Part V.1 governs electronic health records |
| PHI breach occurs affecting multiple patients | Notify affected individuals, IPC, and contain breach | Mandatory breach notification under s. 12 |

## Gotchas

- **Implied consent has limits.** Implied consent applies only within the circle
  of care for treatment purposes. Disclosure for other purposes (research,
  marketing, insurance) requires express consent or a statutory exception.

- **Consent can be withdrawn at any time.** An individual can withdraw consent
  prospectively. The custodian must stop using or disclosing PHI except where
  required by law.

- **Substitute decision-makers.** If an individual is incapable of consenting,
  PHIPA specifies a hierarchy of substitute decision-makers. The Health Care
  Consent Act, 1996 governs capacity determinations.

- **Retention period is 10 years minimum.** PHIPA requires custodians to retain
  PHI for at least 10 years after the last entry (or 10 years after the
  individual turns 18 if a minor). Regulations may specify different periods.

- **Access requests have a 30-day deadline.** Custodians must respond to access
  requests within 30 days. Extensions up to an additional 30 days are permitted
  in limited circumstances with notice.

- **Fees must be reasonable.** Custodians may charge a fee for access requests,
  but it must be reasonable and cannot exceed the prescribed amount. Fees cannot
  be a barrier to access.

- **IPC has order-making power.** Unlike some provincial commissioners, Ontario's
  Information and Privacy Commissioner can issue binding orders under PHIPA, not
  just recommendations.

- **Electronic health records have additional rules.** Part V.1 of PHIPA governs
  electronic health records, health information networks, and electronic service
  providers with specific consent and audit requirements.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Enactment date | November 1, 2004 | First comprehensive health privacy act in Canada |
| Oversight body | Information and Privacy Commissioner of Ontario (IPC) | Has order-making authority |
| Access request response | 30 days | Extension up to 60 days total with notice |
| Minimum retention period | 10 years | From last entry or age 18 for minors |
| Express consent required for | Fundraising, marketing, research (unless waived) | Implied consent insufficient |
| Breach notification | Mandatory | To affected individuals and IPC at first reasonable opportunity |
| Maximum administrative penalty | $500,000 (individuals), $1,000,000 (organizations) | Per PHIPA s. 69 |
| Substantially similar to PIPEDA | Yes | Health sector in Ontario governed by PHIPA |

## Related topics

- **Personal health information definition**: Understanding what constitutes PHI
  is prerequisite to applying PHIPA rules (Objective 4.1)
- **Health privacy laws across provinces**: PHIPA is one of several provincial
  health acts; compare with Alberta HIA, Manitoba PHIA (Objective 4.3)
- **Circle of care and implied consent**: Core PHIPA concept for treatment-
  related disclosures (Objective 4.4)
- **Collection, use, and disclosure rules**: PHIPA's permitted purposes and
  exceptions (Objective 4.5)
- **Breach notification protocols**: PHIPA-specific requirements for health
  sector breaches (Objective 4.8)

### 4.3: Know health privacy laws across provinces and territories

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 4.3: Know health privacy laws across provinces and territories

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Source limitation

The IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide provides only a high-level overview of exam
topics without substantive content on provincial health privacy legislation.
This extract documents what the source contains.

## Key concepts

- **Provincial health privacy statutes**: The guide identifies four key
  provincial health privacy laws that candidates should know:
  - Ontario's Personal Health Information Protection Act (PHIPA)
  - Alberta's Health Information Act (HIA)
  - Manitoba's Personal Health Information Act (PHIA)
  - Saskatchewan's Health Information Protection Act (HIPA)

- **Exam domain weight**: The health sector domain represents 11% of the CIPP/C
  examination, indicating moderate coverage within the overall certification.

- **Body of knowledge objective**: The official objective states candidates
  should "Understand when to apply the various health privacy acts of the
  provinces and territories" -- focusing on applicability rather than detailed
  statutory provisions.

## Common scenarios

| Scenario                              | Correct approach                           | Why                                        |
| ------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------ |
| Health data in Ontario                | Apply PHIPA                                | Provincial health law takes precedence     |
| Health data in Alberta                | Apply HIA                                  | Province-specific health legislation       |
| Health data in Manitoba               | Apply PHIA                                 | Manitoba's dedicated health privacy law    |
| Health data in Saskatchewan           | Apply HIPA                                 | Saskatchewan's health information statute  |

## Gotchas

- **Source depth**: The free study guide does not explain the scope, consent
  requirements, or enforcement mechanisms of each provincial law. Candidates
  must consult the official textbook or other sources for substantive content.

- **Incomplete coverage**: The guide mentions only four provincial statutes.
  Other provinces and territories may have relevant legislation or rely on
  general public-sector privacy laws for health information.

## Related topics

- PIPEDA: Federal private-sector law that may apply to health information in
  provinces without substantially similar provincial legislation
- Provincial private-sector laws: Alberta's PIPA, BC's PIPA, and Quebec's
  private-sector law may intersect with health privacy requirements
- Public-sector privacy laws: Provincial freedom of information and protection
  of privacy acts may govern health information held by government institutions

---

Note: For exam preparation on this objective, candidates should consult
"Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the Practitioner" or the
more detailed CIPP/C Body of Knowledge materials, which provide the substantive
content required to understand when each provincial health privacy act applies.

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 4.3: Know health privacy laws across provinces and territories

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Provincial jurisdiction over health privacy**: Canada has no federal health
  privacy legislation. Each province has enacted its own health-sector-specific
  privacy statute governing personal health information (PHI) held by health
  information custodians. Understanding the applicable provincial law is
  essential for health sector compliance.

- **Provincial health privacy statutes**: Major provincial health acts include:
  - Ontario: Personal Health Information Protection Act (PHIPA), 2004
  - Alberta: Health Information Act (HIA), 2001
  - Manitoba: Personal Health Information Act (PHIA), 1997
  - Saskatchewan: Health Information Protection Act (HIPA), 1999
  - New Brunswick: Personal Health Information Privacy and Access Act (PHIPAA),
    2009
  - Newfoundland and Labrador: Personal Health Information Act (PHIA), 2008
  - Nova Scotia: Personal Health Information Act (PHIA), 2010

- **Provinces without dedicated health acts**: British Columbia, Quebec, and
  Prince Edward Island do not have standalone health privacy legislation.
  In these jurisdictions, PHI is governed by general private sector or public
  sector privacy laws:
  - British Columbia: Personal Information Protection Act (PIPA) and Freedom of
    Information and Protection of Privacy Act (FIPPA)
  - Quebec: Act Respecting the Protection of Personal Information in the Private
    Sector (Quebec Private Sector Act) and Act Respecting Access to Documents
    Held by Public Bodies
  - PEI: Freedom of Information and Protection of Privacy Act (FOIPP)

- **Territories**: Yukon, Northwest Territories, and Nunavut do not have
  health-specific privacy legislation. Health information is governed by access
  and privacy legislation or, for private sector activities, PIPEDA may apply.

- **Alberta Health Information Act (HIA)**: Governs PHI held by health
  custodians including regional health authorities, physicians, pharmacists, and
  Alberta Health. HIA emphasizes individual access rights and requires consent
  for most uses and disclosures outside treatment purposes. The Office of the
  Information and Privacy Commissioner of Alberta (OIPC) provides oversight.

- **Manitoba Personal Health Information Act (PHIA)**: Among the earliest
  provincial health acts (1997). Covers trustees including health professionals,
  hospitals, and government bodies. Manitoba PHIA requires consent for
  collection, use, and disclosure with exceptions for treatment and public
  health purposes.

- **Saskatchewan Health Information Protection Act (HIPA)**: Applies to
  trustees (health facilities, government institutions, health professionals).
  HIPA contains strong consent requirements and permits implied consent within
  the circle of care. Administered by Saskatchewan's Information and Privacy
  Commissioner.

- **Atlantic provinces health acts**: New Brunswick (PHIPAA), Newfoundland and
  Labrador (PHIA), and Nova Scotia (PHIA) each govern PHI held by health
  custodians with variations in definitions, consent requirements, and oversight
  mechanisms.

- **Substantially similar status**: Ontario's PHIPA, Alberta's HIA, Manitoba's
  PHIA, and New Brunswick's PHIPAA have been deemed substantially similar to
  PIPEDA for health sector purposes. This means PIPEDA does not apply to health
  information custodians in those provinces.

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Alberta physician shares PHI with hospital | Follow Alberta HIA requirements | Both are custodians under HIA; consent or exception required |
| BC clinic handles patient records | Apply BC PIPA (private) or FIPPA (public) | BC has no standalone health privacy act |
| Manitoba lab discloses test results to physician | Implied consent within circle of care | PHIA permits treatment-related disclosure |
| Saskatchewan researcher requests PHI access | Require ethics approval and consent or waiver | HIPA has specific research provisions |
| Quebec hospital manages patient data | Follow Quebec public sector privacy law | No dedicated health privacy act |
| PEI private clinic collects PHI | PIPEDA may apply | No provincial health or private sector act |
| Ontario and Alberta institutions share PHI | Comply with both PHIPA and HIA | Cross-provincial transfer requires meeting both laws |
| NWT health center processes patient records | Follow territorial access and privacy legislation | No territory-specific health act |

## Gotchas

- **Substantially similar does not mean identical.** While several health acts
  are deemed substantially similar to PIPEDA, each has distinct provisions.
  Consent requirements, access rights, breach notification rules, and oversight
  mechanisms differ across provinces.

- **Custodian definitions vary.** Each provincial act defines health information
  custodian (or trustee) differently. Some include health insurers and government
  agencies; others are limited to direct care providers. Know who qualifies as
  a custodian under each act.

- **Circle of care scope differs.** Most health acts permit implied consent
  within the circle of care, but the definition of who is included varies. Some
  provinces restrict the circle more narrowly than others.

- **Breach notification requirements are not uniform.** Mandatory breach
  notification exists in some provinces (Ontario PHIPA) but not all. Know the
  notification requirements for each applicable jurisdiction.

- **Research use provisions vary.** Each provincial act has different rules for
  research access to PHI. Some require ethics board approval with consent
  waiver; others impose stricter consent requirements.

- **BC, Quebec, and PEI require different analysis.** Without standalone health
  acts, practitioners must apply general privacy legislation. This can result
  in different obligations than provinces with dedicated health statutes.

- **Cross-border transfers create complexity.** When PHI moves between
  provinces, organizations must comply with multiple health privacy regimes.
  There is no harmonized interprovincial framework.

- **Federal institutions remain under Privacy Act.** Federal health-related
  bodies (e.g., Health Canada, Public Health Agency of Canada) are not subject
  to provincial health acts; the federal Privacy Act governs.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Ontario health act | PHIPA (2004) | Substantially similar to PIPEDA |
| Alberta health act | HIA (2001) | Substantially similar to PIPEDA |
| Manitoba health act | PHIA (1997) | Substantially similar to PIPEDA |
| Saskatchewan health act | HIPA (1999) | Oversight by provincial IPC |
| New Brunswick health act | PHIPAA (2009) | Substantially similar to PIPEDA |
| Newfoundland health act | PHIA (2008) | Overseen by provincial IPC |
| Nova Scotia health act | PHIA (2010) | Overseen by provincial IPC |
| BC health act | None | PIPA/FIPPA apply |
| Quebec health act | None | Provincial private/public sector acts apply |
| PEI health act | None | FOIPP applies; PIPEDA for commercial activity |
| Territories health acts | None | Access legislation and/or PIPEDA apply |

## Related topics

- **Personal health information definition**: Understanding PHI is prerequisite
  to determining which provincial act applies (Objective 4.1)
- **Ontario PHIPA**: Detailed provisions of Ontario's health privacy act
  (Objective 4.2)
- **Consent requirements in health sector**: How consent obligations differ
  across provincial health acts (Objective 4.4)
- **Collection, use, and disclosure of PHI**: Provincial variations in handling
  rules (Objective 4.5)
- **Breach notification protocols**: Health-sector-specific breach requirements
  by province (Objective 4.8)

### 4.4: Understand consent requirements specific to health sector

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 4.4: Understand consent requirements specific to health sector

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide (Effective September 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Health information privacy statutes**: The study guide identifies four
  provincial health privacy laws that CIPP/C candidates must understand:
  - Ontario's Personal Health Information Protection Act (PHIPA)
  - Alberta's Health Information Act (HIA)
  - Manitoba's Personal Health Information Act (PHIA)
  - Saskatchewan's Health Information Protection Act (HIPA)

- **Domain IV focus**: The body of knowledge outline lists Domain IV as
  "Canadian Privacy Laws and Practices - Health Sector" with the objective to
  "Understand when to apply the various health privacy acts of the provinces
  and territories."

## Source limitations

The free study guide is an exam overview document, not instructional material.
It identifies the statutes covered by the CIPP/C exam but does not provide
substantive content on health sector consent requirements. For detailed
coverage of:

- Express versus implied consent
- Circle of care concept
- Consent for minors and incapable individuals
- Consent withdrawal procedures
- Statutory exceptions to consent

Consult the official textbook: "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection Law and
Policy for the Practitioner" or the IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge.

## Related topics

- **Provincial health privacy laws**: Knowing which statute applies is
  prerequisite to understanding consent requirements (Objective 4.3)
- **Collection, use, and disclosure of PHI**: Consent rules govern how PHI may
  be handled under provincial health laws (Objective 4.5)

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 4.4: Understand consent requirements specific to health sector

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Meaningful consent for PHI**: Health privacy legislation requires consent that
  is informed, voluntary, and specific to the purpose. The individual must
  understand what PHI is being collected, why it is needed, how it will be used,
  and to whom it may be disclosed. Consent obtained through coercion, deception,
  or without adequate information is not valid.

- **Express consent**: An explicit, affirmative indication of agreement, either
  verbal or written. Required for:
  - Disclosure of PHI outside the circle of care
  - Use of PHI for purposes unrelated to treatment (e.g., marketing, research)
  - Disclosure to third parties such as insurers, employers, or legal
    representatives
  - Secondary uses not contemplated at the time of collection

- **Implied consent**: Consent inferred from an individual's actions or the
  circumstances. In health settings, implied consent operates within the circle
  of care:
  - When an individual seeks health care, consent to collect and use PHI for
    treatment purposes is implied
  - Disclosure to other health care providers involved in the individual's care
    is permitted without express consent
  - The individual must have a reasonable expectation that their information
    will be shared for treatment purposes

- **Circle of care**: The group of health care providers who require PHI to
  provide treatment to an individual. Key characteristics:
  - Members must be directly involved in the individual's health care
  - Includes physicians, nurses, specialists, pharmacists, and other providers
    delivering or supporting the individual's treatment
  - Does not include administrative staff, researchers, or health care providers
    not involved in that individual's care
  - The individual can restrict the circle of care by withdrawing consent for
    specific providers or types of information

- **Consent withdrawal**: Individuals have the right to withdraw consent at any
  time. Upon withdrawal:
  - The custodian must cease the collection, use, or disclosure for which
    consent was withdrawn
  - Withdrawal does not apply retroactively to uses or disclosures already made
  - The custodian may continue using PHI where required by law or where a
    consent exception applies

- **Consent for minors and incapable individuals**: Provincial health acts
  specify rules for substitute consent:
  - Parents or guardians typically consent for minor children
  - Mature minors (capable of understanding) may consent for themselves
  - Substitute decision-makers consent for individuals who are incapable
  - The hierarchy of substitute decision-makers varies by province

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Patient visits physician for diagnosis | Implied consent for collection and use | Treatment context permits implied consent within circle of care |
| Physician refers patient to specialist | Implied consent for disclosure to specialist | Specialist is within circle of care |
| Hospital shares PHI with patient's employer | Express consent required | Employer is outside circle of care |
| Pharmacist fills prescription from physician | Implied consent for disclosure | Pharmacist is within circle of care for treatment |
| Researcher requests access to patient records | Express consent or research ethics approval with waiver | Research is outside treatment purposes |
| Patient requests information not be shared with family member | Custodian must respect restriction | Patients can limit disclosures within circle of care |
| Emergency treatment when patient is unconscious | Consent exception applies | Health acts permit treatment without consent in emergencies |
| Insurer requests medical records for claim | Express consent required | Insurer is outside circle of care |
| Patient refuses to consent to collection | Custodian may refuse treatment if PHI is necessary | Consent to collect is required, but can affect service |

## Gotchas

- **Implied consent only applies within the circle of care.** Disclosure to
  anyone not directly involved in the individual's health care requires express
  consent or a statutory exception. Administrative staff processing records do
  not fall within the circle of care for consent purposes.

- **Circle of care is not unlimited.** Just because a provider works in health
  care does not mean they are in every patient's circle of care. The provider
  must be directly involved in delivering or supporting that individual's
  treatment.

- **Consent to treatment is not consent to research.** A patient consenting to
  receive care is not thereby consenting to have their PHI used for research.
  Research use requires separate express consent or an approved research ethics
  board waiver.

- **Individuals can restrict implied consent.** A patient may instruct their
  physician not to share information with a specific specialist or provider.
  The custodian must respect this restriction even within the circle of care.

- **Lack of consent does not always block collection.** Provincial health acts
  contain exceptions permitting collection, use, or disclosure without consent
  for purposes such as public health reporting, mandatory disease notification,
  court orders, or preventing serious harm.

- **Substitute consent has a hierarchy.** When an individual cannot consent,
  provincial laws specify who may act as substitute decision-maker. The order
  of priority (e.g., guardian, spouse, adult child) differs by province.

- **Oral consent may be valid for express consent.** While written consent
  provides better documentation, most provincial health acts permit oral express
  consent for PHI disclosure. However, custodians should document oral consent
  in the health record.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Implied consent scope | Circle of care only | Does not extend to disclosures outside treatment |
| Express consent form | Written or oral | Written preferred for documentation purposes |
| Consent withdrawal | Prospective only | Does not undo prior lawful uses or disclosures |
| Minor consent | Varies by province | Mature minor may consent independently |
| Research consent | Express required | Or research ethics board waiver |
| Emergency exception | Applies when patient incapable | Treatment permitted without consent in emergencies |

## Related topics

- **Personal health information definition**: Understanding what constitutes PHI
  is prerequisite to determining when health-sector consent rules apply
  (Objective 4.1)
- **Collection, use, and disclosure of PHI**: Consent requirements interact with
  rules governing how PHI may be handled (Objective 4.5)
- **Access and correction rights**: Individuals may access their PHI and request
  corrections regardless of consent status (Objective 4.6)
- **Provincial health privacy laws**: Consent requirements vary by province;
  know which law applies (Objective 4.3)
- **Breach notification**: Consent is not required to notify individuals of a
  privacy breach (Objective 4.8)

### 4.5: Apply collection, use, and disclosure rules for PHI

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 4.5: Apply collection, use, and disclosure rules for PHI

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Necessity principle**: Provincial health privacy laws limit collection, use, and
  disclosure of PHI to what is reasonably necessary for the purpose. Custodians
  must not collect more PHI than required, use it for unrelated purposes, or
  disclose it beyond what the purpose demands.

- **Collection rules**: PHI may only be collected:
  - Directly from the individual, unless an exception applies
  - For a purpose that is lawful and relates to the custodian's functions
  - In an amount no greater than necessary for the identified purpose
  - With appropriate consent or under a statutory exception

- **Use rules**: Once collected, PHI may only be used:
  - For the purpose for which it was collected
  - For a purpose the individual would reasonably expect
  - With consent for a new purpose
  - Under a statutory exception (e.g., preventing serious harm, legal
    proceedings, health system management)

- **Disclosure rules**: Disclosure of PHI to third parties requires:
  - Express consent from the individual, or
  - Implied consent within the circle of care for treatment purposes, or
  - A statutory exception permitting disclosure without consent

- **Statutory disclosure exceptions**: Provincial health acts permit disclosure
  without consent for:
  - Mandatory public health reporting (communicable diseases, child abuse)
  - Court orders and subpoenas
  - Preventing or reducing serious risk of harm to the individual or others
  - Health system planning, research with ethics board approval
  - Proceedings before a tribunal or regulatory body
  - Disclosure to a successor custodian (e.g., practice sale)

- **Limiting disclosure**: When disclosure is permitted, custodians must disclose
  only the minimum PHI necessary. This includes:
  - De-identifying information where feasible
  - Providing only the specific records relevant to the request
  - Using aggregate data rather than individual records for planning or research

- **Third-party processors**: When PHI is transferred to a third party for
  processing (e.g., transcription services, cloud storage), the custodian remains
  accountable. Requirements include:
  - Written agreement specifying permitted uses
  - Security and confidentiality obligations
  - Restrictions on further disclosure
  - Return or destruction of PHI upon contract termination

- **Retention requirements**: PHI must be retained for the period required by law
  or professional standards. Common requirements include:
  - Hospital records: typically 10 years from last entry
  - Physician records: typically 10 years after last visit, or 10 years after
    patient reaches age of majority
  - After retention period expires, PHI must be securely destroyed

- **Destruction standards**: When PHI is no longer required, it must be destroyed
  in a manner that prevents reconstruction:
  - Paper records: cross-cut shredding or incineration
  - Electronic records: secure deletion, degaussing, or physical destruction
  - Third-party destruction: obtain certificate of destruction

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Physician collects full medical history for annual checkup | Permitted; collection is necessary for care | Direct collection for treatment purpose |
| Hospital discloses patient records to insurer | Requires express consent | Insurer is outside circle of care |
| Lab sends test results to referring physician | Implied consent applies | Physician is within circle of care |
| Public health authority requests HIV test results | Disclosure required by law | Mandatory disease reporting exception |
| Researcher requests identifiable patient data | Requires consent or REB-approved waiver | Research is not treatment |
| Hospital provides aggregate data for health planning | Permitted without consent | Disclosure for health system management |
| Clinic outsources billing to third-party processor | Written agreement required | Custodian remains accountable |
| Police request patient records without warrant | Cannot disclose without consent or court order | Law enforcement request does not override confidentiality |
| Physician retires and sells practice | Disclosure to successor permitted | Successor custodian exception |
| Retention period for patient record expires | Securely destroy the record | Must prevent reconstruction of PHI |

## Gotchas

- **Collection must be direct unless exception applies.** Collecting PHI from
  family members, employers, or other third parties is only permitted when the
  individual is incapable, collection from the individual would compromise safety,
  or another exception applies.

- **Purpose limitation applies to use, not just collection.** Even if PHI was
  lawfully collected, using it for a different purpose requires new consent or a
  statutory exception. Marketing is rarely a permitted secondary use.

- **Disclosure to law enforcement is not automatic.** Police requests without a
  warrant or court order do not require disclosure. The "serious harm" exception
  requires imminent risk, not merely ongoing investigation.

- **Research requires specific authorization.** General consent to treatment does
  not authorize research use. Researchers need either individual consent or a
  research ethics board waiver based on impracticability and minimal risk.

- **Third-party agreements are mandatory, not optional.** Transferring PHI to
  processors without a written agreement violates accountability requirements.
  Verbal assurances are insufficient.

- **Minimum necessary applies to all disclosures.** Even when disclosure is
  authorized, the custodian must limit disclosure to what is needed for the
  specific purpose. Providing entire records when excerpts would suffice violates
  this principle.

- **Retention minimums, not maximums.** The retention period specifies the
  minimum time records must be kept. Custodians may retain longer if there is a
  legitimate purpose, but must eventually destroy records.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Collection source | Direct from individual | Indirect collection requires exception |
| Collection amount | Minimum necessary | Only what is needed for purpose |
| Use scope | Original purpose | New purposes require new consent |
| Disclosure default | Consent required | Unless statutory exception applies |
| Circle of care disclosure | Implied consent | For treatment purposes only |
| Third-party transfers | Written agreement required | Custodian remains accountable |
| Retention period | 10 years typical | Varies by record type and province |
| Destruction method | Must prevent reconstruction | Shredding, secure deletion, or incineration |

## Related topics

- **Personal health information definition**: Understanding what constitutes PHI
  determines when these collection, use, and disclosure rules apply (Objective
  4.1)
- **Consent requirements**: Collection, use, and disclosure often depend on
  obtaining valid consent (Objective 4.4)
- **Provincial health privacy laws**: Rules vary by province; know which law
  applies to the custodian (Objective 4.3)
- **Breach notification**: Unauthorized disclosure triggers breach notification
  obligations (Objective 4.8)
- **Third-party accountability**: Private sector rules under PIPEDA similarly
  require accountability for third-party processors (Objective 2.5)

  - These statutes govern electronic health records in their respective
    jurisdictions
  - Security requirements are embedded within each provincial health privacy

  Practices - Health Sector" with the objective to "Understand when to apply

### 4.7: Know the Genetic Non-Discrimination Act and its implications

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 4.7: Know the Genetic Non-Discrimination Act and its implications

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Genetic Non-Discrimination Act (GNA)**: Federal legislation (S.C. 2017,
  c. 3) that prohibits requiring genetic tests or disclosure of genetic test
  results as a condition of providing goods, services, or entering into
  contracts. It amends both the Canada Labour Code and the Canadian Human
  Rights Act to include genetic characteristics as a prohibited ground of
  discrimination.

- **Genetic test**: A test that analyzes DNA, RNA, or chromosomes for purposes
  such as predicting the risk of developing disease or conditions, or assessing
  characteristics inherited from parents. Includes:
  - Direct-to-consumer genetic tests
  - Clinical genetic testing ordered by physicians
  - Pharmacogenomic testing
  - Carrier testing for heritable conditions

- **Prohibited requirements**: Under the GNA, no person shall require an
  individual to:
  - Undergo a genetic test as a condition of providing goods or services
  - Undergo a genetic test as a condition of entering into a contract
  - Disclose the results of a genetic test as a condition of providing goods or
    services
  - Disclose the results of a genetic test as a condition of entering into a
    contract
  - Collect, use, or disclose genetic test results without written consent

- **Constitutional challenge**: Quebec challenged the GNA's constitutionality,
  arguing it exceeded federal criminal law power. In *Reference re Genetic
  Non-Discrimination Act* (2020 SCC 17), the Supreme Court of Canada upheld the
  law as valid criminal law, affirming Parliament's authority to prohibit
  genetic discrimination.

- **Exceptions under the GNA**: The Act does not apply when:
  - A health care practitioner requires genetic test results to provide health
    services to the individual
  - A researcher requires genetic test results for medical or scientific
    research purposes (with consent)
  - The individual voluntarily discloses results for their own purposes

- **Canadian Human Rights Act amendments**: The GNA added "genetic
  characteristics" as a prohibited ground of discrimination in federal
  jurisdiction. Employers and service providers in federally regulated sectors
  cannot discriminate based on genetic information.

- **Canada Labour Code amendments**: Extended protections to federally regulated
  employees, prohibiting employers from requiring genetic testing or disclosure
  of genetic test results.

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Insurance company requires genetic test before issuing life insurance | Prohibited under GNA | Cannot require genetic test as condition of contract |
| Employer asks job applicant to disclose genetic test results | Prohibited under GNA and Canadian Human Rights Act | Genetic characteristics are prohibited ground of discrimination |
| Physician orders genetic test to diagnose patient condition | Permitted exception | Health care practitioners providing health services are exempt |
| Research study requests genetic samples with informed consent | Permitted exception | Medical research with consent is exempt |
| Insurer denies claim after discovering undisclosed genetic test | Prohibited | Cannot require disclosure of existing test results |
| Individual voluntarily shares genetic test with employer for accommodation | Permitted | Voluntary disclosure for individual's own benefit is allowed |
| Pharmacy refuses service unless customer provides DNA test | Prohibited | Cannot condition goods/services on genetic testing |

## Gotchas

- **Existing genetic test results are protected.** The GNA prohibits requiring
  disclosure of past genetic tests, not only requiring new tests. An insurer
  cannot ask if an individual has ever undergone genetic testing.

- **Written consent is required for collection.** Even when disclosure is
  voluntary, the collecting party must obtain written consent before collecting,
  using, or disclosing genetic test results.

- **Health sector exception is narrow.** The exception for health care
  practitioners applies only when providing health services to that specific
  individual. It does not permit research, insurance purposes, or employment
  decisions.

- **Provincial human rights codes may provide parallel protection.** While the
  GNA applies federally, some provinces have added genetic characteristics to
  their human rights legislation. Know which protections apply in each
  jurisdiction.

- **Pharmacogenomic testing is included.** Tests that determine how an
  individual will respond to medications are genetic tests under the GNA and
  receive the same protections.

- **Genetic information is also PHI.** Under provincial health privacy acts,
  genetic test results are personal health information. The GNA provides
  additional federal protections beyond provincial health privacy requirements.

- **Family history is distinct from genetic testing.** The GNA specifically
  covers genetic test results. Requiring disclosure of family medical history
  may not trigger GNA protections, though it may engage other privacy
  considerations.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Federal statute | S.C. 2017, c. 3 | Genetic Non-Discrimination Act |
| Supreme Court reference | 2020 SCC 17 | Upheld constitutionality of GNA |
| Prohibited ground added to CHRA | Genetic characteristics | Federal human rights protection |
| Offences | Criminal Code offences | Summary conviction or indictable |
| Maximum penalty (summary) | Fine up to $300,000 or 12 months imprisonment | For individuals |
| Maximum penalty (indictable) | Fine up to $1,000,000 or 5 years imprisonment | For individuals |
| Written consent required | Yes | For collection, use, or disclosure of genetic test results |
| Effective date | May 4, 2017 | Royal Assent |

## Related topics

- **Personal health information (PHI)**: Genetic test results qualify as PHI
  under provincial health privacy acts, receiving protection under both federal
  GNA and provincial health legislation (Objective 4.1)
- **Consent in health sector**: Collection of genetic information requires
  consent under both GNA and provincial health acts (Objective 4.4)
- **Electronic health record security**: Genetic information in EHRs requires
  technical safeguards (Objective 4.6)
- **Canadian Human Rights Act**: Genetic characteristics as prohibited ground
  of discrimination (Objective 1.1)
- **Federal vs provincial jurisdiction**: GNA applies federally; provincial
  health acts govern PHI in provincial jurisdiction (Objective 4.3)

### 4.8: Apply health-sector-specific breach notification protocols

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 4.8: Apply health-sector-specific breach notification protocols

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

> **Note**: The IAPP Free Study Guide provides only a high-level outline of exam
> topics. This extract synthesizes the objective framework with foundational
> health privacy breach notification concepts. For comprehensive exam
> preparation, consult the official textbook "Canadian Privacy: Data Protection
> Law and Policy for the Practitioner."

## Key concepts

- **Health information breach**: Unauthorized access, collection, use,
  disclosure, or disposal of personal health information (PHI), or loss or
  theft of PHI
  - Thresholds and definitions vary by provincial health privacy statute
  - More stringent than general private sector breach definitions under PIPEDA

- **Provincial health privacy statutes with breach notification**:
  - Ontario PHIPA (Personal Health Information Protection Act)
  - Alberta HIA (Health Information Act)
  - Manitoba PHIA (Personal Health Information Act)
  - Saskatchewan HIPA (Health Information Protection Act)
  - Each has distinct notification triggers, timelines, and requirements

- **Custodian vs. agent distinction**:
  - Health information custodian bears primary breach notification
    responsibility
  - Agents (processors) must notify custodians of breaches
  - Contractual requirements should specify breach reporting timelines

- **Notification recipients**:
  - Affected individuals (mandatory in most jurisdictions)
  - Provincial privacy commissioner or regulatory body
  - Professional regulatory bodies (in some cases)
  - Other custodians who may need to take action

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Lost unencrypted USB with patient records | Notify commissioner and affected individuals; assess scope | Most provincial health statutes require notification for loss of PHI |
| Employee snooping in patient records | Investigate, notify affected patients, report to commissioner | Unauthorized access triggers notification even without external disclosure |
| Ransomware attack on health system | Treat as breach; notify commissioner and patients; assess data exfiltration | Encryption by attacker constitutes unauthorized access |
| Misdirected fax with PHI | Assess sensitivity and volume; notify if significant risk | Even routine operational errors can trigger notification obligations |

## Gotchas

- **PIPEDA breach rules don't automatically apply**: Health sector has separate
  statutory frameworks; don't assume PIPEDA's breach provisions govern health
  information custodians
- **Lower thresholds than private sector**: Many health statutes require
  notification for any breach, not just those creating "real risk of
  significant harm"
- **Agent vs. custodian confusion**: Agents must report to custodians, but
  custodians retain notification responsibility to individuals and regulators
- **Electronic vs. paper records**: Breach notification applies equally to both
  formats
- **Commissioner notification timing**: Some statutes require notification "at
  the first reasonable opportunity" rather than fixed timelines

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| PHIPA notification to IPC | At first reasonable opportunity | No fixed timeline |
| Alberta HIA notification | Without unreasonable delay | Risk-based threshold |
| Content of notification | Description of breach, steps taken, contact info | Varies by statute |
| Record retention post-breach | Follow normal retention plus investigation needs | Document response |

## Related topics

- **4.1 Personal health information definition**: Understanding what
  constitutes PHI determines breach notification applicability
- **4.2 Ontario PHIPA**: Primary health privacy statute with specific breach
  requirements
- **4.3 Provincial health privacy laws**: Each jurisdiction has distinct breach
  notification frameworks
- **4.6 Electronic health record security**: Preventive controls reduce breach
  likelihood
- **2.15 PIPEDA breach notification**: Compare health sector requirements to
  general private sector obligations

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 4.8: Apply health-sector-specific breach notification protocols

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Breach of personal health information (PHI)**: Unauthorized collection, use,
  disclosure, retention, or disposal of PHI, or theft or loss of PHI, regardless
  of the medium in which the information is stored. Includes both intentional
  unauthorized access and inadvertent disclosures.

- **Risk of significant harm**: The threshold for mandatory breach notification
  in most provincial health privacy acts. Factors include: sensitivity of the
  PHI, whether the information was encrypted, likelihood of misuse, and
  potential consequences to the individual (identity theft, reputational harm,
  physical safety, financial loss).

- **Circle of care disclosure vs breach**: Disclosures within the circle of care
  for treatment purposes are not breaches, even without express consent. A
  breach occurs when PHI is disclosed outside authorized purposes or to
  unauthorized recipients.

- **Custodian obligations**: Health information custodians must contain the
  breach, evaluate the risk, notify affected individuals and regulators when
  required, and implement measures to prevent recurrence. Documentation of all
  breaches is mandatory regardless of notification requirements.

- **Mandatory notification to individuals**: Required when the breach creates a
  risk of significant harm. Notification must include: description of the
  breach, date or time period, type of PHI involved, steps taken to reduce harm,
  steps the individual can take, and contact information for the custodian.

- **Mandatory notification to regulators**: Provincial health privacy acts
  require notification to the Information and Privacy Commissioner (IPC) or
  equivalent oversight body when breaches meet specified thresholds. Some
  require notification of all breaches; others only when risk of harm exists.

## Provincial variation summary

- **Ontario (PHIPA)**: Mandatory notification to IPC for all breaches involving
  theft, loss, or unauthorized access. Notification to individuals required
  where significant harm is reasonably foreseeable. 72-hour recommended timeline
  for regulatory notification.

- **Alberta (HIA)**: Mandatory notification to the Commissioner for breaches
  with risk of harm. Notification to individuals required when breach creates
  risk of harm. Written notification records must be maintained.

- **Manitoba (PHIA)**: Breach notification to Ombudsman and affected individuals
  required when there is a risk of identity theft, significant harm to
  reputation, or other significant harm.

- **Saskatchewan (HIPA)**: Trustees must notify affected individuals of a breach
  if there is a real risk of significant harm. Notification to IPC is required.

- **New Brunswick (PHIPAA)**: Custodians must notify individuals and the
  Commissioner when there is a real risk of significant harm from the breach.

- **Newfoundland and Labrador (PHIA)**: Custodians must notify individuals when
  breach creates real risk of significant harm. Commissioner notification
  required.

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Unencrypted USB with patient records lost by employee | Immediate containment, notify IPC and affected patients, provide credit monitoring | Theft/loss of unencrypted PHI creates risk of significant harm |
| Fax sent to wrong healthcare provider | Assess if receiving provider is in circle of care; if so, request deletion | Within circle of care may not constitute reportable breach |
| Ransomware encrypts hospital patient database | Notify IPC immediately, notify affected individuals, engage forensic investigation | Unauthorized access with unknown exfiltration creates significant harm risk |
| Physician discusses patient with family without consent | Document incident, assess if patient suffered harm, report if significant harm likely | Privacy violation requiring internal documentation; notification depends on harm assessment |
| Email containing PHI sent to wrong patient | Notify both patients, document breach, assess need for IPC notification | Disclosure to unauthorized recipient requires individual notification |
| Employee accesses ex-spouse's health records | Terminate access, notify affected individual and IPC, discipline employee | Snooping is unauthorized access requiring mandatory reporting |

## Gotchas

- **Not all provinces require notification for all breaches.** Ontario requires
  IPC notification for any theft, loss, or unauthorized access. Other provinces
  only require notification when risk of significant harm exists. Know the
  threshold for each jurisdiction.

- **"Risk of significant harm" is jurisdiction-specific.** Some acts define this
  term; others leave it to interpretation. When in doubt, err toward
  notification to comply with custodian accountability requirements.

- **Encryption matters but does not eliminate notification.** If PHI was
  encrypted and the encryption key was not compromised, notification may not be
  required. However, if there is any possibility of key compromise, treat as
  requiring notification.

- **Notification to individuals must be direct.** Indirect notification (e.g.,
  website posting) is only acceptable when direct notification would cause
  further harm or when contact information is unavailable. Direct mail or
  communication is the standard.

- **Documentation is always mandatory.** Even when notification is not required,
  custodians must document the breach, the risk assessment, and the rationale
  for any decision not to notify. IPCs may audit these records.

- **Third-party processor breaches are custodian responsibility.** When an agent
  or service provider causes a breach, the custodian remains responsible for
  notification obligations. Contracts should require immediate breach reporting
  from processors.

- **Time limits vary by province.** Ontario recommends 72 hours for IPC
  notification. Other provinces specify "without unreasonable delay" or "as soon
  as reasonably possible." Delayed notification may itself constitute
  non-compliance.

- **Health sector notification differs from PIPEDA/private sector.** Health
  privacy acts often have lower thresholds and stricter timelines than PIPEDA's
  "real risk of significant harm" standard. Do not conflate the two regimes.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| Ontario IPC notification | All theft, loss, or unauthorized access | Regardless of harm assessment |
| Ontario individual notification | Risk of significant harm | Assessed by custodian |
| Ontario recommended timeline | 72 hours | For IPC notification |
| Alberta notification threshold | Risk of harm | To Commissioner and individuals |
| PIPEDA notification threshold | Real risk of significant harm | For comparison; health acts may differ |
| Record retention for breaches | Minimum 1 year after last use or as required by statute | Varies by province; some require longer |
| Notification content | Description, date, PHI type, mitigation steps, individual steps, contact info | Minimum required elements |

## Related topics

- **Personal health information (PHI)**: Definition and scope of protected
  information determines what constitutes a breach (Objective 4.1)
- **Circle of care**: Understanding authorized disclosures distinguishes
  permitted uses from breaches (Objective 4.4)
- **Safeguarding PHI**: Technical and administrative safeguards that prevent
  breaches and affect harm assessment (Objective 4.6)
- **PIPEDA breach notification**: General private sector requirements for
  comparison with health-specific protocols (Objective 2.15)
- **Provincial health information custodians**: Entities responsible for breach
  notification compliance (Objective 4.2)

### 4.9: Identify differences between health sector and general privacy requirements

*Source: iapp-cippc-free-study-guide*

# 4.9: Identify differences between health sector and general privacy requirements

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Free Study Guide

## Source limitation

The IAPP Free Study Guide is a preparation overview document that does not contain
substantive content for this objective. The guide only references that the CIPP/C
covers "select health information privacy statutes such as Ontario's PHIPA,
Alberta's HIA, Manitoba's PHIA and Saskatchewan's HIPA" without explaining the
differences between health sector and general privacy requirements.

For detailed content on this objective, refer to the primary textbook: "Canadian
Privacy: Data Protection Law and Policy for the Practitioner."

## Key concepts (from body of knowledge outline)

- **Health privacy statutes**: Provincial health information acts (PHIPA, HIA,
  PHIA, HIPA) govern health information separately from general private-sector
  laws like PIPEDA or provincial PIPAs.
- **Scope difference**: Health sector laws apply to health information custodians
  (hospitals, physicians, pharmacies) while general laws apply to commercial
  activities broadly.
- **Sensitivity recognition**: Health information receives heightened protection
  due to its sensitive nature, with stricter consent and disclosure requirements.

## Related topics

- Provincial health privacy acts (Objectives 4.1-4.8): Specific requirements under
  PHIPA, HIA, PHIA, and HIPA
- PIPEDA (Domain II): General private-sector privacy framework for comparison
- Provincial PIPAs (Domain II): Alberta and BC private-sector laws for comparison

*Source: iapp-cippc-bok*

# 4.9: Identify differences between health sector and general privacy requirements

Source: IAPP CIPP/C Body of Knowledge (Version 3.1.0, Effective 1 Sept. 2025)

## Key concepts

- **Different legislative frameworks**: Health sector privacy is governed by
  provincial health privacy acts (PHIPA, HIA, PHIA, HIPA), while general
  private-sector privacy is governed by PIPEDA or provincial private-sector laws
  (Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA, Quebec Law 25). These regimes operate independently
  with distinct terminology, obligations, and oversight mechanisms.

- **Personal health information (PHI) vs personal information (PI)**: Health
  acts use the term "personal health information" with specific statutory
  definitions covering health status, health services, and health care payments.
  PIPEDA and private-sector laws use "personal information" as a broader
  category. PHI is a subset of PI that receives heightened protection.

- **Custodians vs organizations**: Health acts impose obligations on "health
  information custodians" (physicians, hospitals, pharmacies, health ministries).
  PIPEDA imposes obligations on "organizations" engaged in commercial activity.
  The same entity may be subject to both regimes for different types of
  information.

- **Circle of care and implied consent**: Health sector allows implied consent
  for disclosures within the circle of care for treatment purposes. PIPEDA has
  no equivalent concept; implied consent is narrower and based on reasonable
  expectations in commercial contexts, not care relationships.

- **Consent model differences**: Health acts permit broad implied consent for
  treatment purposes; express consent is required for disclosures outside the
  circle of care. PIPEDA requires consent to be appropriate to the sensitivity;
  opt-out may suffice for less sensitive information in commercial contexts.

- **Breach notification thresholds**: Some provincial health acts (e.g., Ontario
  PHIPA) require notification to the Commissioner for all theft, loss, or
  unauthorized access, regardless of harm. PIPEDA requires notification only when
  there is a "real risk of significant harm." Health sector thresholds are often
  more stringent.

- **Oversight authority**: Health acts assign oversight to provincial
  Information and Privacy Commissioners or Ombudsmen. PIPEDA assigns oversight
  to the federal Privacy Commissioner. Different commissioners have different
  powers, investigation procedures, and enforcement approaches.

## Comparative summary

| Aspect | Health sector | General private sector (PIPEDA) |
| ------ | ------------- | ------------------------------- |
| Governing law | Provincial health privacy acts (PHIPA, HIA, etc.) | PIPEDA or substantially similar provincial law |
| Terminology | Personal health information (PHI), custodian | Personal information (PI), organization |
| Implied consent scope | Circle of care for treatment | Reasonable expectations in commercial context |
| Express consent trigger | Disclosure outside circle of care | Sensitive information or new purposes |
| Breach notification | All theft/loss/access (Ontario) or risk of harm | Real risk of significant harm only |
| Oversight | Provincial IPC or Ombudsman | Federal Privacy Commissioner |
| Access request timeline | Varies by province (often 30-45 days) | 30 days (PIPEDA s. 8) |
| Employee information | Covered if held by custodian | Generally excluded from PIPEDA |

## Common scenarios

| Scenario | Correct approach | Why |
| -------- | ---------------- | --- |
| Physician discloses patient records to another treating physician | Implied consent under health act | Circle of care permits disclosure for treatment |
| Retailer shares customer data with affiliate for marketing | Express consent under PIPEDA required | No circle of care concept; new purpose requires consent |
| Hospital laptop with unencrypted patient data is stolen | Notify IPC and affected individuals under health act | Health acts often have lower notification thresholds |
| Insurance company loses customer financial data | Assess real risk of significant harm before notifying | PIPEDA harm threshold applies to non-health sector |
| Employer requests employee's medical records from physician | Express consent required under health act | Employer is outside circle of care; health act applies |
| Bank collects customer SIN for account opening | Implied consent may apply under PIPEDA | Reasonable expectation in commercial context for regulatory compliance |
| Pharmacy sells de-identified prescription data | Depends on adequacy of de-identification | Must meet health act de-identification standards, not just PIPEDA |

## Gotchas

- **Same information, different regime.** A diagnosis on a hospital record is
  PHI under the health act; the same diagnosis on an employer's sick leave form
  is PI under PIPEDA. The legal regime depends on who holds the information and
  in what context.

- **Substantially similar does not mean identical.** Provincial private-sector
  laws (Alberta PIPA, BC PIPA) are deemed substantially similar to PIPEDA but
  have their own requirements. Do not assume PIPEDA rules apply to provincially
  regulated private-sector activity.

- **Circle of care has no private-sector equivalent.** Implied consent for
  treatment disclosures is a health-sector-only concept. Businesses cannot claim
  "circle of care" to justify sharing customer data without consent.

- **Breach notification timelines differ.** Ontario PHIPA recommends 72-hour
  notification to the IPC for breaches. PIPEDA specifies "as soon as feasible"
  with no fixed timeline. Do not apply PIPEDA timelines to health-sector
  breaches.

- **Health acts may cover non-commercial activity.** PIPEDA applies to
  commercial activity only. Health acts apply to custodians regardless of
  whether the activity is commercial. A hospital's treatment of patients is
  governed by the health act even if no payment is involved.

- **Access request fees differ.** Some health acts prohibit or limit fees for
  PHI access requests. PIPEDA permits reasonable cost-recovery fees. Know the
  applicable fee rules for each regime.

- **Research exemptions differ.** Health acts have specific provisions for
  research ethics board approval and consent waivers. PIPEDA's research
  exemptions are narrower and less detailed.

## Limits and defaults

| Item | Value | Notes |
| ---- | ----- | ----- |
| PIPEDA access request response | 30 days | May extend with notice |
| Ontario PHIPA access request response | 30 days | Standard timeline |
| PIPEDA breach notification threshold | Real risk of significant harm | Risk-based assessment required |
| Ontario PHIPA IPC notification | All theft, loss, or unauthorized access | Lower threshold than PIPEDA |
| PIPEDA oversight | Federal Privacy Commissioner | Ombudsman model; no order-making power |
| Ontario PHIPA oversight | Ontario IPC | Order-making power for health information |
| Circle of care concept | Health sector only | Not applicable under PIPEDA |
| Custodian concept | Health sector only | PIPEDA uses "organization" |

## Related topics

- **PIPEDA principles**: Understanding the 10 PIPEDA principles provides baseline
  for comparison with health-sector requirements (Objective 2.1)
- **Provincial health privacy acts**: Specific requirements vary by province
  (Objective 4.3)
- **Circle of care and consent**: Health-sector-specific consent mechanism
  (Objective 4.4)
- **PHI definition**: Understanding what constitutes PHI versus general PI
  (Objective 4.1)
- **Breach notification protocols**: Differences in health sector vs PIPEDA
  notification requirements (Objective 4.8)
- **Substantially similar laws**: Provincial private-sector laws that replace
  PIPEDA for intra-provincial activity (Objective 2.14)
